Abstract   
Denmark’s National Energy and Climate Plan (NECP) sets ambitious targets, including a 70% reduction 
in greenhouse gas emissions by 2030 and climate neutrality by 2045. Municipalities play a central role 
in achieving these goals, yet existing literature lacks clarity on how effectively municipal tools address 
the specific challenges posed by different clean technologies, and how these tools can best be deployed 
at the municipal level. 
This thesis addresses this research gap by asking: How feasible are the clean technologies in the NECP 
within a municipal context, taking into account the effectiveness of municipal tools in mitigating their 
associated challenges. 
To answer this question, the study applies a mixed-methods approach, combining a literature review, 
an economic analysis of clean technologies, and a case study of Roskilde Municipality’s climate plan 
supported by stakeholder interviews. These methods are integrated into the development of the Green 
Light Model, a decision-support framework designed to evaluate the feasibility of clean technologies 
at the municipal level across three dimensions: technological, governance, and economic. 
The thesis findings result in a model that establishes a framework for assessing how effectively 
municipal tools can mitigate technology-specific challenges and provide a clearer understanding of 
clean technology resilience in this area. In the model, this is termed management resilience. By 
combining management resilience with political resilience and financial resilience, the model provides 
an overall assessment of the feasibility of the clean technologies under examination. Beyond the case 
study, the thesis contributes to both theory and practice by offering a structured tool for identifying how 
municipalities can better align their climate strategies with national targets by clarifying the degree of 
management, political, and financial resilience. The model is designed to generate suggestions on how 
municipalities can adapt their climate plans to achieve a more resilient and therefore more feasible 
approach. 
The model was tested on large-scale solar PV utilities for a generic municipality. The Green Light 
Model proved effective in systematically mapping technical barriers against municipal tools, 
highlighting where governance instruments are strong and where they are weak. Furthermore, the model 
integrates economic and political indicators to show the overall feasibility of solar PV utilities. Finally, 
the model provides specific suggestions for the generic municipality on how it may make its climate 
approach more feasible. One suggestion was that the municipality should examine adding sufficient 
battery capacity to the solar PV utilities to smooth electricity delivery to the grid over a 24-hour period. 
The municipality should then run this combination through the model again. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:5==
 
 
Resumé 
 
Danmarks nationale energi- og klimaplan (NECP) opstiller ambitiøse mål, herunder en reduktion af 
drivhusgasudledninger på 70 % inden 2030 og klimaneutralitet i 2045. Kommunerne spiller en central 
rolle i at nå disse mål, men den eksisterende litteratur mangler klarhed over, hvor effektivt kommunale 
politiske værktøjer adresserer de specifikke udfordringer, som følger med forskellige former for grønne 
teknologier, samt hvordan disse værktøjer bedst kan anvendes på kommunalt niveau. 
Dette kandidatspeciale adresserer denne forskningsmæssige mangel ved at stille spørgsmålet: Hvor 
gennemførlige er de grønne teknologi løsninger, der indgår i NECP, i en kommunal kontekst, når man 
også tager højde for effektiviteten af kommunale værktøjer til at afbøde deres tilknyttede udfordringer? 
For at besvare dette spørgsmål anvender studiet en mixed-methods-tilgang, der kombinerer et 
litteraturreview, en økonomisk analyse af grønne teknologier og et casestudie af Roskilde Kommunes 
klimaplan understøttet af interessentinterviews. Disse metoder integreres i udviklingen af Green Light-
modellen, et beslutningsstøtteværktøj designet til at evaluere gennemførligheden af grønne teknologier 
på kommunalt niveau ud fra tre dimensioner: teknologisk, forvaltningsmæssig og økonomisk. 
Specialets resultater munder ud i en model, der etablerer en ramme for at vurdere, hvor effektivt 
kommunale værktøjer kan afbøde teknologispecifikke udfordringer, og giver en klarere forståelse af 
grønne teknologiers robusthed i denne sammenhæng. I modellen betegnes dette som 
forvaltningsmæssig robusthed. Ved at kombinere forvaltningsmæssig robusthed med politisk robusthed 
og økonomisk robusthed giver modellen en samlet vurdering af de undersøgte grønne teknologi-
løsningers gennemførlighed. Ud over casestudiet bidrager specialet til både teori og praksis ved at 
tilbyde et struktureret værktøj til at identificere, hvordan kommuner bedre kan tilpasse deres 
klimastrategier til nationale mål gennem en klarlægning af graden af forvaltningsmæssig, politisk og 
økonomisk robusthed. Modellen er designet til at generere forslag til, hvordan kommuner kan tilpasse 
deres klimaplaner for at opnå en mere robust og dermed mere gennemførlig tilgang. 
Modellen blev testet på store solcelleanlæg i en generisk kommune. Green Light-modellen viste sig 
effektiv til systematisk at kortlægge tekniske barrierer i forhold til kommunale værktøjer og 
fremhævede, hvor forvaltningsmæssige instrumenterner er stærke, og hvor de er svage. Derudover 
integrerer modellen økonomiske og politiske indikatorer for at vise den samlede gennemførlighed af 
solcelleanlæg. Endelig giver modellen specifikke forslag til den generiske kommune om, hvordan den 
kan gøre sin klimaindsats mere gennemførlig. Ét forslag var, at kommunen bør undersøge muligheden 
for at tilføje tilstrækkelig batterikapacitet til solcelleanlæggene for at udjævne elleverancen til elnettet 
over et 24-timers. Kommunen bør derefter køre denne kombination igennem modellen igen.

1 Introduction 
In recent years, several municipalities in Denmark have launched ambitious clean tech initiatives to 
transition towards sustainable energy systems [1]. This case highlights the need for a critical 
examination of how well municipal tools align with the municipal climate ambitions. This thesis aims 
to investigate this alignment, identifying gaps and opportunities for improvement to enhance the 
effectiveness and sustainability of local green initiatives. 
As a city council member actively involved in developing Roskilde Municipality's Climate and Energy 
Plan, I have witnessed the dedication and hard work that municipalities invest in achieving their specific 
climate goals. These localized efforts are crucial and commendable. The municipalities' climate goals 
are largely set to be at least as ambitious as the goals set out in Denmark’s National Energy and Climate 
Plan (NECP)  [1], which aims for a 70% reduction in greenhouse gas emissions by 2030 compared to 
1990 levels and achieving climate neutrality by 2045, ultimately targeting a 110% reduction by 2050. 
 
Municipalities design their strategies to fit their priorities and unique circumstances, resulting in a 
diverse array of plans from municipality to municipality. The challenge is that when municipalities 
commit to clean technologies, they often do not know the circumstances very well. This is because 
clean technologies are relatively new and innovative, developing at a rapid pace and must be integrated 
into complex contexts. 
A particular challenge lies in assessing the financial resilience of the clean technologies aimed at 
reducing emissions and enhancing sustainability [2]. Clean technologies are often highly innovative 
and relatively new on the market, which can bring many associated challenges into play. These 
challenges may be costly and poorly mapped out, meaning they are not always properly accounted for 
when clean technologies are integrated into municipal climate plans. This is a challenge because the 
municipality has only a limited amount of funding available and only one source of income: its 
residents. If the green transition becomes too costly, the municipality risks losing public support for its 
climate plans. If the municipality has outsourced the project and only poorly mapped out the challenges, 
these may not be incorporated into the agreements with the external partner. As a result, the municipality 
risks being left with consequences from the challenges that could create inconveniences for citizens and 
businesses. Thus, even outsourcing clean technology does not remove the need to understand the 
financial resilience of the clean technology the municipality commits to in its climate plan. 
It is also crucial to make sure that there is a certain degree of political resilience to ensure that the 
municipality’s chosen clean technology commitment remains viable, even as political commitment to 
the green transition changes over time. Immature clean technologies with a need for financial support 
in the beginning can mature and become able to operate sustainably without heavy reliance on ongoing 
subsidies in the long run. If a clean technology cannot become subsidy-free in the long run, it will 
remain dependent on political goodwill. Once that political support fades, the unprofitable technology 
risks losing its financial support, and thereby also risks no longer being financially viable and gradually 
being phased out of the market. The municipality then runs the risk of being left with indirect 
consequences from the challenges, which could create disruptions for citizens and businesses. Thus, 
even outsourcing clean technology does not remove the need to understand the financial resilience of 
the clean technology the municipality commits to in its climate plan [3]. As a result, the money spent 
on getting the technology up and running will be lost and could have been invested in other clean 
technologies with a better long-term economic outlook, which could have continued to drive CO₂ 
reductions long after political support has faded. Technologies that cannot achieve long-term economic 
sustainability are therefore not politically resilient and pose a significant risk to the green transition. 
 
It is very important that the municipalities have some implementation resilience when implementing 
clean technology and initiatives as part of the green transition. Implementation resilience implies a 
certain degree of clarity on the challenges that come with a clean technology, the municipal tools 
available to address these challenges, and how these tools mitigate the challenges. It is very important 
==PAGE:9==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 4 of 59 
to have a clearer view of how the municipality’s tools can mitigate these challenges in order to develop 
more realistic climate plans, without later having to delay the climate plans and thereby lower the level 
of ambition due to unforeseen challenges. The sum of these reductions in municipal climate plans risks 
leading to delays in the national climate plans, as these are partly based on the municipalities’ climate 
results. 
It is obvious that the municipalities play a central role in achieving the national goals, yet existing 
literature lacks clarity on how effectively municipal tools mitigate the specific challenges posed 
by different clean technologies in the NECP, and how these tools can best be deployed at the 
municipal level. 
This thesis addresses this research gap by asking: What is the effectiveness of municipal tools in 
mitigating the associated challenges from the clean techs in the NECP? How feasible are the clean 
technologies in the NECP within a municipal context, taking into account both implementation 
resilience, financial resilience, and political resilience? 
This thesis aims to explore all these aspects by building a framework that provides a clearer picture of 
the overall feasibility. The framework is named the Green Light Model (GLM) and is an assessment 
model for administrative guidance purposes and decision support. 
The Green Light Model is built by first identifying three fundamental areas that will have a major 
influence on the clean technology that a municipality plans to build into its climate plan. In the GLM, 
they are referred to as main dimensions: technology, governance, and economics. 
In the first step, the interactions between the main dimensions are not analyzed. Instead, the model 
clarifies specific conditions for the chosen clean technology within each dimension. The model does 
not conclude on the conditions that emerge from the work with the three dimensions in this step. 
In reality, these main dimensions are strongly interacting and give rise to a range of processes, 
conditions, and prerequisites for success. 
In the second step, the analysis focuses on the processes and conditions that emerge from the 
interactions between the dimensions. This step emphasizes the aspects that concern the resilience of the 
clean technology in three areas: implementation resilience, financial resilience, and political resilience. 
The model assesses the strength of resilience in each area and indicates the degree of certainty the 
municipality can rely on within these three categories. 
In the third and last step, the assessments of the three resilience areas are combined in a consideration 
of the strength of the overall feasibility of the clean technology under study. 
It is important to note that all assessments are relative to the specific municipality applied in the model 
when analyzing the given technology. 
The Green Light Model can help municipalities gain an overview of which of their municipal tools, and 
to what extent, mitigate the challenges associated with the clean tech commitments included in the 
municipality’s climate plan. The model will thus reveal if a technology presents challenges that the 
municipality does not have the tools to mitigate. Using this model, the municipality can gain an early 
overview. Several clean tech initiatives can be compared using the model, allowing the municipality to 
establish a basis for comparison before selecting its clean tech commitments. This overview can then 
inform the decision about which clean tech commitments to include in the municipality's climate plans. 
The model also works to identify the economic cost of mitigating the challenges associated with a clean 
tech commitment and compare this to conventional alternatives. Regardless of whether the municipality 
itself or a third party has financially invested in the clean tech commitment, knowing whether the cost 
of mitigation is considered worthwhile can be useful—particularly when deciding whether the 
==PAGE:10==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 5 of 59 
municipality should bear the cost or whether it should be passed on to a third party. 
The model also ensures that the municipality gains a better understanding of the clean tech 
commitment’s political resilience to shifting political trends. This will enable the municipality to better 
assess the extent to which investments surrounding the technology are future-proof. 
Finally, the model can be used to identify which clean technologies are most feasible for a specific 
municipality to prioritize in their climate plans.

The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 6 of 59 
2 Literature Review 
 
An initial literature search was conducted to determine whether a similar analysis has previously been 
made that addresses the same resource gap as mine. The literature search did not identify any sources 
that describe how to assess how well each municipality’s instruments are suited to addressing the 
specific challenges associated with each individual technology. Those sources that had some relevance 
to the issue tended to look either at procedures, i.e. the planning process itself, often with a focus on 
participatory planning methods (involvement of stakeholders), or on how to conduct feasibility studies 
for specific technologies (such as waste treatment plants etc.). Several studies also looked at municipal 
energy planning from a modelling perspective, while others simply reviewed a selection of municipal 
energy or climate plans and the challenges associated with their implementation (again, often from a 
governance, institutional and/or stakeholder involvement perspective). 
 
Searches have been conducted using DTU Find, Google, and ChatGPT, Scite AI and Perplexity. The 
snowball method has also been applied in AI tools, inspired by the DTU webinar on Scite AI. 
 
The article that came closest to addressing the research gap identified for this thesis is titled 
“Municipalities as Change Agents? Reconsidering Roles and Policies in Local Energy Sector-
Coupling” [4]. The article shares a similar overarching approach to categorizing municipal tools as the 
framework adopted in this thesis. However, the article specifically focuses on promoting sector 
coupling activities and is tailored to German municipalities, as evidenced by its case studies. In contrast, 
this thesis extends the discussion by incorporating the economic perspective, including short- and long-
term financial risks. Unlike the referenced article, this thesis works on the premise that poor economic 
business cases over the long term can render initiatives more vulnerable to political instability, a critical 
dimension absent from the article. While the article leans more toward constructing an implementation 
plan, this thesis focuses on the municipality's capacity to influence projects where challenges may arise 
due to specific technologies. Nonetheless, the key challenges identified in the article closely align with 
those discussed in this thesis.  
 
Another notable distinction lies in the focus areas of the two works. The article emphasizes creating an 
incentive structure to drive progress in the green transition, whereas this thesis examines municipal 
influence with regard to the technological challenges inherent in various projects. By doing so, the thesis 
broadens the scope to assess not just implementation strategies but also the factors affecting the 
feasibility and resilience of municipal initiatives in the context of sector coupling [4]. 
The main dimensions of the Green Light Model are technology, governance and economy, as illustrated 
in Figure 1.  
Figure 1: The main dimensions of the Green Light Model operationalized in the literature review. 
 
The figure illustrates how the main dimensions of the Green Light Model are operationalized in the following section of the 
literature review. The literature review focus areas within the Green Light Model are: Technological challenges (section 2.2), 
which addresses the technical barriers for the clean technology that is being examined; the governance toolbox (section 2.3), 
==PAGE:12==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 7 of 59 
which covers the instruments and approaches available to policymakers and local authorities; and the Cost of Avoided CO₂ 
(CACO₂) (section 2.4), which operationalizes the economic dimension by putting at price tag on the avoided CO2. Together, 
these elements provide the framework for analyzing how the Green Light Model is discussed in existing literature. 
 
In the following literature review, the main dimensions of the Green Light Model are further 
operationalized as technological challenge, governance toolbox, and CACO2 (Cost of Avoided CO2), 
which together provide a more tangible framework.  The interactions between the circles are therefore 
not addressed in the literature section. The interaction will be examined in the analysis section 4. 
 
2.1 Denmark’s National Energy and Climate Plan (NECP) 
 
Denmark’s National Energy and Climate Plan (NECP) for 2021-2030, as outlined by The Danish 
Energy Agency, sets ambitious targets for reducing greenhouse gas emissions by 70% by 2030 
compared to 1990 levels and achieving climate neutrality by 2045, and has set a target of 110% 
reduction by 2050 [1]. The plan emphasizes a transition to renewable energy sources, increased energy 
efficiency, and the development of new technologies. 
The table below shows a list of the technologies that are included in Denmark’s National Energy and 
Climate Plan [1]. In the following, the main challenges associated with each of these technologies will 
be identified based on the initial literature review. A key objective of the analysis that will be carried 
out in my thesis will be to identify feasiblity to implement at the municipal level (including specifically 
in Roskilde Municipality) given these challenges. This will be based on an extended literature review, 
case study interviews and analysis work outlined in the methodology (chapter 3). 
I identified the following technologies in Denmark’s National Energy and Climate Plan: 
• 
Solar PV  
• 
On shore wind 
• 
Offshore wind 
• 
District Heating  
• 
Electric Vehicles, Infrastructure 
• 
Energy Efficiency Measures 
• 
Electric Distribution Grid Enhancements 
• 
Battery Energy Storage Systems (BESS) 
• 
CCS 
• 
Carbon Capture and Utilization (CCU) 
• 
Biomass 
• 
Geothermal 
• 
Energy Storage Capacities (Thermal Storage in District Heating Systems) 
• 
Heat Pumps large scale 
• 
Heat Pumps small (private homes) 
• 
Green Hydrogen 
• 
Wave Power 
• 
PTX 
• 
Pyrolysis 
 
 
2.2 Technology 
 
2.2.1 
Categorizing the challenges of the technologies 
This section focuses on categorizing the challenges of the technologies from Denmark's National 
Energy and Climate Plan [1], as listed in section 2.1.  
==PAGE:13==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 8 of 59 
To later quantify the challenges associated with the technologies, I categorize these challenges into 
overarching categories. In my search for how this has been done elsewhere, I found “Municipalities as 
change agents?” by Schmieder et al. (2023) [4], and “Net-Zero Europe” by McKinsey & Company 
(2022) [2]. 
 
“Municipalities as change agents?” addresses local governance in energy sector coupling and "Net-Zero 
Europe” outlines uncertainties in EU-wide decarbonization. 
 
Although these sources differ in focus from this thesis, they offer a categorization of challenges that are 
suitable for the challenges I later identify for each of the following technologies. 
 
Schmieder et al. (2023) [4] identify nine categories of municipal challenges related to local energy 
transitions. While their study focuses on sector coupling, many of the obstacles they describe—such as 
legal and regulatory hurdles, resource scarcity, technical complexity and infrastructure gaps, and public 
acceptance—are highly relevant for the implementation of the technologies examined in this report. [4, 
pp. p.4, tabel 1.] 
 
The article “Net-Zero Europe” [2] also outlines several uncertainties across different technology areas. 
These uncertainties extend beyond the specific implementation challenges of each technology as I 
examine in my report. It includes broader concerns about e.g. whether they will contribute sufficiently 
and in time to the green transition. The article also takes a broader perspective on technology areas. 
Nevertheless, it offers a level of precision in outlining uncertainties that makes it highly relevant when 
examining the challenges associated with each technology. For that reason, I have distilled the parts of 
the report that relate directly to the specific challenges I investigate  [2]  
 
Drawing on both sources, I have chosen four main challenge categories. These categories are selected 
to be both comprehensive—covering all the relevant challenge types from the two sources—and 
practical for later evaluation in a municipal planning context. I have excluded economic challenges, as 
these are discussed separately in Section 4.3. The main categories I have chosen are: 
 
Resource Limitations 
Challenges related to availability of materials or skilled labour, infrastructure readiness, land 
availability. 
 
Regulatory Hurdles 
Legal or institutional barriers, unclear mandates, complex permitting procedures, and limitations in 
municipal authority. 
 
Lack of Public Acceptance 
Resistance from citizens or stakeholders, Not in My Backyard, communication difficulties and 
challenges in engaging the public meaningfully. 
 
Technical Barriers 
Technological immaturity, integration complexity, system compatibility issues, or interdependencies 
with other technologies. 
2.2.2 
Solar Photovoltaics (PV) 
While Denmark's National Energy and Climate Plan [1] includes a wide range of technologies, this 
report focuses on solar PV. For solar PV, the focus is further limited to ilarge utilities, which I will refer 
to as solar PV utilities. 
Solar cells, also known as photovoltaic (PV) systems, play a big role in the global transition to more 
sustainable energy solutions. Solar energy not only offers a cleaner alternative to fossil fuels but also 
==PAGE:14==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 9 of 59 
possesses key characteristics that position it as a cornerstone of the energy transition. As we will see 
later in the thesis, a big advantage is the continuous cost reduction in solar PV systems.  
2.2.3 
Development Forecast  
Solar energy is poised to become a driving force in Europe’s energy transition. The EU aims to reach 
nearly 600 gigawatts (GW) of installed solar PV capacity by 2030, up from approximately 263 GW in 
2024. [5].  
Denmark has set an ambitious goal to quadruple onshore electricity production from renewable sources, 
amounting to around 50 TWh. In line with this ambition, the Danish Government reached a Climate 
Agreement in December 2023 focused on increasing solar and wind energy production onshore. The 
agreement establishes a framework to support this fourfold expansion and enables the state to take a 
more active role in planning major land-based energy parks [1]  
Specifically for solar power in Denmark, the installed capacity of solar PV is projected to reach 17.788 
MW by 2030, up from 6.649 MW in 2025. [1] 
In Roskilde Municipality, there is no separate target for solar PV alone. However, the municipality’s 
climate plan sets a broader goal of at least 150 MW of renewable electricity capacity by 2030. [6] 
2.2.4 
Challenges  
In the section on challenges, the focus is specifically on issues related to the planning of Solar PV 
utilities. 
2.2.5 
Resource Limitations  
 
• 
Lack of suitable land 
Resource limitations include the scarcity of suitable land for solar farms, especially in densely populated 
areas where land competition is intense. [7]. 
 
The demand for land for clean energy exceeds the amount of conflict-free areas available. According 
to the report “Fra modstand til medvind”, Denmark's overall plans require land equivalent to 130–140% 
of the country’s total area [8]. When too many interests compete for too little space, conflicts are bound 
to arise. 
 
Stakeholders concerned with nature conservation may oppose wind turbines because of their impact on 
bird populations. Holiday homeowners may fear impaired views and disturbances caused by solar 
farms. 
Such opposition to renewable energy installations is often explained by the so-called NIMBY effect—
the idea that people become dissatisfied when a facility is placed in their own backyard [8]. A lack of 
attention to these issues, which are tied to certain technology’s, could therefore suddenly compromise 
the municipality's climate plan and prevent it from meeting its climate targets, as the municipality has 
invested in technologies for which it lacks adequate tools to mitigate. 
 
• 
Grid limitations  
Denmark’s collective electricity grid presents challenges for installing solar panels in certain areas. 
Energinet has pointed out that many new solar farms are being placed in sparsely populated rural areas 
with ample space and local support—regions where electricity consumption is low and the existing grid 
is not designed to handle large-scale electricity production [9]. 
 
The challenges with the grid is shown in Figure 2  
 
 
 
==PAGE:15==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 10 of 59 
Figure 2: The electricity consumption coverage by the electricity grid per municipality 
 
Source: [9] 
Note:  From the capacity map we see, the challenge faced by the electricity grid not having sufficient capacity. In the top right 
corner of the capacity map, it can be seen that Denmark’s total consumption coverage is 123%, meaning that the country as 
a whole produces more electricity than it consumes. On the map, each municipality is color-coded according to the scale 
shown in the top left corner, indicating how much of the municipality’s own electricity consumption is covered by its own 
production. Some municipalities produce more than 200% of the electricity they consume locally, while others produce less 
than 100%. The map also illustrates the challenges faced by the electricity grid in distributing power between municipalities 
with surplus production and those with a deficit. In several areas, the map shows transmission lines marked in red. According 
to the legend in the bottom right corner, some of these overloaded lines are operating at more than 125% of their capacity. 
The Danish Energy Agency's projections indicate a significant increase in new installations. This surge 
will be so substantial that some high-voltage connections will “go into the red” and, at times, be unable 
to transmit the electricity. If the planned projects across the country are realized, the problem will only 
escalate because solar farms are approved and installed faster than grid expansions [9]. Lack of grid 
capacity can therefore compromise the municipal climate plan’s ambitions for clean renewable 
electricity, or project partners may suddenly find themselves with a solar power facility that is unable 
to feed all of its generated electricity into the grid. Shareholders can, in principle, be the municipality’s 
residents.  
I have chosen to disregard the fluctuating energy production under the challenge category "Resource 
limitations". Meaning that the grid limitations in the category “Resource limitations” were evaluated on 
the assumption that the electricity production from solar PV utilities is smoothed out. The challenge of 
fluctuating electricity production is addressed in the section "Technical barriers" instead. 
==PAGE:16==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 11 of 59 
2.2.6 
Regulatory Hurdles 
Solar parks often encounter delays, because of the complexity in permitting, approval and procedural 
processes. These processes require interactions with various regulatory agencies to ensure compliance, 
which is both time-consuming and costly [7]. Especially the grid connection procedures are a major 
cause of delays in photovoltaic (PV) system development. According to the German Solar Industry 
Association, grid connection procedures are the greatest cause of delay in photovoltaic (PV) projects 
[10].  
If the solar energy targets are to be reached, meeting these targets requires continued focus on 
permitting, approvals, and procedures [5]. The Danish energy Agency has addressed these challenges 
by introducing new rules on permits for renewable energy (RE) which will come into effect in July 
2024. The rules are intended to make it faster and simpler to obtain the necessary permits for RE projects 
[11]. 
2.2.7 
Lack of Public Acceptance 
The spread of solar parks is transforming rural areas, often dramatically altering land use and reshaping 
how people perceive the landscape. These changes have sparked increased debate among local 
communities, citizens, and governments about the environmental, spatial, and visual impacts of such 
installations—and whether they ultimately damage the landscape’s visual identity [12]. Although Danes 
are generally positive about the green transition and the installation of renewable energy facilities, a 
study shows that 19 percent of Danes are unwilling to live near large-scale wind farms [13]. 
 
A successful implementation of solar PV projects requires local community acceptance. Misinformation 
and perceived inequity can lead to opposition. Without acceptance, projects risk delays or increased 
costs [14].  This opposition can make it increasingly difficult to install the renewable energy technology 
envisioned in the municipality's climate plans. 
 
2.2.8 
Technical barriers 
A major challenge for solar utilities is the fluctuating energy production. Denmark's commitment to 
expanding renewable energy sources, particularly solar energy, poses significant challenges because of 
the influx of high volumes of intermittent renewable energy. For instance, the anticipated trend is that 
the increased share of installed renewable energy in Denmark will primarily be accomplished by 
integrating large concentrations of offshore wind power plants and large-scale concentrated 
photovoltaic (PV) plants into the transmission system. This foreseen high penetration of such 
intermittent power sources can lead to peak production times when electricity demand may not coincide 
with production due to the intermittent nature of these resources [15].  
There are fluctuations over the seasons, with many dark months which may coincide with low wind 
conditions (often referred to as "Dunkelflaute"), resulting in an almost complete lack of renewable 
energy production. These challenges are more of a national scope, concerning how Denmark as a nation 
can secure electricity in a cost-effective manner during the winter months when renewable energy 
facilities produce very little power. It is beyond the scope for this thesis to investigate how Denmark 
can address these issues by e.g. making agreements with other nations and using national tools such as 
legislation and financial support schemes to promote the right technologies for long-term storage, which 
must be part of the appropriate national energy mix combined with a flexible energy demand on a 
national level. This must be clarified as part of the development of the national energy and climate plan. 
The fluctuating production over a 24-hour period can, on the other hand, be considered a municipal 
issue, as it is highly dependent on the extent to which, and the manner in which, the municipality uses 
its land for solar park utilities. It tends to place a significant strain on the local power grid due to the 
==PAGE:17==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 12 of 59 
highly sporadic nature of the production. This will reduce the grid capacity available to the municipality 
for meeting its climate targets with renewable energy production goals. The intermittent nature of solar 
PV generation therefore poses a challenge for balancing supply and demand in real-time. Without 
adequate energy storage solutions and demand response strategies, solar energy cannot be utilized 
efficiently [16].  
If the solar PV utilities size are to be designed to handle the grid must be able to transport electricity 
during periods of maximum production, a solar park without adequate storage capacity, they will 
occupy excessive grid capacity relative to their average production. It therefor wasting grid capacity 
that could have been used for more renewable electricity. In turn, this could pose a risk to the 
municipality’s climate plans. 
 
2.3 The governance toolbox available to municipalities 
The municipality, by virtue of its different roles, has access to different tools that can be used to meet 
challenges in eg. low carbon projects. When I, in the following, describe the tools used by the 
municipality, I will link each tool to the specific role in which the municipality uses it. I do this because 
it gives the tools context, which helps ensure that they are understood in a more clear and consistent 
way. 
Based on comprehensive desk research I inspected different roles of the municipality. The article 
"Municipalities as Change Agents?" divides the municipality's roles into four main categories: 
"Consumer and role model," "Planner and regulator," "Provider and supplier," and "Advisor 
and promoter" [4]. In Roskilde Municipality’s climate plan, the following main roles for the 
municipality were slightly differently defined: “The municipality as a partner, facilitator, and 
provider of information,” “The municipality as owner of companies,” “The municipality as an 
authority,” and “The municipality as a business” [6]. 
With a few minor adjustments, I choose to use Roskilde Municipality’s definition of the municipality’s 
roles to categorize different tools the municipality has at its disposal to meet challenges in low carbon 
projects. This will make it easier for the stakeholders I am going to interview in Roskilde Municipality 
to relate to my questions. 
Table 1 shows Roskilde Municipality's categorization of different types of involvement. Going 
forward, I will use this as a framework to categorize different tools the municipalities have at their 
disposal in renewable energy projects. I have made some adjustments to the terminology used in 
Roskilde Municipality’s framework to ensure a clear distinction between types of involvement and the 
municipality’s actual tools in my thesis.

2.3.1 
The municipality in terms of soft governance, collaborative efforts and public 
engagement 
Partnerships, facilitation of sustainable initiatives, and engaging in public awareness initiatives are 
important tools in municipalities' efforts to play a role in advancing the green transition. The tools can 
help enabling an environment that ensures that local actions align with broader environmental policies. 
Maintaining partnerships with the private sector is crucial for municipalities, as this can attract 
investment in sustainable projects and create an environment that supports the implementation of 
sustainable practices [17]. In this way, municipalities can play a central role in coordinating such 
initiatives, ensuring that local projects align with broader sustainability objectives [18]. Partnerships 
serve as a key tool for municipalities, as they enable collaboration between educational institutions, 
private companies, and research organizations. Through such collaborations, municipalities can pool 
resources, share knowledge, and foster innovation Research shows that community-academic 
partnerships can support the involvement of new actors and resources, which is essential for achieving 
technical and social innovation in urban environments [19]. Public awareness is another vital part of 
the strategy for promoting sustainability. Through programs designed to educate local communities 
about climate change, energy conservation, and the benefits of renewable energy, municipalities can 
increase public awareness and engagement [20]. Studies have shown that such educational programs 
play a measurable role in encouraging pro-environmental behavior among citizens [21]. By fostering a 
culture of environmental responsibility, municipalities can support both local and national 
environmental strategies. 
Roskilde Municipality aims to advance the green transition by leveraging partnerships, facilitating 
sustainable infrastructure, and engaging in public awareness initiatives. The goal is to promote 
sustainability, foster innovation, and ensure that local actions align with broader environmental policies. 
The municipality’s involvement with partnerships such as Risø Gateway and organizations including 
Klimarådet, Grøn Puls, Corolab, and Gate 21 exemplifies its commitment to advancing environmental 
goals. [6] 
In summary, partnerships, the facilitation of sustainable initiatives, and public awareness efforts are 
important elements in municipalities’ contributions to the green transition. These tools help align local 
actions with national sustainability goals and demonstrate how effective collaboration can drive 
meaningful change at the community level. 
The Table 2 presents different soft governance tools available to municipalities, focusing on 
collaborative efforts and public engagement.

2.3.2 
The municipality as the co-owner of various companies  
Municipalities can benefit greatly from the influence, competencies, and knowledge they possess 
through ownership or co-ownership of waste and energy services when developing low-carbon 
solutions. Research has found that municipalities that have specific competencies for the direct 
provision of waste or energy services, such as is the case in many northern European countries, can 
have significant capacity to address climate change that other local authorities lack [23]. The 
competencies that municipalities have access to through ownership or co-ownership of waste and 
energy companies are in demand when knowledge is shared as part of the collaboration to develop low-
carbon solutions. The municipality can eg. formulate its heating plan in dialogue with district heating 
and gas utilities, local industry and major demand centres (such as housing associations) to support 
information-sharing and expand the knowledge pool even further [22]. 
An example of how collective knowledge can lead to a coherent plan with multiple synergies is found 
in the city of Stockholm. The city adopted an integrated approach in the development of the Hammarby 
==PAGE:20==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 15 of 59 
Sjöstad district (11,000 apartments), ensuring that the planning of both the area and its buildings aligned 
with the principle of natural cycles. This meant that resource cycles should be closed at as local a level 
as possible, and energy should be sourced from renewable and preferably local sources. As part of an 
environmental programme, an eco-cycle model was developed, which helped reduce the metabolic 
flows within Hammarby Sjöstad through a coordinated and integrated system. The model emerged from 
a collaboration between municipal organisations such as Stockholm Energi (now Fortum), Stockholm 
Vatten AB, and the city’s waste management companies. The Hammarby model adopts a cross-sectoral 
planning approach, focusing on material flows to create synergies, with the overarching goal of making 
the district “climate neutral” by 2030. The experience and knowledge gained from Hammarby Sjöstad 
were later applied in the development of Stockholm’s Royal Seaport district. Here, an updated Eco-
cycle Model 2.0 was introduced, with the main objective of highlighting and explaining key 
interconnections and synergies between various resource flows [33]. 
Roskilde Municipality gains important political tools to influence the green transition through its 
ownership stakes in companies such as ARGO, VEKS, and Fors. ARGO is responsible for waste 
management and energy production, VEKS is a transmission company that distributes district heating 
between different district heating areas, while Fors manages water supply, wastewater treatment, and 
waste management. The municipality's involvement as a co-owner provides a unique opportunity to 
steer these organizations towards sustainable practices and technologies, aligning their operations with 
the broader goals of the green transition. This involvement enables the municipality to support and 
accelerate the green transition through strategic decisions in waste management, district heating, and 
water services [6].

2.3.3 
The municipality as a planning authority  
Planning is a prerequisite for an effective green transition. As planning authorities, municipalities have 
strong opportunities to take a green and long-term approach. Many initiatives within specific sectors 
have impacts across other sectors, requiring a cross-cutting and long-term effort that must be carefully 
planned. As a general rule, municipalities hold planning authority. They designate and define 
regulations for land use through municipal and local plans. Municipal planning determines the land use 
of an area—for example, for housing, public purposes, retail, or business activities, including high-risk 
enterprises. Local plans establish regulations concerning, among other things, the scale and design of 
construction. This may also include designating areas for renewable energy, which should be done with 
consideration for nature, biodiversity, and recreational interests [26]. Through thoughtful standards for 
urban planning, they can shape the municipality by incorporating green spaces, which not only support 
biodiversity but also improve citizens’ quality of life. A core component of this effort is the development 
of sustainable infrastructure—solutions that meet urban needs while minimizing environmental harm. 
Examples include electric buses that reduce emissions, as well as parks and public squares designed 
with vegetation to absorb carbon dioxide and provide recreational spaces for residents  [17]. Another 
example could be that the municipality in its role as planner and regulator introduces "carrot policies" 
such as free or discounted parking spaces for electric vehicles [4]. 
Another important task for the municipality as a planning authority, is to implement national 
environmental policies [24]. For example, the municipal waste management plans should be prepared 
in line with the national strategy/plan and municipal waste management plans should be prepared to 
guide local implementation. To do so, a process should be adopted to align municipal waste 
management plans with the national strategy/plans. When building new infrastructure and facilities, 
individual municipalities should be encouraged to follow the adopted plans to ensure cohesive 
development of the sector across the whole country. This would ensure the efficient use of limited 
public resources and the coordinated development of the sector [30] 
Municipalities can designate specific zones for clean technology projects, including Cleantech Parks, 
which serve as innovation hubs where businesses, research institutions, and the public sector can 
collaborate. These areas provide an ecosystem that nurtures the development and commercialization of 
new sustainable technologies, fostering economic growth and environmental sustainability. Through 
the allocation of staff resources and specialized expertise within the municipal administration dedicated 
to this area, local authorities can expedite approval processes, reduce regulatory bottlenecks, assist 
applications for grants and in some cases grant exemptions from existing regulations, making it easier 
for businesses to invest in clean infrastructure projects. 
Such regulatory “sandboxes” are increasingly being used to create a temporary regulatory environment 
in which existing regulation is adjusted or lifted to test new measures and gain expertise. The 
derogations from existing rules have the effect of lifting the regulatory barriers for a given period, e.g., 
to test new business models or technical solutions  [22]. 
For example, Roskilde Municipality has been granted a dispensation to deviate from the Finger Plan 
[34], which has made it possible to prepare a local development plan designating a large area north of 
Roskilde city for activities related to cleantech. The purpose of the local development plan is to 
designate the area for companies with expertise in production, research, and development, as well as 
related administrative functions within the field of cleantech and green solutions. Furthermore, the plan 
aims to ensure that the area maintains a green and open character with public access via roads and 
pathways [35].  
It is the ambition to build a cleantech park that can become a global hotspot for sustainable energy. This 
development is to take place in collaboration between Roskilde municipality, DTU and Danish and 
international companies, as well as in close partnership with local businesses. The total development 
area covers 500,000 square meters, and the current local development plan allows for the construction 
==PAGE:22==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 17 of 59 
of a new campus as well as additional research and testing facilities in a first phase covering 
approximately 248,000 square meters [36]. 
Thus, the Municipality can as a planning authority, significantly contribute to establishing favorable 
infrastructure and local development plans, facilitating the integration of clean technologies and 
supporting sustainable urban growth. 
The Table 4 outlines municipal tools related to their role as planning authorities, including 
zoning for green technology, regulatory frameworks, and infrastructure development planning.

These instruments enable municipalities to influence clean tech projects. 
 
2.3.4 
The municipality as a business 
The climate change initiatives that municipalities can most easily implement appear to be green local 
government procurement standards, public-private partnership agreements with local businesses, and 
tree planting as an internal municipal green project [24]. The annual greenhouse gas emissions from 
municipal and regional procurement and construction amount to 2.7 and 5.9 million tonnes of CO₂, 
respectively, and there are many initiatives that could reduce these emissions. Municipalities and 
regions can use their “purchasing power” to ensure CO₂ reductions and to steer the market in a greener 
direction [26]. For example, the conversion of the municipal vehicle fleet could be directly addressed 
through the public procurement system by buying new electric vehicles. But a more indirect approach 
to procurement can also be used to influence traffic within the municipality, such as by installing 
charging capacity for cars or cargo bikes when constructing or renovating public properties [4]. Thus, 
procurement policy can be used more broadly than just for the procurement of goods – transport, 
delivery, and the value chain (including subcontractors), among others, can also be included. 
Furthermore, the purchase of services can be qualified by environmental standards to ensure that 
environmental requirements cannot be circumvented by outsourcing, for example, transport, cleaning, 
catering, or maintenance [26]. 
The municipality also has the opportunity to carry out its own projects. For example, municipalities 
have a large number of buildings with expansive roof areas, where installing solar panels is cheaper 
than on single-family homes and does not interfere with nature conservation or agricultural production,

as solar farms on open land might. The administration and separation of municipal solar power 
companies is now simpler than before, and municipalities can establish a single company to manage 
multiple installations. According to a report from Momentum, installing solar panels on municipal 
rooftops larger than 500 m² could result in an annual CO₂ reduction of 175,000 tonnes. Solar panels on 
schools, childcare centers, and sports facilities may also have an educational and engaging effect on 
citizens, potentially encouraging further green transition [26]. 
In Roskilde Municipality, one of the municipality’s internal projects is the establishment of forests. In 
collaboration with the Danish Nature Agency, the municipality buys up land areas for this purpose. This 
helps the municipality achieve negative CO₂ emissions, which will be necessary to reach net-zero CO₂ 
emissions overall. Roskilde Municipality has an annual purchasing volume of approximately DKK 1.6 
billion. The municipality also uses its procurement and tendering power to influence the market for 
green goods and services, taking a more circular approach [6]. 
As both developers and a significant consumers of goods and services, municipalities can leverage their 
purchasing power to promote climate-friendly products and practices. By adopting green procurement 
policies, a municipality can prioritize buying goods and services that have lower environmental impacts, 
thereby supporting the market for sustainable products and reducing the municipality's overall carbon 
footprint. By prioritizing climate-friendly purchasing and projects, a municipality can not only reduce 
its own carbon footprint but also drive broader market shifts toward sustainability. This approach 
encourages local businesses to adopt greener practices, fosters innovation in eco-friendly products, and 
sets a positive example for residents and other organizations. Implementing robust verification 
processes and engaging with suppliers to ensure compliance are crucial steps in maximizing the 
environmental benefits of green procurement.

2.4 Economy 
 
2.4.1 
Current Cost of avoided CO2 (CACO2) 
The municipal climate plans often work with concrete targets for how much reduction in CO2 
emissions the municipality must achieve. The municipality’s Climate and Environment Department 
operates with a limited budget, which makes the cost of avoided CO2 (CACO2) of different initiatives 
crucial, if the municipal economy is directly involved. As illustrated in Figure 3, the cost of avoiding 
CO₂ emissions varies significantly depending on the chosen climate initiative. Consequently, the 
municipality’s selection of climate initiatives leads to substantial differences in the total amount of 
avoided CO₂ emissions. 
 
Figure 3: Abatement cost curve, EUR/tCO2e (year 2030) 
 
 
Source: [2] 
The figure shows the abatement cost curve for 2030, measured in EUR per ton of CO₂e reduced in a European context. The x-
axis represents the abatement potential (in MtCO₂e), while the y-axis indicates the cost of abatement. Measures below zero 
represent cost-saving solutions (negative abatement costs), such as solar thermal heating, electric vehicles, and energy 
efficiency improvements. Measures above zero indicate technologies with positive abatement costs, such as hydrogen-based 
steelmaking, biofuels in aviation, and heat pumps in various applications. The curve illustrates which technologies can deliver 
emission reductions most cost-effectively, highlighting the economic trade-offs of different decarburization strategies. 
 
 
If we look at solar PV utilities, which are most often installed by developers, the cost of CO2 abatement 
does not directly affect the municipality’s own finances. However, the information on the abatement 
cost per ton CO2 can be useful in other ways, for instance by assessing how strong the economic driver 
is for the market to use municipal land for setting up solar PV utilities.  
 
When looking at the cost of removing CO2, it is important to consider the marginal abatement cost, as 
this cost metric also takes into account the current energy mix. The table below shows the marginal 
abatements costs for CO2 reductions for the different technologies, compared against the current energy 
mix.  
 
The Table 6 table shows the marginal costs of avoiding one ton of CO₂ emissions (CACO2) across 
different technologies and regions.  
 
==PAGE:25==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 20 of 59 
 
 
Source: [38] 
Note: The figures vary significantly depending on technology and country, reflecting differences in energy systems, resources, 
and policy frameworks. For example, wind and solar are typically among the cheapest technologies for CO₂ reduction, while 
technologies such as Carbon Capture and Storage (CCS) and OCGT with hydrogen are at the higher end of the cost scale. The 
table thus illustrates which technologies deliver the most cost-effective CO₂ reductions, and how priorities may differ across 
Europe, the United States, and globally. An “x” indicates that the technology does not result in lower emissions compared to 
the current energy mix. The figures are based on 2022 data. 
 
I didn’t find any sources with the cost per avoided CO₂ for the Danish energy mix. Therefore we use 
the European data in Table 6 in order to compare the different technologies with solar. If we narrow 
down the technologies presented in Table 6 to only those that also appear in the Denmark's National 
Energy and Climate Plan [1] (also listed in section 2.1), we obtain the technologies onshore wind, 
offshore 
wind 
and 
geothermal 
as 
a 
basis 
for 
comparison 
with 
solar. 
 
Of those three alternatives to solar, only onshore wind is better in terms of CACO2 at a cost of 190 
USD/tCO2e. Solar has, at a cost of 226 USD/tCO2e, also a good CACO2 profile. Based on these 
considerations, solar is a very cost-efficient way to reduce CO2 for the municipality. The municipality 
could, based on this comparison, consider whether to explore the possibility of supplementing its clean 
tech initiatives with onshore wind within the municipality. 
 
3 Methods 
 
Figure 5 provides an overview of the approach taken in the methodology. The three main dimensions 
of technology, governance and economy are combined, and we address the overlaps between them, 
which will serve as the basis for the analysis. The overlaps between the dimensions show how these 
themes interact in practice. When two dimensions are combined in the model, the analysis provides a 
nuanced assessment of the interaction, with a focus on the resilience of that specific combination. The 
three combinations, referred to here as management, financial, and political resilience, and together 
form an overall picture of the technology’s Clean Tech feasibility

Note: The figure illustrates the structure of the Green Light Model for a given clean tech, where the three main dimensions – 
technology, governance, and economy – each play a crucial role in assessing Clean Tech feasibility. The relevant sections of 
the methodology are indicated in parentheses beneath each circle and overlap, allowing the figure to serve as a guide to the 
structure of the report. 
 
3.1 Technology: Identifying challenges to the clean technologies 
In the Green Light Model, these challenges are defined as barriers that may hinder or complicate the 
municipality’s progress towards achieving the goals outlined in its climate plan. The main challenges 
associated with each technology were identified through an extensive literature review in Section 2.2. 
Based on the nature of each challenge, they are categorized into four groups: resource limitations, 
regulatory hurdles, lack of public acceptance, and technical barriers. The rationale behind this 
categorization is explained in section 2.2.1 The challenges rooted in the clean tech commitment should 
be understood as obstacles to the municipality achieving its climate goals as planned. 
 
3.2 Governance: Identifying tools of the municipality  
The municipality’s main tools for influencing clean tech projects were also identified through an 
extensive literature review. Based on the context of each tool, they were categorized under four 
municipal roles: “Soft governance: Collaborative efforts and Public engagement”, “The Municipality 
as Co-owner of companies”, “The Municipality as an Authority” and “The Municipality as a Business”   
The tools that are identified and the rationale behind this categorization is explained in section 2.3. Each 
tool is linked to the specific category, that is the role in which the municipality employs it. The reason 
I use this approach is that in the literature I came across where a link between tools and the municipal 
Figure 4: Visualization of the structure of the Green Light Model in the method-sections 
==PAGE:27==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 22 of 59 
role had been established, I found that it provided the tools with greater contextual clarity and helped 
ensure that the tools were understood in a more consistent and coherent manner [4] [6]. 
 
3.3 Management resilience: Measure how effective the municipality's tools are 
for mitigating the challenges associated with the clean technologies 
For each technology, the available municipal tools will be assessed as to how effectively each specific 
tool mitigates a specific challenge. The results will be presented in a table providing an overview of 
how effectively each of the municipality’s tools help mitigate each of the challenges associated with 
solar PV utilities. In the Green Light Model, these challenges are defined as barriers that may hinder or 
complicate the municipality’s progress towards achieving the goals outlined in its climate plan. 
3.3.1 
Detailed rating of municipal governance tools' effectiveness in mitigating technology 
challenges 
The effectiveness of each tool in mitigating each challenge category will be rated on a scale. I have 
developed a rating system with a set of descriptions that provide indicative meanings for assessing the 
effectiveness of a specific tool in mitigating a specific challenge. That is what you see in Table 7 
 
Table 7: Criteria for rating the degree of effectiveness of municipal governance tools on mitigating the technology challenges 
 
Score 
Name 
Description (Indicative meaning) 
0 
Very low effect/ 
no effect 
By using the tool, the municipality cannot mitigate the challenge or no 
meaningful connection can be established between the tool and the 
challenge. If there is any effect at all, the desk research didn’t identify 
it, and it is most likely very limited. 
+ 
Low effect 
By using the tool, the municipality may be able to play an indirect role 
in mitigating the challenge - for example, by influencing project 
participants or third parties through encouragement or signaling, 
thereby helping to mitigate the specific challenge. 
These third parties might typically include other private companies, 
citizens, or national authorities. However, there are no guarantees of 
effect, and if there are any, they are most likely very limited. 
++ 
Moderate effect 
By using the tool, the municipality gains the ability to address the 
challenge, which may mitigate the challenge with reasonable effect. 
For example, the tool can enable the municipality to create concrete 
incentives for project participants or third parties to help mitigate the 
challenge.  
This way, the tool may allow the municipality to contribute indirectly 
to mitigating the challenge. There are no guarantees, but the tool could 
have a reasonable effect. 
+++ 
High effect 
Using the tool, the municipality has a high probability of mitigating 
the challenge. While the municipality can take independent and 
active measures with this tool, the complete resolution of the 
challenge also depends significantly on external factors. The external 
factors could be e.g. market dynamics, price fluctuations, supply chain 
availability. 
==PAGE:28==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 23 of 59 
This tool enables the municipality to directly act or create substantial 
and tangible incentives for project participants or third parties to help 
mitigate the challenge.  
++++ 
Very high effect/ 
Resolving effect 
Using this tool, the municipality has a direct and substantial ability to 
resolve the challenge, or, with a high degree of certainty, to 
significantly mitigate it. 
This table defines the scoring scale used to assess the effectiveness of municipal governance tools in mitigating technology-
specific challenges. Scores range from 0 (no or very limited effect) to ++++ (very high effect), with each level accompanied 
by a description that indicates the degree to which a given tool can mitigate a given challenge. 
The method lends itself to reviewing all the clean tech included in the Danish National Climate Plan, 
as this would allow for a comparison of the overall ratings across technologies. However, since this 
would go beyond the scope of the present thesis, I have chosen to focus on a single technology in my 
analysis. 
For each clean tech reviewed, a table will be made that will show how effective each municipal tool is 
for mitigating the specific challenge categories that are associated with the specific clean tech addressed 
in the table. Each table will only contain the challenges identified for the specific clean tech. 
An example of such a table with ratings is shown in Table 8. The idea is that the aggregated rating in 
the bottom row of the table will be used in the next part of the method. The score in the button row will 
match the score of the most effective tool in mitigating the challenge. This is based on the logic that the 
municipality’s overall ability to mitigate a certain challenge category to a certain extent is defined by 
the tool that is most effective in mitigating this challenge category.

3.3.2 
Aggregated score across challenge category 
 
The previous part of the analysis 3.3.1 analyzed how the municipality’s tools can mitigate the different 
challenge categories associated with a clean technology. 
The table below provides an aggregated score in the last column. This is done by listing the lowest score 
across all 4 challenge categories. This approach is based on the assumption that overall resilience of a 
clean technology cannot be regarded as more resilient than its least mitigated challenge among the four 
challenge categories. 
 
Table 9 Aggregated score across all four challenge categories for each clean technology. 
The table shows how effectively municipal tools mitigate the four key challenge categories associated with each clean tech 
project. The aggregated score in the last column highlights the weakest point across alle four challenge categories, i.e. the 
challenge category where municipal tools provide the least support. A lower aggregated score indicates that the municipality 
cannot rely on its tools to sufficiently mitigate all challenges identified for the clean technology.. 
The lower the aggregated score is, the less the municipality can rely on its tools to mitigate the 
challenges for a clean technology. In terms of management resilience, I consider 4 and 3 pluses to 
indicate that a technology has management resilience in the sense that the municipality can rely on its 
tools to a sufficient degree to mitigate the challenges that may arise. I consider 2 pluses as having 
uncertain resilience. And 1 or 0 pluses means that the municipality cannot rely on its tools to be able to 
mitigate the challenge that may arise - meaning that it has no management resilience.

3.4 Economic sustainability analysis 
 
3.4.1 
Current Cost of Avoided CO2 (CACO2) 
The cost of avoiding CO₂ emissions varies significantly depending on the chosen approach. 
Consequently, the municipality’s selection of climate initiatives leads to substantial differences in the 
total amount of avoided CO₂ emissions.  
The municipality here makes an informed assessment of how much CO₂ reduction each clean tech 
projects will contribute and benchmarks it. 
3.4.2 
Financial resilience: Cost of handling the challenges of the clean tech 
Make a calculation of the economic cost of mitigating the clean tech commitment’s challenges – The 
municipality can then assess whether the cost of mitigation is justified and whether the municipality 
should bear the expense or pass it on to the developer.  
In this thesis, I regard CO₂ reductions as the primary product of clean tech projects. If the clean tech 
projects generate a marketable product in addition to the CO₂ reductions, I consider this a by-product. 
Many clean tech projects can, in that understanding, build a positive business case based on their by-
product. In the case of solar PV utilities, one can say that, besides avoiding CO₂ emissions, they also 
produce electricity that they can build a business case on. 
But if the cost of mitigating the challenge with economic means is too high, it could undermine the 
clean tech initiative’s business case, which may make it difficult to attract investors or developers. 
Therefore, the cost of mitigating the tech challenge will be factored into the clean tech initiative’s 
original price and compared to conventional alternatives to see if it is still competitive on the market. 
Since the by-product here is electricity, it makes good sense to use LCOE for benchmarking. 
In the end, it is up to the market to determine profitability in a bidding round. Therefore, I consider it 
sufficient that there is only a small overlap in the market price range between the production cost of the 
clean tech project and competing non-clean tech that is currently selected on the market. 
 
I consider clean-tech financially resilient if the challenges that cannot be mitigated with municipal tools 
can instead be mitigated by modifying the clean technology. This applies if the 5th percentile production 
price of the by-product from the modified clean technology is still lower than the 95th percentile 
production price of the byproduct from fossil-based alternatives. And since that is the case here, the 
Green Light Model defines solar PV utilities as financial resilience. 
 
3.4.3 
Political resilience: Clean Technologies being resilient to political trends over time 
I consider a future positive business case to mean that the technology is resilient to political trends over 
time.  
When municipalities make a clean technology commitment, they create a path dependency. A path 
dependency refers to the idea that once a certain technological pathway has been chosen, it becomes 
increasingly difficult to change direction, even if better alternatives emerge later. Early decisions create 
“lock-in” effects because investments in infrastructure, knowledge, institutions, and regulations 
accumulate over time. This means that the initial choice of technology can strongly influence (and 
sometimes limit) the range of future options, making it costly or politically challenging to switch to 
==PAGE:31==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 26 of 59 
alternative solutions [39]. This is why it is important for municipalities to think long-term and choose 
technologies that will stay relevant for many years. 
If the chosen clean technology remains relevant, the municipality avoids having to change its entire 
approach once the first installations are worn out, which would otherwise mean starting over. A key 
factor for the clean technology to stay relevant over time is hence that it can maintain its relevance in 
the future energy mix, even as different political priorities may arise.  
One of the biggest weaknesses a clean tech can have to polical opposition is a perennially poor business 
case, causing the technology to remain reliant on subsidies or tax exemptions compared to other 
alternatives [40]. This is because an obvious political tool at any given time is the removal of subsidies 
or the repeal of taxes on competing alternative technologies. There are many examples where a quick 
political decision to remove subsidies has put an end to projects that lacked a viable business case 
without the subsidy [3]. 
The markets generally favor the lowest-cost product and without subsidies, this will often still be fossil-
based products. Hence, one of the key tasks in making the costumer’s first choice product a green one 
also lies in making green products attractive from a cost perspective [41]. 
If the cleantech gets a positive business case, removing its subsidies will no longer be sufficient to stop 
its adoption. At that point, the technology will represent a sound investment opportunity even without 
political economic support tools. It will be competitive and therefore also equipped to succeed in other 
international markets, where the main driver is purely economic. We see that effect in the United States, 
where there is currently political opposition to onshore wind turbines. Nevertheless, the U.S. 
government is finding it difficult to halt the construction of onshore wind turbines, because market 
forces — in the form of the tech giants - are choosing them, which can be attributed to the low 
production cost of electricity from wind turbines [42]. 
Therefore I have chosen to examine long-term political stability of a clean tech by investigating if the 
technology has the potential to achieve a positive business case in the future without public subsidy 
schemes or taxes on the technologies it could be substituted with. Here, we address this by calculating 
a forecast business case for 2040/2050, as it is the furthest point in time for which there are broadly 
supported studies. 
Almost all clean technologies require subsidies in the beginning [43]. CO₂ reductions have no direct 
economic value for the company achieving them, as the benefits are shared by society, not the firm 
itself. Therefore, subsidies, such as CO₂ pricing or credits, are needed to create financial incentives to 
reduce emissions and investing in clean technologies. But some clean technologies still have the 
potential to become economically self-sustaining and eventually outcompete fossil alternatives without 
subsidies. 
Electricity production is one of the by-products alongside reduced CO₂ emissions, that has brought 
clean technologies close to a point where they can compete with fossil-based products. For some clean 
technologies, this stage of direct competitiveness with conventional “black” products has already been 
reached. The figure below illustrates the remarkable reduction in costs for certain clean technologies 
that generate renewable electricity. The figure shows that the cost of electricity from some clean 
technologies such as solar photovoltaic and onshore wind are now on par with fossil-based technologies. 
In Figure 5, it can be observed that across all technologies, the relative LCOE from renewables have 
declined significantly. For instance, solar PV dropped from being 710% more expensive than fossil 
fuels in 2010 to 29% cheaper in 2022. Similar trends are observed for offshore and onshore wind, both 
of which moved from being costlier than fossil fuels to becoming competitive or even cheaper. This 
development highlights the rapid cost reductions and improved competitiveness of some renewables 
within just over a decade. 
==PAGE:32==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 27 of 59 
 
Figure 5: Change in competitiveness of solar and wind by country based on global weighted average LCOE. 2010-2022 
 
 
Source: 
[44] 
The figure illustrates the change in competitiveness of renewable energy technologies (solar PV, concentrating solar power, 
offshore wind, and onshore wind) compared to fossil fuels between 2010 and 2022, measured by the global weighted average 
LCOE. 
In Figure 6, we can see that not all clean technologies experience a drastic decline in electricity 
production costs. Some of the clean technologies appear to level off. It is worth noting, however, that 
this flattening occurs only after they have reached a lower level than the alternative fossil fuel cost 
range. 
 
Figure 6: Global LCOE from newly commissioned utility-scale renewable power technologies 
 
 
[44] 
Note: These data are for the year of commissioning. The thick lines are the global weighted average LCOE value derived from 
the individual plants commissioned in each year. The LCOE is calculated with project-specific installed costs and capacity 
factors. The grey band represents the fossil fuel-fired power generation cost in 2022, assuming that 2021 fossil gas prices 
==PAGE:33==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions

were the correct lifetime benchmark rather than the crisis prices of 2022. The bands for each technology and year represent 
the 5’th and 95’th percentile bands for renewable projects. 
If the clean technologies the government invests in do not have a long-term byproduct besides reducing 
CO₂ that can create a positive business case, they will remain dependent on some form of subsidy. This 
means that such technologies will always rely on political support. If they lose political backing in virtue 
of their ability to remove CO₂, at some point, they will have no secondary drivers to sustain further 
development. As a result, these technologies could disappear from the market, and the investments in 
supporting the clean technologies in its early phases may have had a reduced effect in the green 
transition [41]. 
However, if a clean technology has a by-product besides CO2 reductions (for example, electricity) that 
can act as an independent market driver, it has a better chance of surviving. Some clean technologies 
might even achieve a positive business case, allowing them to compete with fossil-based alternatives. 
And in some cases even replace them over time. When clean technologies have a chance of having an 
unsubsidized positive business case in the long run, they will be more resilient to policy movements in 
the long run too. 
The comparison will have the format shown in Table 10. The analysis will include a comparison 
between LCOE forecast for 2040 for selected clean technologies and electricity spot price forecasts to 
determine the (expected) longer-term business case for the clean technologies.

So when the estimated spotprice of (2040) > estimated LCOE in 2040 from the clean tech, that is 
examined in the GLM, then it have political resilience.
