Table 11, the political and administrative actors can quickly get an overview of which areas of resilience 
they need to be aware of when it comes to specific clean technologies. They can then investigate these 
points of concern further by looking at the underlying tables, thereby gaining a more qualified 
understanding of the different challenges. This would for example, help the policymaker quickly 
address the right elements to make their decision. 
It may occur that the technology selected for evaluation using the method is not feasible because its 
challenges cannot be mitigated with the municipality’s tools. However, the same challenges might be 
mitigated economically. In such cases, one can choose to combine the clean tech with the economic 
mitigation initiatives and consider the whole package as a new clean tech. This new clean tech can then 
be reassessed using the full method to determine whether it results in a feasible technology investment. 
 
3.6 Stakeholder interviews 
A series of interviews was conducted, focusing primarily on the political and technological aspects. 
Consequently, the interviews do not cover the economic dimension of this thesis. 
The purpose of the interviews was to obtain feedback on the method and to gain deeper insight into the 
municipalities’ tools, the challenges faced by the technologies, and the evaluation scale for assessing 
the mitigating effect of the tools on these challenges. 
The results of the analysis were validated through discussions in the interviews. The interviewees were 
asked whether they agreed with the key findings, whether additional nuances should be considered 
based on their experience, and which lessons could be identified. 
The interviews with representatives from Roskilde Municipality formed the basis for the Roskilde 
Municipality case study. In addition, all interviews were analysed collectively to extract key points 
relevant for the generic municipality in which the Green Light Method was tested

4 Analysis 
 
The following analysis focuses on the overlaps between the Green Light Model’s main themes, 
providing insights into the selected clean technology’s resilience in terms of political resilience, 
financial resilience, and management resilience, as shown in the Table 8. 
 
Figure 7: The Green Light Model now operationalizes the resilience in the analysis section for solar PV utilities. 
 
The Green Light Model operationalizes the Management resilience by assessing the effectiveness of the governance toolbox 
in mitigating the technological challenge. The Green Light Model analyzes financial resilience by pricing the challenges that 
arise in the planning of the clean technologies, and it examines political resilience by estimating the future business potential 
without political or economic support schemes. 
 
4.1 Detailed rating of municipal governance tools against technology challenges 
In this section, we analyze the different combinations of tools and challenges for each technology. The 
purpose of the analysis is to determine the extent to which the municipality’s tools address the identified 
challenges for each technology. Each tool will be given a score indicating its effectiveness. With 13 
tools and 4 challenge categories, there are 52 possible combinations for each technology, and it would 
be beyond the scope of this thesis to examine all of them. Therefore, 5 tools have been selected for 
inclusion in the analysis. To select the 5 tools, I conducted an extended literature review. I prioritized 
tools that had the clearest definitions in the literature I found. I also prioritized based on the amount of 
available literature on each tool, as I assumed this, all else being equal, would indicate a certain level 
of relevance. Where the literature showed significant overlap between two tools, I chose to exclude one. 
For example, the literature often categorizes "Educational Outreach" and "Public Awareness 
Campaigns" in nearly the same way. Nearly no emphasis was placed on objectivity or critical thinking 
in relation to education, even though that’s the difference between educational and a campaign, that 
often focuses more on trying to influence people to act a certain way. Therefore "Public Awareness 
Campaigns" was selected. The chosen tools are: Partnerships, Public Awareness Campaigns, 
Ownership in district heating companies, Zoning for Green Technology, and Green Procurement 
Policies. In the following, the model is tested on a generic municipality. It is very important to keep in 
mind here that we are working with a fictitious, generic municipality. The conditions of this generic 
municipality are compiled from different municipalities across the country. The rating system from 
methodology section 4.1.1 will be applied to each combination. 
==PAGE:38==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 33 of 59 
4.1.1 
 Evaluation of Governance Tools vs. Challenges for Large-Scale Solar Farms 
 
 
T2-A (Partnerships)/C1 (Resource Limitations)  
 
Score: +  
 
Partnerships have a low level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related to resource limitations 
for solar PV utilities. Partnerships can marginally ease resource constraints by pooling assets or 
coordinating land use, but they cannot create new land or grid capacity for solar parks. Multifunctional 
land use, as practiced in Assens Municipality, can be achieved through partnerships to ease the 
competition for scarce land [45]. 
The municipalities and possible partners typically do not own the electricity grid. Therefore, 
partnerships have very little impact on overcoming problems with grid capacity. The provision of 
adequate electricity cable infrastructure tends to occur more as an information flow from the utility 
energy companies than as true partnerships [46]. 
 
 
T2-A (Partnerships)/C2 (Regulatory Barriers)  
 
Score: 0  
 
Partnerships have little to no effect on mitigating challenges related to regulatory barriers for solar PV 
utilities. It has not been possible to find any literature that fully supports the idea that partnerships can 
mitigate challenges related to regulatory processes in development of solar park. This likely indicates 
that there are, in fact, very few examples that substantiate such a claim. 
 
 
T2-A (Partnerships)/C3 (Lack of Public Acceptance)  
 
Score: +++ 
 
Partnerships have a high level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related to lack of public 
acceptance for solar PV utilities. Partnerships, especially those that include community participation or 
ownership, have proven extremely effective in boosting local acceptance of solar parks in Denmark. 
Engaging with stakeholders and offering community benefits are essential for fostering support 
Transforming residents into stakeholders can turn opposition into support. A notable case is Lemvig, 
where the municipal utility Jysk Energi partnered with local citizens to co-own renewable projects. 
Their model led to a solar park being approved entirely without objections – for the first time ever in 
Denmark [47]. Jysk Energi’s co-ownership model enabled local citizens to invest in renewable energy 
projects. The company covered all planning and land acquisition costs and committed to the project for 
25 years, including the establishment of environmental funds. Up to 49% of the project was offered as 
shares to local residents—first to neighbors, then to others in the municipality. Shares were sold at cost 
price, ensuring equal investment terms and profit potential for both citizens and Jysk Energi  [47]. By 
choosing to structure the project under the framework of Denmark’s “Køberetsordning” (ownership 
offer scheme), Lemvig Municipality establish the legal and procedural foundation that enabled Jysk 
Energi to engage in a co-ownership partnership with local citizens. According to the Renewable Energy 
Act (VE-loven), this scheme requires developers of wind or solar projects to offer at least 20% of the 
ownership to local residents [48].  
Lemvig’s political and administrative support for this model made it possible for Jysk Energi to 
implement a cost-price share offering, ensuring that citizens could invest on equal terms. This approach 
not only fostered local engagement but also strengthened public support for the project.  This 
==PAGE:39==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 34 of 59 
exemplifies that community-centric partnerships directly address aesthetic and land-use conflicts, 
making them a powerful tool to achieve local acceptance [49]. 
 
 
T2-A (Partnerships)/C4 (Technical Barriers)  
 
Score: + 
 
Partnerships have a low level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related to technical barriers for 
solar PV utilities. It is difficult to find literature with examples where direct partnerships between 
municipalities and actors involved in the development of solar parks has helped mitigate the specific 
technical challenge I have identified for solar parks projects. The use of soft governance partnerships 
appears to be limited to information pooling during the development of solar parks. An example of this 
could be the strategic energy community “ENERGI PÅ TVÆRS” [50]. However, this is more flow of 
information with at feedback than actual partnerships. 
 
 
T3-A (Public Awareness Campaigns)/C1 (Resource Limitations)  
 
Score +  
Public awareness campaigns have a low level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related to 
resource limitations for solar PV utilities. It was almost impossible to find any literature to support the 
hypothesis that Public Awareness Campaigns could help mitigate the challenge of scarcity of suitable 
land in any way. However, I did find some literature where it was pointed out that a well-informed 
public can advocate for policy changes and support initiatives that promote solar park development 
[51]. 
Lack of grid constraints are structural and economic, typically falling under Energinet’s or the 
developer's responsibility rather than the municipality’s informational efforts. For example, the 
Energinet capacity map clearly shows that many rural areas suitable for solar parks lack the grid 
capacity needed to absorb large-scale solar power, and Public Awareness Campaigns do not influence 
this physical limitation [9]. These campaigns might indirectly influence public support for projects, 
helping the municipality to be able to persuade third parties to remove or reduce the challenge with 
scarcity of suitable land or grid limitations. In general, the effectness of Public Awareness Campaigns 
must be considered rather limited in relation to this challenge. 
 
 
T3-A (Public Awareness Campaigns)/C2: Regulatory Barriers  
 
Score: 0 
 
Public awareness campaigns have little to no effect on mitigating challenges related to regulatory 
barriers for solar PV utilities. The direct impact on regulatory processes is limited, and policy decisions 
are often influenced by factors beyond public opinion [52]. 
 
 
T3-A (Public Awareness Campaigns)/C3 (Lack of Public Acceptance)  
 
Score +++ 
 
Public awareness campaigns have a high level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related to lack 
of public acceptance for solar PV utilities. Educational outreach that provides information and early 
engagement can improve acceptance by dispelling myths and involving residents when developing of

solar parks. NEKST highlights the Lack of Public Acceptance often comes because citizens were not 
properly involved 
 
 
T3-A (Public Awareness Campaigns)/C4: Technical Barriers 
 
Score: 0 
 
Public awareness campaigns have little to no effect on mitigating challenges related to technical barriers 
for solar PV utilities. Informing citizens about shifting electricity-consuming activities towards peak 
hours might help ease some of the consequences of this technical challenge. But municipal campaigns 
will have very limited impact, as the price is determined at a much larger scale than a single 
municipality, meaning that the issue of electricity prices being lowest when the solar park generates the 
most is not being addressed. And regarding the issues the grid faces in handling electricity during peak 
periods, the electricity still needs to be transported from the solar park to the consumer, which means 
that the grid problems also remain. 
The effectiveness of public awareness campaigns in a single municipality will have very limited effect 
in mitigating the technical challenge identified for solar parks [53]. 
 
T2-B: Ownership in district heating companies/C1: Resource Limitations 
  
Score: 0  
Ownership in district heating companies has little to no effect on mitigating challenges related to 
resource limitations for solar PV utilities.  
District heating offers many opportunities for sector coupling. However, if solutions are to be found 
between district heating companies and solar parks, it requires a robust electricity grid to connect the 
two. This means that the problem of insufficient grid capacity remains unresolved, with the ownership 
in district heating company.  
I found no literature indicating that district heating could be used to address the challenge of limited 
land availability for solar parks 
 
T2-B: Ownership in district heating companies/C2: Regulatory Barriers 
 
Score: 0 
Ownership in district heating companies has little to no effect on mitigating challenges related to 
regulatory barriers for solar PV utilities. As a tool, the ownership in district heating companies by 
municipalities offers no meaningful effect in overcoming the challenge of regulatory barriers in the 
development of solar parks. This is my assessment based on the fact that the extended literature review 
did not yield any sources containing theoretical or practical examples of the tool being, or potentially 
being, used in that manner - suggesting that such an application is either undocumented or not yet 
explored. 
 
T2-B: Ownership in district heating companies/C3: Lack of Public Acceptance 
 
Score: +  
 
Ownership in district heating companies has a low level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related 
to lack of public acceptance for solar PV utilities. Although ownership or co-ownership enables the 
municipality to support and accelerate the green transition through strategic decisions in electrifying 
district heating [6], I found no literature to substantiate the idea that municipal ownership of a district 
==PAGE:41==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 36 of 59 
heating company directly can or has been used to foster greater public support for solar parks. Therefore, 
any effect of such ownership of district heating on public acceptance for solar parks must be considered 
highly indirect - likely limited to the municipality’s enhanced ability to communicate with citizens, as 
ownership of district heating offers improved opportunities for information-sharing and grants access 
to a broader pool of knowledge.  And thereby compensate for possible lack of knowledge [22] in the 
interactions with the citizens which could potentially contribute to lower public acceptance with the 
large scale solar parks.  
 
 
T2-B: Ownership in district heating companies/C4: Technical Barriers 
Score: ++ 
Ownership in district heating companies has a moderate level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges 
related to technical barriers for solar PV utilities. Municipalities have, through their ownership of 
district heating utilities, a strong platform to advocate for hybrid systems in district heating networks, 
improving grid stability. Roskilde Municipality has eg. established several forums where the progress 
of various initiatives is coordinated and discussed with their ownership in eg. VEKS on district heating 
company rollout. [6] 
The solar parks can be combined with technology in the districh heating company as heat pumps or 
thermal storage and can thereby minimize the challenges with fluctuating power production from the 
large scale solarparks. 
For years, heat pumps in districh heating have contributed to system stability by aligning production. 
District heating companies can plan to operate heat pumps simultaneously to absorb this excess power. 
The heat generated can be stored in thermal storage systems, enabling heat supply during periods when 
renewable generation is limited—thus avoiding the need for heat production from boilers or other less 
sustainable technologies. From the power system's perspective, producing for thermal storage functions 
much like a battery, which is why this concept is often referred to as a "virtual battery" (Flørning, 
Holtegaard, & Thomsen, 2024, section: [54]. 
Municipal ownership enables strategic planning to optimize energy distribution and reduce grid 
problems for fluctuating electricity producting. For example, Roskilde Municipality uses its ownership 
stake in VEKS (a district heating transmission company) to coordinate heat distribution across regions, 
which can alleviate pressure on local electricity grids by prioritizing heat storage during low-demand 
periods [55]  [56]. 
However, the fluctuating electricity still needs to be transported from the solar park to the district 
heating facility before the electricity can be smoothed out. This requires planning district heating and 
solar installations close to each other, so the solar park owners could minimize the need for investment 
in secondary power cables. In any case, district heating can only be said to mitigate the challenges to a 
rather limited extent. 
 
 
T1-C: Zoning for Green Technology / C1: Resource Limitations 
 
Score: +++ 
Zoning for green technology has a high level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related to 
resource limitations for solar PV utilities. By designating specific zones for renewable energy projects, 
the municipality can use its role as the planning authority to zone and reserve land for large-scale solar 
parks. This enables long-term planning of eg. integrating solar development with nature conservation, 
==PAGE:42==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 37 of 59 
biodiversity, and recreational interests, helping to reduce conflicts over land use for solar installations 
[26]. 
At the same time, municipalities can designate certain zoned areas for solar development as 
multifunctional land-use sites by incorporating guidelines for grazing, biodiversity, and vegetation 
management in the local plan. This approach ensures that solar parks contribute not only to energy 
production but also to supporting nature. It allows solar installations in areas that might not otherwise 
be considered, thereby optimizing land use without compromising other interests. 
Overall, this combination of targeted zoning and multifunctional strategies enables more efficient and 
responsible land use. In this way, zoning becomes a strategic tool to expand the available land for solar 
parks, facilitating the long-term reservation of areas and aligning energy development with 
environmental, biodiversity, and community interests [37]. 
By designating specific zones for renewable energy projects, the municipality can reduce some of the 
challenges of not having sufficient grid capacity or not having major electricity consumers nearby  [9]. 
However, municipalities cannot directly fix grid capacity issues that Energinet controls. They rely on 
energy authorities to address these infrastructure limits in the zones they create [9]. 
However, it cannot be guaranteed that this tool will fully solve the issue of limited land or grid 
availability. Nevertheless, it can go a long way in mitigating the challenge, which justifies a high 
effectiveness score for the tool zoning of areas 
 
T1-C: Zoning for Green Technology / C2: Regulatory Barriers 
 
Score: +++ 
Zoning for green technology has a high level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related to 
regulatory barriers for solar PV utilities. However, its impact is largely dependent on sustained support 
from the state to effectively lift or relax rules in the zones. I have therefore assessed this tool as having 
a moderate level of effectiveness. 
But there are signs of state-level support for such municipal of zoning with relaxed regulations more 
permanent. The Danish Minister for Climate, Energy, and Utilities, Lars Aagaard, has stated: 
“We are well underway in laying the tracks towards a fourfold increase in renewable energy on land, 
not least with much more solar energy. Together with municipalities, the government is finding the best 
places for energy parks, which can include both solar and wind. We will simplify the paperwork and 
strengthen local processes with neighbours and consideration for nature”.  
[57] 
 
 
T1-C: Zoning for Green Technology / C3: Lack of Public Acceptance 
 
Score: ++ 
 
Zoning for green technology has a moderate level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related to 
lack of public acceptance for solar PV. Municipalities can designate areas for renewable energy 
development, a task that must be carried out with due consideration for nature, biodiversity, recreational 
and many more interests [26]. This gives municipalities the opportunity to reserve areas with the lowest 
potential for conflict early on. They can eg. zone areas away from homes and scenic landscapes to help 
reduce local opposition. 
 
==PAGE:43==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 38 of 59 
However, the challenge of lacking public acceptance is closely linked to the challenge of land scarcity. 
If we look at plans in the Danish local and national plans in all land use categories, the demand for land 
will exceed the amount of conflict-free areas available for reservation. According to the report “Fra 
modstand til medvind”, Denmark's overall plans require land equivalent to 130–140% of the country’s 
total area [8]. This means municipalities may be pressured into designating areas that are not entirely 
free of conflict. While zoning cannot mitigate all challenges related to local acceptance, it can help 
reduce them. For this reason, I assess the tool as having a moderate effect. 
 
 
T1-C: Zoning for Green Technology / C4: Technical Barriers 
 
Score: ++ 
Zoning for green technology has a moderate level of effectiveness in mitigating challenges related to 
technical barriers for solar PV utilities. Zoning of solar parks can to some extent help mitigating 
technical challenges related to fluctuating production by providing potential energy storage capacities 
for solar power, to mitigate the challenges of fluctuating energy production. But as we identified in 
section 2.2.8, it is the cables required to transport the electricity away from the solar PV utility that 
constitute the technical barrier. This means that the green zoning must be placed almost adjacent to the 
solar PV utilities to avoid the need for excessively long cables. Therefore, the zoning for solar parks 
does not directly resolve technical challenges of fluctuating production, because the fluctuating 
electricity production from the solar park still needs to be transported to the stabilizing units. Even with 
zoning, it will not always be possible to place the solar park and the stabilizing units close enough to 
each other, which is why I only evaluate zoning as having a moderate level of effectiveness of mitigating 
the technical challenge.

Green procurement policies have little to no effect on mitigating challenges related to resource 
limitations for solar PV utilities. 
The extended literature review found no justification to support that green procurement policies directly 
addresses land scarcity or grid capacity limitations in relation to the development of large-scale solar 
farms in Denmark.

All the grid-balancing investments that the municipality could make require a power grid connection 
between the solar park and the grid-balancing measures. Thus, the problem of the grid struggling to 
handle fluctuating power remains unresolved. The improvements brought about by grid-balancing 
investments would increase the value of electricity during peak demand periods, which would 
significantly improve the business case for the solar park developer and thereby attract more 
development. However, since price formation occurs in a much larger area than just Roskilde 
Municipality, this effect would be greatly reduced unless the municipality collaborates with other 
municipalities

As described in section 2.2.8. The main technical barrier of solar PV utility is its highly fluctuating 
energy production over time. And in at municipal scope, wish to seize more grid capacity than 
necessary. When choosing the right type of electricity istorage, it makes sense to look at the periods 
where we can be certain that the facility is not operating at full capacity, giving predictable low-
production periods to balance electricity generation across. Here, we have the nights and the seasonal 
shifts with limited daylight. Since, in this thesis, I regard seasonal shifts as a national matter, the 
following will focus on storage capacity over a 24-hour period, ensuring that we have the nights 
available for balancing power output.  
 
  
 
Source: 
[58] 
Note:  Short-term storage is provided by capacitors and coils, while batteries cover from hours to days. Pumped storage and 
heatstorage and compressed air extend to weeks, and power-to-gas (Power to X) enables large-scale, seasonal storage. Colors 
indicate storage types: chemical, thermal, mechanical, electrochemical, and electrical. 
 
In the figure, it is shown that batteries have the appropriate profile to handle the challenge of fluctuating 
energy throughout the day, as there are general battery solutions capable of discharging over periods 
ranging from minutes to several days.  
Therefore, we will now focus on utility-scale solar PV systems equipped with batteries. The challenge 
could be mitigated with sufficient storage batteries directly connected to the solar panels. Due to the 
delimitation of themes and the physical scope of this thesis, I will not go into detail about which exact 
battery capacity would be optimal for solar PV utilities to smooth out power production over a 24-hour 
period. Instead, I have chosen a battery pack capable of sustaining full load for 4 hours, as there is 
extensive available literature on this capacity in connection with solar PV utilities. In the following, I 
build on the assumption that a full-load 4-hour battery will have a significant mitigating effect on the 
fluctuating electricity that must be delivered to the grid. In Figure 5 below, comparisons are shown 
between solar PV utility + storage and other energy sources.

Figure 9 shows that when batteries are added for four hours of storage, it can compromise the business 
case for solar power in terms of LCOE, compared to fossil alternatives such as gas combined cycle. This 
means that it may be more challenging to attract developers willing to invest in the solar PV utilities 
because it is not necessarily competitive. As a result, certain solar PV utilities can still be competitive, 
since solar PV utilities with storage can be delivered at the 5th percentile for around $60/MWh (51 
Euro/Mwh), which is still lower than the 95th percentile cost of gas combined cycle at $108/MWh (92 
Euro/Mwh). And that is the Green Light Model’s definition of being financially resilient.  
 
4.3 Political resilience: Ability to achieve a positive business case in the long run   
==PAGE:48==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 43 of 59 
As explained in the methodology chapter, the ability to achieve a positive business case in the long run 
is essential for attaining political resilience. This section examines which technologies are capable of 
achieving a positive business case through their byproducts in the long run. Here, a global benchmarking 
scope should be applied alongside the local (national) scope. This because a positive business case in 
the international market for clean technologies is important to enable large-scale production and thus 
substantial technological development. In this way, it will be ensured that the municipality can build on 
its clean tech initiative with readily available spare parts for maintenance and replacement of 
installations as they reach the end of their lifespan. As explained in the methodology, we also examine 
the future business case of solar cells at a local level to estimate whether, and to what extent, the solar 
PV utilities will depend on subsidies in the future. 
We look at the average LCOE on both a local and global scale. I have chosen to use LCOE instead of 
IRR, as LCOE excludes taxes, subsidies and depreciation. And because I have realized that LCOE is 
what is used in the standard literature when benchmarking and for future estimations, whereas average 
IRR is almost impossible to find for that purpose - especially at the international level. IRR is more 
commonly applied to individual projects, and if one has to calculate a fictitious project independently 
for 2040, the uncertainties of the input data are so large that the result would be too random to base any 
analysis on. Therefore, since what we are examining are general trends, a generic municipality, and 
specifically the business case without subsidies or tax schemes, we choose LCOE.  
We start by looking at the global scope. Figure 10 below, shows the competitiveness of coal, solar, 
offshore wind and onshore wind based on their smoothed electricity costs (LCOE) [1]. Note that the 
fossil fuel benchmark in this table is based on coal instead of gas, because in the global scope, coal will 
still be used in some parts of the world in 2040. And since we are only examining whether solar cells 
will have any kind of international relevance in 2040, the ability to outcompete the most expensive 
fossil alternative in 2040 will be enough to assume that it is highly likely that this will provide solar 
some kind of international relevance in the market. 
Figure 10: Smoothed electricity cost (LCoE) 2020-2040 in EUR/MWh broken down by solar, landwind, oceanic wind and coal 
power. 
 
Source: 
[1] 
Note: For coal power plants, the starting point is “Combined heat and power 400-700 MW”

In this thesis, I have chosen to only set measurable criteria for the local scope when assessing political 
resilience. This is to limit the scope of the thesis in terms physical volume of this thesis.

investment is therefore in the Green Light Model defined as having an acceptable degree of political 
resilience. This is because the survival condition of solar PV has been freed from the most obvious 
political instrument, which is the prioritization of subsidies, since the technology can now compete in 
the market on the basis of a positive business case without subsidies.

The table provides the result of the resilience strength for each of the three areas: management resilience, financial resilience, 
and political resilience. The overall assessment of each technology is determined by the least feasible assessment among the 
three parameters. 
As seen in Table 17the solar PV utilities is not overall feasible because the clean tech cannot achieve 
resilience in all 3 resilience areas. Management resilience ends up getting the lowest assessment which, 
as outlined in the methodology section 3.5, then defines the overall assessment of the solar PV utilities. 
The aggregate assessment of the solar PV utilities is thus that the overall feasibility is uncertain.  
Again, it is also important to note that this thesis has not reviewed all of the municipality’s tools, which 
means that there may be other tools available that could mitigate the challenge more effectively. 
 
 
4.5 Validation of the model from interviews in selected municipalities  
The interviews are based on stakeholders from a specific municipality. The statements from the 
interviewed stakeholders will therefore reflect the conditions of that particular municipality, even 
though the interviewees’ broader insights into climate plans also come into play. I have chosen to use 
the inputs that can be regarded as relevant for our analysis of the generic municipality in my discussion 
and conclusion. I have chosen to isolate stakeholders from Roskilde Municipality in this analysis section 
as a case study. 
 
4.5.1 
Key points from stakeholder interviews exclusive in Roskilde Municipality (Case study) 
The analytical model developed in this thesis was first tested on a generic municipality to establish a 
baseline for how municipal governance tools can address the challenges of specific clean technologies. 
The interviews with stakeholders from Roskilde can then serve as a case study for Roskilde 
Municipality to apply the same model in a specific municipal setting, revealing where the outcomes 
align with, and where they diverge from, the generic assumptions. 
The five interviews conducted with key stakeholders in Roskilde Municipality — Karim Friis Arfaoui, 
Karina Hein Andrade, Kim Dalstrøm, Jeppe Trolle, and Trine Borre — confirm that the model’s five-
step rating scale is well-suited for municipal climate planning. In Roskilde’s context, the structured 
format was seen as a practical tool for identifying governance blind spots early and ensuring that climate 
initiatives match the municipality’s real capacity.  
Regarding the toolbox categories, Roskilde interviewees broadly confirmed their relevance. Tools such 
as zoning for green technology, partnerships, and public awareness campaigns are all actively used in 
Roskilde’s climate strategy. However, unlike the generic municipality, Roskilde’s stakeholders 
highlighted the need to explicitly consider political feasibility and the integration of technical expertise 
within the administration. For example, Karim Friis and Kim Dalstrøm stressed that without in-house 
technical competence, even well-designed tools risk losing impact. 
On the technology side, the key challenges identified in the generic model - grid capacity constraints, 
competition for land, fluctuating production, and public acceptance - were also confirmed as highly 
relevant in Roskilde. Grid limitations stood out as the most critical barrier; Jeppe Trolle and Karina 
Hein Andrade warned that delays caused by insufficient grid capacity could undermine local climate 
targets. Roskilde’s case also showed how these challenges can interact: grid bottlenecks can amplify 
public resistance if projects are postponed or scaled back. 
Overall, applying the model to Roskilde Municipality validates its usefulness in mapping municipal 
tools against technology-specific challenges while illustrating how a municipality’s unique political, 
==PAGE:52==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 47 of 59 
technical, and spatial conditions can shift feasibility outcomes. Compared to the generic municipality, 
Roskilde’s case underscores the added importance of technical expertise for achieving ambitious 
climategoals to realistic climate goals.

5 Discussion and Conclusion 
 
In the discussion and conclusion, we examine the results produced by the Green Light Model after 
analyzing the solar PV utility. We then consider what conclusions can be drawn about the usefulness 
of the model itself, and finally, we explore which clean tech solutions could be applied to the model as 
well as potential improvements to it. This will be based on insights gained through the analysis and the 
interviews. 
 
5.1 Results for solar PV utilities from the current version of the model 
If we first conclude on the analysis of solar PV utilities in a generic municipality. The result, as shown 
in Table 17 was that not all fields could receive a green light. Therefore, according to the Green Light 
Model, solar PV utilities may pose a risk to the climate goals set out in the generic municipality’s 
climate plan. 
The table also shows that it was in the area of Management resilience (Governance – Technology) that 
a green light could not be achieved indicating that the clean tech in this area has uncertain resilience. 
Figure 12 The Green Light Model result of analyzing Solar PV utilities 
 
The figure shows that Solar PV utilities do not achieve green light for management resilience in a Green Light Model 
assessment. Since the overall clean tech feasibility is defined by the measured combination with the lowest level of resilience, 
the technology cannot be classified as green. Solar PV utilities therefore end up in the yellow category, indicating that they 
cannot readily be recommended to the generic municipality on which the model has been tested. 
It is important to note that, due to space constraints in the thesis, not all of the municipality’s tools were 
reviewed. It is therefore possible that if all generic municipal tools had been examined, Policy resilience 
could have ended up with a green light. However, based on what is reviewed in the thesis, Table 12 
shows that it is specifically Challenge 4 – technical barriers - fluctuating energy production that cannot 
achieve green light. 
This means that the municipality runs a big risk of uncertainty of negative consequences from 
fluctuating energy production, which cannot be avoided if a municipality commits to Solar PV utilities. 
In other words, this model cannot recommend Solar PV utilities for the generic municipality. This is 
due to the fluctuating energy production, because it will occupy excessive grid capacity relative to its 
average production. 
==PAGE:54==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 49 of 59 
 
For this clean tech, its occur that the clean technology selected for evaluation using the method is not 
feasible because the clean tech cannot achieve resilience in all 3 areas. The lack of resilience can again 
be pinned down to the absence of management resilience. And the lack of management resilience can 
again be pinned down to the municipality not having sufficient tools to mitigate Challenge 4: the 
technical barriers associated with solar PV utilities. The technical barrier in the form of fluctuating 
energy production cannot be mitigated with the municipality’s tools. However, section 394.2 shows 
that the same challenges might be mitigated economically by combining the clean tech with economic 
mitigation initiatives. In this case, by combining the solar PV utilities with 4-hour battery storage. Table 
18 shows how the economic means from the section on finance may compensate for the municipality’s 
lack of ability to mitigate Challenge 4 with the tools at their disposal.  
 
 
Table 18: The mitigating capacity of management tools or financial instruments. 
The table presents management resilience and financial resilience, subdivided according to the different challenges faced by 
Solar PV utilities. 
 
This setup can be viewed as a single clean tech solution. It is important to keep in mind that it is only 
Challenge 4 within financial resilience that has been examined in the Green Light Model for the 
combined technology of solar PV utilities + 4h batteries. The rest of the results in the Green Light 
Model are still based on solar PV utilities as a standalone clean technology. We don’t know if the 
combined technology might be compromised on other challenges or resilience areas. Therefore, the 
combined solar PV utility with a 4-hour battery should be fully assessed through the GLM. This would 
determine whether the combined technology can achieve resilience across all three resilience areas, and 
thereby attain overall feasibility. 
 
5.2 Conclusions on the usefulness of the Green Light Model 
The results of this thesis show that municipal tools vary greatly in their ability to mitigate the 
challenges associated with large-scale solar PV utilities. Some challenges, such as lack of 
public acceptance, can be mitigated through stakeholder engagement measures like public 
awareness campaigns and partnership tools. Others, such as regulatory hurdles and resource 
limitations, can be mitigated through municipalities’ strategic use of planning authority 
instruments, for instance zoning for green technology. However, fluctuating electricity 
production proved far more difficult to mitigate with municipal tools alone—at least within 
the scope of the tools examined in this thesis. It therefore cannot be ruled out that if the full 
Green Light Model were applied, the additional tools might have been able to mitigate these 
technical barriers as well. 
Another functional implication of the model is that financial resilience, in some cases, can be 
used to compensate for municipal tools. If municipal tools alone cannot mitigate a 
technology’s challenges, they may still be mitigated through economic solutions—such as 
battery storage. In such cases, the technology and its mitigation measures can be treated as an 
integrated cleantech package and re-evaluated within the model.

==PAGE:55==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 50 of 59 
 
By running the Green Light Model with the currently selected tools and in their present form, 
a key insight regarding solar PV utilities is that the utility cannot be considered fully feasible 
without coupling it to battery storage. It’s important that the batteries are directly connected 
to the solar PV utilities; otherwise, they do not mitigate the excessive strain that fluctuating 
production places on the grid. Without storage, it occupies valuable grid capacity during peak 
production, creating bottlenecks that can delay or block other renewable projects. Running 
the Green Light Model on “solar PV utilities with integrated batteries” as a combined 
cleantech package could therefore provide a test of feasibility of the solar PV utilities with 
intergrated batteries. 
Stakeholder interviews revealed that while many municipalities acknowledge grid limitations 
and fluctuating energy production, they continue to plan large-scale solar projects even 
though it is external actors, such as transmission system operators, regulators, or private 
developers, that will mitigate these issues. This dependency weakens the resilience of 
municipal climate strategies, since external decisions fall outside local control. In such cases, 
feasibility depends not only on technical and economic factors but also on the reliability of 
external actors to implement complementary mitigation measures. Therefore, benefit-sharing 
schemes such as energy communities were also mentioned as potential ways to mitigate these 
barriers. 
It is also important to emphasize that in this thesis the Green Light Model has been applied to 
a generic municipality to test its general applicability. In practice, however, the model would 
be used and adapted to the specific context, capacities, and priorities of individual 
municipalities. This flexibility is central to the model’s usefulness, as no two municipalities 
face the exact same preconditions, which will influence the mix of technical challenges, 
governance tools, and economics. 
Looking beyond the municipal scale, several interviews highlighted that some barriers, e.g. 
national grid constraints, can only be mitigated through coordinated national action. This 
opens the possibility of applying the Green Light Model at the national level, for example by 
testing the Danish National Energy and Climate Plan against the Paris Agreement.. 
In sum, the Green Light Model provides clarity on how the municipality’s tools mitigate the 
specific challenges posed by different clean technologies in the NECP. The Green Light 
Model equips decision-makers with a structured approach to prioritizing clean tech initiatives 
in relation to the clean tech’s feasibility for the specific municipality before committing. This 
will ensure greater certainty that municipalities can achieve their ambitions for CO₂ 
reductions. Since municipal ambitions contribute to the national targets, these objectives will 
likewise gain greater certainty of being achieved. This will help ensure better implementation 
of the National Energy and Climate Plan. 
 
5.3 Further Study  
 
There are promising opportunities for further study of the model. This includes incorporating interview 
insights directly into the analysis, applying the model to a wider range of technologies, and testing it on 
a specific municipality. In addition, there are rich opportunities for further development of the model 
into new versions with expanded capabilities. 
==PAGE:56==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
Page 51 of 59 
5.3.1 
Areas for more and better use of the model 
• 
Improve the current analysis of solar PV utilities in the model by integrating key insights from 
the interviews and carry out the completion of the remaining tools for solar PV utilities in the 
Green Light Model.  
• 
Carry out a analysis of solar PV utilities for a specific municipality. 
• 
Examine the coupling of solar PV utilities and batteries with capacities other than 4h. 
• 
Running the Green Light Model for solar PV utilities integrated with batteries as a single 
cleantech package. 
• 
Running the Green Light Model for rooftop PV in urban settings to compare its feasibility 
profile with large-scale solar parks. 
• 
Running the Green Light Model on various long-term storage technologies to assess their 
ability to complement intermittent renewable generation. 
5.3.2 
Improvements and further development of the model 
• 
Integrate keypoints from interviews to improve model. 
• 
Further expansion of the Green Light Model with a risk matrix incorporating risk (probability) 
and consequences (magnitude) based on the Green Light Model’s analysis results 
• 
Developing a national-level application of the Green Light Model – National-Version to align 
the NECP-targets with Paris Agreement. 
• 
Running the Green Light Model – National-Version on seasonal electricity storage capacity 
covering half a year, essential if a country like Denmark is to rely partly on solar power. 
With the many opportunities for further study of the method, and the positive feedback from the 
interviews, I clearly see adapted versions of the Green Light Model as a valuable supplement to the 
analytical tools municipalities use when deciding which clean tech initiatives to commit to and in what 
way. In my own work as a politician, I can say that my political colleagues and I have often lacked the 
combination of insight and overview that the Green Light Model provides as its final output.

1. Interview protocol 
 
Element 
Description 
Purpose 
To collect qualitative knowledge on municipal tool application and challenges 
of clean tech, and stakeholder reflections on the model and its outcomes. 
Interview Format 
Semi-structured, approx. 60 minutes. 
Participants 
Key persons with insight into climate and clean tech, from a municipal 
governance, business, and/or policy perspective. 
Data Handling 
Interviews will be recorded (with consent), transcribed, and thematically 
analyzed. 
Ethical Considerations 
Participants informed of: 
- Purpose 
- Voluntary participation 
- Anonymity option 
- Data usage and storage 
- Consent is obtained before the interview. 
Analysis Method 
Compile keypoints from the interviews and use these in my analysis and 
discussion in the thesis. 
Roles 
Troels Toft is interviewer and responsible for analysis and use in the thesis.
