4. Interview transcripts  
Note! All interview transcripts have been translated from Danish to English. 
2025-06-11 T10:40:12Z _ Interview_Karina Hein Andrade 
Troels Toft: Hi, my name is Troels Toft, and today I’ve invited Karina for a meeting to go through my report, 
evaluate it, and interview her about what she thinks works well, what works less well, and where there might 
be room for input. First, I just want to ask if I may record the interview. 
Karina Hein Andrade: Yes. 
Troels Toft: Can you tell me a bit about yourself? 
Karina Hein Andrade: My name is Karina Hein. I’m a senior consultant and team leader of the Climate and 
Energy Team in Roskilde Municipality. In this role, we act as the authority on heating, we work as energy 
planners, and we also comment on the board agendas of our utility companies, and so on. My background 
throughout my career has been in various types of energy planning – everything from biogas plants to wind 
turbines, district heating, and individual solutions. 
Troels Toft: Thanks for that. What’s your educational background? 
Karina Hein Andrade: I studied geography at the University of Copenhagen. 
Troels Toft: Great, thanks. Let’s dive in. I sent you some material in advance, and I know you’ve skimmed 
through most of it. I thought we could start by looking at the overall framework and then work our way 
backwards – where we also have combinations of tools and challenges laid out side by side. But starting with 
the overarching table where the evaluation is laid out. If we look at partnerships, and maybe resource 
limitations too... In my report, I identified land scarcity as a key issue, and I concluded that partnerships could 
help alleviate that by enabling dual-use of land. I concluded it would probably end up being rated as a two – 
because it doesn't solve everything. But what do you think, if we take just that one? 
Karina Hein Andrade: Yes, I actually very much agree. I think it's a relatively effective tool, because it 
enables you to influence through dialogue in these partnerships, like discussing what should be planned where 
in the municipality. And as you said, getting multifunctional land use is always a good thing. We need to 
protect groundwater in certain areas, we want more biodiversity, and we also want renewable energy 
production. So yes, I think it's a good approach and definitely something municipalities need to keep in mind. 
Troels Toft: Cool! Great answer. What I take away from your response is that I might want to reconsider the 
rating – whether it should be a “low” effect or maybe a “moderate” effect. I originally chose “low” because 
the resource shortage is so severe – even the most skilled municipal employee can only address a small part of 
the problem. But I’ll keep your input in mind. Thanks. Next, we have the regulatory hurdle, and how 
partnerships might help overcome that. Regulatory hurdles – like the complexity of the approval process for 
solar – seem, to my understanding, quite tricky. Maybe I’m overstating it. But I see it as complex, and I’m 
unsure how much partnerships can really help. I rated it a two, based on some success stories from dialogue 
with solar developers. What are your thoughts on that? 
Karina Hein Andrade: Yes, I definitely agree. In fact, sometimes partnerships are a prerequisite for getting 
good projects off the ground. At first glance, it may seem like a low impact, but as you said, the processes are 
complex, and it's extremely important that people understand each other. We all want the same outcome, but 
sometimes there's mutual mistrust between developers and the municipality – it goes both ways. Getting 
==PAGE:73==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 14 
through the process isn't just about ticking boxes and writing the right things – sometimes it's hard to 
understand what authorities are actually thinking and aiming for. So partnerships can be a great way to build 
mutual understanding and ease those difficult processes. 
Troels Toft: Is there a way to formalize these partnerships? 
Karina Hein Andrade: That’s a really good question. 
Troels Toft: And when we’re talking about partnerships here, it's in the context of soft governance. So it's still 
informal. I’ve personally found it difficult to identify strong examples. 
Karina Hein Andrade: Yes, because everyone is supposed to be treated equally in the approval and review 
processes. So maybe it's more about incorporating it into the actual review process – starting from a place of 
dialogue before the formal process begins. You could also institutionalize a regular feedback loop, where you 
meet with developers and talk about what works and what doesn’t – a kind of ongoing evaluation. 
Troels Toft: Do all approvals need to be in place before the tender phase, or can some come afterward? 
Karina Hein Andrade: The process I’m especially thinking of is what we have in Roskilde Municipality, 
where you can submit a preliminary application showing that it's a good project. You have to explain why you 
want it there, how big it is, provide visualizations, etc. That’s the phase where I think partnerships can really 
help. If your project isn’t approved at that stage, you won’t get to the local planning phase. So the better 
prepared you are at that early stage, the better the outcome. 
Troels Toft: So what I hear you saying is that it might actually be beneficial to focus partnerships before the 
tender phase. Whereas intuitively, advisors might focus more after the tender. 
Karina Hein Andrade: Yes, absolutely. 
Troels Toft: So there’s value in building trust in the materials and the process early on. 
Karina Hein Andrade: Exactly – building some trust in the process the municipality follows, and mutual 
understanding. 
Troels Toft: Great point. Thanks. Now let’s move on to governance. Or actually, partnerships in relation to 
lack of public acceptance. In the case of solar, I had identified two kinds of resistance: one based on visual 
objections – people thinking it's ugly – and the other based on disagreement over land use: should it be used 
for biodiversity, for something else? I rated this a four, because I found examples where partnerships helped 
align interests – e.g., raising the solar panels so that the area beneath can be used for biodiversity. That’s why 
I rated it so highly. But maybe that was too optimistic? 
Karina Hein Andrade: I actually think it’s not far off. Dialogue really can have a significant impact in this 
area. Whether it's a four, I don’t know – maybe slightly lower – but it’s definitely a strong lever. This tool 
allows you to reconcile multiple interests. For example, building trails in open countryside is usually really 
difficult, but they’re so important to local residents. You can solve that through these partnerships. People 
might accept the solar panels if they get a trail in return – so they don’t have to walk or cycle on a dangerous 
road without a bike lane. 
Troels Toft: So this can bring peace to the area. 
==PAGE:74==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 15 
Karina Hein Andrade: Exactly. Just being able to move safely from A to B can make a huge difference in 
acceptance. Even if someone dislikes the look of solar panels, the benefit they get – like a bike path – can 
offset that. 
Troels Toft: But are there limits – like situations where partnerships won’t help? 
Karina Hein Andrade: Definitely. Once it becomes about personal property rights – “this is my land, my 
view” – there will always be people you can’t move. But you don’t need to move everyone. You just need to 
get enough people on board – often by offering something in return. 
Troels Toft: That makes sense for solar. I suppose once you’re past the land scarcity issue, the remaining 
issues are like a sum of different smaller problems – and partnerships can be weighed against those collectively. 
I also found examples of developers offering a small ownership share to locals, which seemed to drastically 
reduce opposition. I think that’s what got me excited. 
Karina Hein Andrade: Yes, that's similar to what we talked about earlier with energy communities. It's still 
a bit untested, but the idea is that you could get a benefit on your electricity bill, making it cheaper for your 
household. 
Troels Toft: So that’s driven by the utility companies? 
Karina Hein Andrade: It can be. I have the same theory when it comes to heating: if you own part of your 
heating utility and benefit from that ownership, it matters. That idea of shared benefit is becoming more 
important – and I think we’ll see that with wind power too. 
Troels Toft: I thought it had to be formal shares – but that’s not necessary? 
Karina Hein Andrade: No, it doesn’t have to be. There can be agreements about cost savings that follow the 
property, for instance. But you can still do ownership shares if you want. That’s been done in the past. Troels 
Toft: Okay. 
Karina Hein Andrade: The challenge with shares is that only a certain group can afford to buy in – and others 
can’t. So with cost savings, you can potentially spread the benefits more evenly. 
Troels Toft: We’ve now gone through some parts systematically. Now I’d like to ask you: do you think the 
challenges I identified for solar are the right ones? I had: climate tasrgets, land scarcity, application complexity, 
and public acceptance – both visual and land-use conflicts. And on the technical side, there was the fluctuating 
nature of solar and grid limitations. Even with lots of storage, you’d still have grid capacity issues. Is there 
anything you think I’ve missed? 
Karina Hein Andrade: No – now that you list them again, I think you’ve captured the main ones. Those are 
the same challenges we generally see. 
Troels Toft: And we briefly reviewed the tools beforehand – are there any you remember that I haven’t 
included? 
Karina Hein Andrade: No, I think you’ve covered things well. Partnerships are hard to describe, and their 
local effect can vary more than people realize – even within different parts of the same municipality. So the 
impact is somewhat unpredictable. We sometimes try to bring behavioral science into it. There are different 
ways to approach partnerships depending on how committed you want to be as an authority. So yes, I agree 
with the tools you’ve chosen – with some nuances that need to be addressed locally. 
==PAGE:75==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 16 
Troels Toft: That makes sense – the report is based on a generic municipality. And yes, you’re absolutely 
right that local variations matter. I’m interviewing other municipalities too to get a broader picture. Is there 
anything in the evaluation model you felt didn’t quite fit? 
Karina Hein Andrade: No, I think it works well. The categories are differentiated enough to compare 
different factors. I think the model functions quite nicely. 
Troels Toft: I also had some questions about stakeholder risks. From your perspective, what’s the risk to a 
municipality if a solar project goes wrong? Any experiences? 
Karina Hein Andrade: I think solar is actually one of the less risky technologies. What’s the worst that could 
happen? Maybe a permit is granted but the installation never gets built – perhaps due to overproduction in 
Denmark or declining demand. But if you’ve ensured that the land isn’t used for intensive farming, maybe 
added a land covenant or voluntary agreement, then you’ve still made progress. You could use the land for 
biodiversity or another purpose. So overall, it’s not that high-risk – at least not compared to something like 
district heating, where consequences can be more severe. 
Troels Toft: That’s helpful – I had a risk scale with consequences, and you’re right: things might go wrong, 
but if the consequence is low, that’s okay. It lies a bit outside the scope of my report, but I’ll touch on it briefly. 
This was actually my final question. I built this as a tool – thinking a municipality might use it to navigate 
various themes. Do you think that’s realistic? 
Karina Hein Andrade: Yes, I think so. It encourages reflection around decisions and processes – so people 
don’t just run on autopilot. It helps you stop and think: what impact will this actually have? And where could 
we get even more out of it? 
Troels Toft: Thank you! Karina Hein Andrade: You’re welcome 
. 
2025-06-20_Interview_Jonas Bjørn Whitehorn 
Troels Toft: I’ve invited you here today, Jonas. Is it okay if I record the interview? 
Jonas Whitehorn: Yes, that’s fine. 
Troels Toft: Could you tell me a bit about yourself? 
Jonas Whitehorn: My name is Jonas Bjørn Whitehorn. I’m a city council member in Køge Municipality. 
Troels Toft: And what’s your background? 
Jonas Whitehorn: I have a professional background as a project manager for climate and energy in Dragør 
Municipality, and I’ve worked in that field for many years. 
Troels Toft: And you also have political experience? 
Jonas Whitehorn: Yes, as mentioned, I’m on the city council in Køge, and I’m also the network coordinator 
for the Green Network in the Social Democrats. 
==PAGE:76==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 17 
Troels Toft: That combination—both technical and political—is what makes you very relevant for this. My 
project compares challenges tied to various technologies implemented in municipalities with the tools available 
to municipalities, in order to assess where they have the right instruments, where they don’t, and to what 
degree. Also, it evaluates whether they need to act and how, and what risks they run. It's a form of preliminary 
analysis before risk assessment. Let’s jump into the evaluation matrix, which is a table that matches the 
challenges with the municipal tools. We can start with the first one, looking at solar parks and soft 
governance—partnerships. I rated it as low to moderate effect. I saw potential in soft partnerships that could 
bring stakeholders together for multifunctional land use, potentially reducing disputes around whether land 
should be used for biodiversity or solar energy. But I didn’t find any clear connection to the electricity grid 
issue. What do you think? 
Jonas Whitehorn: I think you should rate it quite low. In many cases, if you want large solar parks, they need 
to be locally accepted. Many projects fail not because of technology or economics, but due to local resistance. 
So, creating partnerships that add local or community-level value is absolutely crucial. The government has 
regulated away the so-called “Tønder model,” where the municipality would negotiate a holistic package with 
the developer—such as integrating recreation or nature into the project. Instead, it has become a financial issue 
where developers pay a fixed amount. But what citizens want is not just cash in a green fund—they want 
integrated models. Developers often want that too. Many design for recreational use and nature, but have no 
chance to discuss it with the municipality. If regulation allowed it, it could really promote these kinds of 
projects. 
Troels Toft: I see your point. Trying to make the project more socially acceptable without involving the local 
people just doesn’t cut it. Partnerships offer a way to include locals with insights about how the project could 
serve their area more directly. That’s how I hear you. 
Jonas Whitehorn: Yes, that helps depolarize the debate. You also mentioned multifunctionality—solar 
installations can, for example, protect groundwater. But the current system separates everything, which makes 
synergies hard to develop. 
Troels Toft: So the municipality could say to developers: “Talk to our water utility and come back with a joint 
proposal.” But under current regulation, that’s not allowed. 
Jonas Whitehorn: Exactly. 
Troels Toft: Let’s jump ahead. If we take public awareness campaigns as a municipal tool and hold it up 
against the challenge of public acceptance—especially visual impacts—I rated the effect as moderate. The idea 
is that campaigns can help, but they won’t solve everything. There are studies showing that if all solar plans 
were implemented, they’d cover more than 100% of Denmark’s land area. So no matter how well you 
campaign, some challenges will remain. That’s why I rated it a 3. What do you think? 
Jonas Whitehorn: Local resistance is very instinctive and deeply rooted. It’s often masked with various 
rational arguments, but really, it’s emotional. The visual aspect is extremely local. There’s also a broader 
debate about land use—some perceive solar parks as an attack on agriculture, even though farmers are often 
the ones driving the development. I don’t think campaigns are very effective either way. What’s more effective 
is giving locals a stake in the project—cheaper electricity or access to nearby nature. For example, the Outdoor 
Council found that rural residents actually have less access to nature, because fields dominate the landscape. 
Solar parks can improve that. 
Troels Toft: That’s very interesting. So partnerships might actually be more effective than awareness 
campaigns, which just try to persuade people that they’re wrong for disliking solar panels. 
==PAGE:77==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 18 
Jonas Whitehorn: Right. Campaigns often polarize. They position the sender in opposition to the community. 
Partnerships offer a chance to sit down with people over dinner and work things out—even if you don’t change 
the appearance of the solar park, you can change the surrounding conditions and create shared benefits. 
Troels Toft: Let’s move on to ownership of district heating and its relevance to the technical barrier of 
fluctuating energy production. I rated it a 2, thinking that district heating companies might implement hybrid 
systems, like thermal storage, and thereby relieve grid pressure by absorbing excess electricity. What’s your 
take? 
Jonas Whitehorn: I think there’s huge potential in sector coupling—especially between district heating and 
green electricity. The problem isn’t lack of solar projects or financing. The problem is demand for green 
electricity. District heating has a unique ability to shift demand—not just hour by hour, but even week by 
week. It can take in electricity when it’s green, store it as heat, and use it later. Storing heat is around 100,000 
times cheaper than storing electricity. So when green electricity is abundant, district heating should absorb it. 
Troels Toft: What slowed me down from giving it a higher score was that it still depends on the electricity 
grid to transport power from solar farms to district heating facilities. 
Jonas Whitehorn: In Køge, a developer wants to set up a few wind turbines. One would supply electricity 
directly to the local district heating plant, which currently runs on straw. It would be a direct connection. You 
could imagine the same with solar. 
Troels Toft: So proximity between solar farms and heating plants is crucial? 
Jonas Whitehorn: I don’t think proximity is necessarily decisive. The point is: we need demand for green 
power when it’s available. Otherwise, negative electricity prices increase, and renewable expansion stalls. 
District heating can fix that—absorbing surplus electricity and converting it to useful heat. 
Troels Toft: I didn’t evaluate ownership of water utilities, but Karim from FORS mentioned that drinking 
water systems also consume a lot of energy. If you have fluctuating energy production, like excess solar, you 
could use basic electric heating—like a submerged heating element—to dump energy usefully. 
Jonas Whitehorn: Right. Also, solar parks require major grid expansion. If the grid can send power in both 
directions, it’s more efficient. Some projects now combine wind and solar to make better use of cables, since 
wind and sun produce at different times. 
Troels Toft: I see. Having a base load—for example, a water utility—near a solar plant could help justify the 
grid investment. That’s valuable. 
Jonas Whitehorn: Plus, there are other advantages from multifunctional land use. Water utilities can now 
supply “technical water”—like treated wastewater—which can be used to extract heat and feed it into district 
heating. That’s another sector coupling. The municipality, as planner, regulator, and owner, plays a key role. 
Troels Toft: And there might be tolerance for slight variations in temperature in district heating systems—say 
48 instead of 50 degrees—which lets you integrate fluctuating sources like heat pumps. 
Jonas Whitehorn: Yes. District heating is ideal for variable heat production because storage is cheap, and 
delivery doesn’t have to be instantaneous. 
==PAGE:78==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 19 
Troels Toft: That makes sense. Moving on: what about the municipality as an enterprise? For instance, 
adopting a green procurement policy. Initially I thought it could be very useful, but I struggled to find strong 
examples of it solving actual problems. Your thoughts? 
Jonas Whitehorn: I’d start with the regulatory issue. Many citizens look at public buildings and wonder why 
there aren’t solar panels on them—before we put them on farmland. Politically and ethically, that’s a valid 
concern. Yet municipalities are blocked by electricity market rules from installing panels on their own roofs. 
There are legal issues around whether municipalities can even buy electricity from their own panels. That’s a 
regulatory barrier. 
Troels Toft: And if those restrictions didn’t exist? 
Jonas Whitehorn: Then many would do it. Solar on rooftops makes political and practical sense, especially 
since many municipal buildings use a lot of power when the sun shines. It might not be cheaper than grid 
power, but it has value. 
Troels Toft: So it’s not scalable, but as pilot projects they’re useful. Still, when scaling up, large solar farms 
offer cheaper electricity per kilowatt-hour. 
Jonas Whitehorn: But the larger the park, the more power you need to transmit. Locally produced power 
reduces transmission needs. In Denmark, the market largely determines transmission costs—except for 
municipalities, which are stuck with additional taxes and skewed incentives. 
Troels Toft: I’ve seen that. Once you strip away all the subsidies and special taxes, small-scale solar often 
struggles to compete economically. 
Jonas Whitehorn: I don’t think it has to compete one-to-one. If municipalities want to buy their own 
electricity, and they accept that it costs slightly more, that’s the market at work—if the added value is worth it 
politically. 
Troels Toft: What about “green procurement” in general? 
Jonas Whitehorn: It’s a good way to exercise public purchasing power, but the public sector is not one single 
entity. 98 municipalities buying 70 different things in different ways doesn’t send a clear signal to the market. 
Meanwhile, some municipalities spend excessive time defining what counts as “green.” More direct 
interventions in their own operations might be more effective. 
Troels Toft: That’s consistent with what I found. When I tried to track successful examples, they were hard 
to find. 
Jonas Whitehorn: The scale you’ve built seems solid. But I’d recommend weighting the high-effect tools 
more heavily—maybe exponentially—so they stand out. 
Troels Toft: That’s a good idea. 
Jonas Whitehorn: And I think municipalities could use this kind of systematic evaluation in their climate 
plans. Often, these plans contain a list of tools that range from solid to wishful thinking. This model could help 
prioritize what’s actually effective. 
Troels Toft: And if a tool scores very low, maybe the municipality shouldn’t touch that kind of project at all. 
==PAGE:79==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 20 
Jonas Whitehorn: Exactly. Municipalities have limited resources. If they invest in tools that don’t work, they 
waste effort that could have gone elsewhere. 
Troels Toft: Like repair cafés—they sound great, but the climate effect is tiny compared to, say, direct reuse 
at recycling centers. 
Jonas Whitehorn: Yes. You need staff, a building, volunteers, and meetings... sometimes you end up with 
nice-looking projects that have almost no effect. 
Troels Toft: Thanks again. You’ve given me a lot of strong input I can build into the project. 
Jonas Whitehorn: My pleasure. Great discussion. 
Troels Toft: We’ll wrap up here. Thanks! 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2025-06-17_Interview_Karim Arfaoui 
Troels Toft: I’ve invited you today, Karim, to talk about my master’s project, which is about evaluating the 
municipality’s tools against the challenges associated with specific technologies that are being implemented 
==PAGE:80==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 21 
in municipal projects. My name is Troels Toft, and I’m currently doing a master’s in Sustainable Energy at 
DTU. First, may I record this interview? 
Karim A: Yes, that’s fine. 
Troels Toft: Would you like to tell me a bit about yourself? 
Karim A: My name is Karim Friis, and I’m the head of Planning, Water, and Wastewater at Aarhus, which is 
the utility company for Roskilde, Holbæk, and your area. So, through that, I work closely with the municipality 
to help realize their plans and projects related to both water and climate adaptation as well as district heating 
expansion. We’re also one of the utilities providing district heating to Roskilde and its citizens. 
Troels Toft: Thanks for that. And what’s your educational background? 
Karim A: I’m an engineer. I studied structural engineering, but my thesis was actually on environmental 
issues—specifically bathing water quality. Also at DTU. 
Troels Toft: Fantastic. Let’s jump right in. We’ll start with the evaluation form, and specifically with 
“Channels, Talks, Resource Limits, Missions,” and how partnerships might help mitigate those. We’re talking 
about solar panels here. What I’ve identified as key resource limitations in this area include a lack of available 
land. Some studies show that if we were to carry out all the planned solar projects in Denmark, we’d need 
130–140% of Denmark’s land area. So it’s obvious that land use will become a source of conflict. Another 
implementation challenge is the electricity grid—it’s difficult to find locations in Denmark where the grid has 
sufficient capacity. Even if we used batteries to stabilize the fluctuating power from solar panels, the grid 
infrastructure still presents a bottleneck. 
So those are the two main limits I’ve found. Then I looked into “partnerships” as a soft governance tool. I 
found that partnerships don’t really help much with those two issues—you can’t generate new grid capacity or 
create new land through partnerships. However, some progress can be made through land consolidation, where 
multiple interests collaborate on multi-purpose land use. That’s why I’ve given partnerships a 2 out of 4 in 
effectiveness. It’s still a relatively weak tool for addressing these particular challenges. What are your 
thoughts? 
Karim A: I’m a bit unsure whether partnerships are relevant here. But I’d like to bring up the Green Tripartite 
Agreement. While it’s mainly about nutrients and CO₂, solar panels are, of course, a way to reduce CO₂ 
emissions in energy production. In my work with drinking water, we’ve assessed whether solar panels could 
help protect groundwater, and we concluded they aren’t suitable because of the risk of PFAS contamination. 
Interestingly, the areas identified in the Green Tripartite Agreement for rewilding or protection don’t always 
align with groundwater interests. For instance, low-lying lands could potentially be used for solar panels if 
they’re not already prioritized for climate adaptation. There is some overlap between lands targeted for 
environmental protection and those that could support renewable energy. But it's important to communicate 
that land use must be multifunctional—combining energy production, nature, forestry, flood management, and 
even organic farming. That way, we maximize land value across different goals, which also makes the story 
easier to communicate to the public. 
Troels Toft: That’s very interesting. I want to explore that more in relation to lack of public acceptance and 
how partnerships might help address that issue. It creates a trade-off between land use expectations and actual 
needs. Another thing I wanted to ask, even though I haven’t developed background research on it, is about 
ownership or management of water utilities. My assumption was that this doesn't help solve grid or land-related 
challenges, but I’m curious what you think? 
==PAGE:81==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 22 
Karim A: No, I don’t think utility ownership really helps with those challenges. Unless you're talking about 
using available utility land for solar installations. We’ve done that in Roskilde West by the Margrethe reservoir, 
and at the Holbæk treatment plant. Utilities do have some land, so it’s possible to use rooftops, parking areas, 
etc., for solar. But we don’t have a significant amount of that type of space. 
Troels Toft: I also wondered if fluctuating solar power could be used to heat water—say, by using surplus 
electricity. Would that be a viable use? 
Karim A: Possibly, but it’s more a regulatory issue. For example, we have a heat pump system on Bjergvejen 
powered by wastewater. It runs best when electricity is cheap. But there are limitations: agreements with 
suppliers and the electricity grid constrain how much and when we can operate it. Also, during summer we 
don’t need much heat, and waste incineration plants are prioritized for heat delivery, so our system often can’t 
run when it’s most effective. 
Troels Toft: Isn’t incineration harder to regulate? I’d imagine they could just burn less waste. 
Karim A: I don’t know. It’s possible they prioritize based on electricity generation rather than heat needs. 
Troels Toft: You showed me a project in Holbæk as an example of multifunctional land use—organic farming, 
afforestation, grazing. That’s an interesting model. 
Karim A: Yes, and it could include solar panels too. We worked with the Organic Farming Foundation and 
the municipality to combine environmental and agricultural goals on that site. 
Troels Toft: Would you be able to send me that example? 
Karim A: I think so—I'll check with Martin, but it’s been approved by Holbæk’s city council, so I should be 
able to. 
Troels Toft: Great. Now, one of the municipal tools is planning authority—reserving areas for green tech. 
Like Risø Cleantech Park or zoning for solar farms. I’ve rated this tool as somewhat effective for managing 
fluctuating energy, because planning can accommodate storage capacity nearby—thermal storage or possibly 
even electric heating. 
Karim A: Isn’t that what thermal pits are for—absorbing excess electricity to heat water? 
Troels Toft: Yes, it’s more about shifting the energy to another time. Dipping a heating element into water is 
more about immediate consumption. So they’re related but serve different purposes. 
Karim A: True, but it could be coordinated with production companies that have flexible energy needs. 
Municipal planning can link energy production with potential consumers. A good example is how Roskilde’s 
electric buses are charged at a central station located near major power lines and a transformer station. That 
planning made the whole setup feasible. 
Troels Toft: That’s actually a strong example. I might have rated planning too low in my matrix. Maybe it’s 
a more effective tool than I thought. 
Karim A: It depends on the municipality’s industrial profile. If there are energy-flexible businesses, planning 
can be more effective. 
==PAGE:82==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 23 
Troels Toft: Could the municipality even purchase flexibility rights? Like the right to delay EV charging 
during peak hours? 
Karim A: That’s possible. A bus company, for example, could agree to only charge when electricity is 
cheapest—either from the grid or from local solar production. 
Troels Toft: That aligns with the upcoming collective grid model and vehicle-to-grid policies starting in 2026. 
Karim A: Using EVs as batteries to stabilize the grid—very smart! 
Troels Toft: I’ve rated the municipal tools on a scale. Low effect means they can inspire or encourage others. 
Moderate effect means they can influence business cases or make strategic decisions. High effect means they 
can guarantee the challenge is solved. Does that make sense to you? 
Karim A: Yes, your categories are well described. But it's important to provide explanatory background for 
each assessment. For example, you rated partnership effectiveness low, but with good reasoning and examples, 
it makes sense. 
Troels Toft: Thanks. I’ve extracted key examples and put them in appendices, so the main report doesn’t get 
too long. 
Karim A: Smart approach. Avoid making it too complicated. Your categories make sense, and I think your 
breakdown of municipal tools is fair. I especially liked your inclusion of ownership and partnerships. 
Troels Toft: That’s great to hear. I was trying to get a structured view of what municipalities can and cannot 
influence. 
Karim A: Just one more point: municipalities are politically led. That gives them some influence over national 
regulation—especially if KL (Local Government Denmark) speaks with a unified voice. 
Troels Toft: That’s helpful to include in the definition of “partnership.” Partnerships can involve private 
actors, research institutions, and political lobbying. 
Karim A: Exactly. Regarding planning categories, if you want to support solar for transport or logistics 
companies, use land use code 31 55—“Transport and Logistics.” You can always revise the zoning later if no 
one moves in. 
Troels Toft: Are there any major solar challenges I’ve overlooked? I’ve covered land constraints, grid 
limitations, complex permitting, public resistance, and technical intermittency. 
Karim A: No, I think you’ve covered it well—probably better than I could. 
Troels Toft: Final question: When municipalities run into issues they can’t fix—like grid capacity—should 
they avoid those projects entirely? 
Karim A: That’s not the municipality’s responsibility. The state has funding programs like MUDP and EUDP 
for that. Municipalities shouldn’t develop or test technologies themselves—they can support demonstration 
projects, but not lead R&D. 
Troels Toft: So if a project gets a low effectiveness score, the municipality should try to secure early 
commitments from grid operators or the state. That’s a form of risk management. 
==PAGE:83==

Karim A: Exactly. The state should prioritize projects based on cost-effectiveness and national impact. If 
Roskilde ranks high, fine—if not, another city’s project may make more sense to fund first.

especially during hype cycles? 
Karim A: Definitely. Municipalities already think about these issues, but this method adds systematics. It’s

Troels Toft: And if a department can’t answer certain questions, it might mean they need to hire someone or

Jesper G: Yes, my name is Jesper Grarup. I’m retired and an active member of the Green Transition Alliance

Municipality. I’ve been the lead on that area, and as part of that, I applied for and received a small amount of 
funding from the municipality to reach out to local village councils to get renewable energy projects going. 
It’s a bit of an uphill battle, but also very exciting. 
Troels Toft: So you’ve retired now. 
Jesper G: Yes, I’ve had a long career in research. I’m a trained veterinarian and worked as a practicing vet

pharmaceutical industry. Later, I moved into public hospital research—at Hvidovre Hospital, the Panum 
Institute, and Rigshospitalet—where I was part of a large research group responsible for raising 60–70 million 
kroner annually to keep the operation running. For the last five years of my career, I was head of research in 
Region Zealand, helping doctors across the region build networks, secure funding, and access tools to advance 
research as much as possible. 
Troels Toft: Thank you. We’ve reviewed the method, so let’s dive in. Jesper, I’ll start with a specific point. 
In front of you, you’ll see the evaluation matrix and the conclusion from the report. The idea is to assess tools 
in relation to specific challenges. I thought we could look at partnerships in relation to the grid challenge—
that the grid is currently underdimensioned. I’ve rated “partnerships” as a 2, and I’ll explain why. Then you 
can comment. I gave it a 2 because, with the grid, you can still regulate to some degree. What brought it up 
was the idea of dual-purpose landscapes, which connects to partnerships. 
Troels Toft: When we look at the grid limitations, there were some examples of energy communities that had 
come together and figured out how to create value—not only for the communities themselves but also to 
address the grid challenge. Yet, we’re still faced with an extreme land scarcity. So even with partnerships, you 
can't be sure someone will help solve the issue. You remain dependent on others’ willingness to help. I don’t 
know if you have any thoughts or feelings on that. 
Jesper G: There are some deeply complicated conditions. For example, implementing energy communities in 
Denmark is far more difficult than in countries like Spain or Poland. It’s EU legislation, yes, but Denmark has 
under-implemented it. The rules are overly complex. I live in a cohousing community where we’re installing 
solar panels. We have six housing blocks. Even though one block’s panels can supply all of us, we’re not 
allowed to share electricity across buildings—even though we’re all on the same grid connection. It’s 
completely absurd, and municipalities can’t do anything about that. So, for me, the tool municipalities should 
use is not just “soft power.” They should be more actively involved in organizing energy communities. The 
legislation allows for this. Municipalities can and should take a visible role, because energy communities 
challenge the old thinking that power must be centrally produced and distributed across massive networks. 
With modern technology, we can produce energy where it’s used. 
==PAGE:85==

Jesper G: I think municipalities should step up and say: This isn’t just soft governance. We are putting our 
skin in the game by actively helping to develop energy communities. That’s one key point. Another is that 
technically-minded people like us often forget the human side. We see technical fixes, but forget to consider 
what citizens actually want or need. When a solar park is proposed, some landowners benefit financially, and 
the municipality can tick its megawatt box and feel green. But no one asks: What do citizens want? How can 
we bring life back to villages that are dying out because there’s no shared purpose? For example, the town of 
Vor Frue no longer has a school past 7th grade. It’s imploding socially. But what if that community built its 
own heating system with a heat pump, became part-owner of a solar park, or even installed a wind turbine? 
That kind of ownership and pride could revitalize the village. 
Jesper G: The challenge is that most people think individually. They install their own heat pump and are done. 
No one wants to deal with the hassle. It requires passionate people. That’s where municipalities can help—by 
connecting those local champions, supporting them, and giving them tools and training. Because unless we 
revive the cooperative spirit that once defined Denmark, we’ll default to isolated solutions. And while 
individual heat pumps aren’t bad, they miss the broader potential of shared infrastructure. 
Troels Toft: That’s very interesting. When you talk about partnerships and locality, that’s actually the core

takes to produce them — there’s actually a huge spreadsheet, constantly updated, that accounts for all the 
elements in producing a solar panel: from raw materials to deployment, and even through their operational 
phase. So some solar panels will never actually break even in terms of CO₂ emissions — which feels

Jesper G: You can present it on screen and have a guide. But it’s all connected. Because so many procurements

Troels Toft: Exactly. Like if you had a policy that normally says “we buy green,” but someone still procures

centimeter per hour. When they start moving, tension arises everywhere. And how do you split that up? It’s 
been debated for decades. I remember at DTU, there was this April Fool’s joke: someone claimed they’d solved 
it — since Denmark wants to be free of nuclear power, they found a way to label electrons. Like, with spin — 
topspin or backspin — and that way, you could filter out nuclear-generated electricity. It even got some funding 
and momentum before people realized it was a hoax. Still, it illustrates how deeply we want to believe we can 
separate things.

Troels Toft: So what tool would you call that? How would you formulate it?

Kim Dalstrøm: And there are other examples where that has been done. Just look at the F-35 fighter jets.

APPENDIX      Page 40 
Troels Toft: Yes, they’re kicked off the grid—and that’s actually due to the grid challenges. So the issue isn’t

larger than for average consumption. 
Kim Dalstrøm: That’s mostly for the consumption side. 
Troels Toft: Unless you require the producer to bring the solution. You could say: fine, you want to build

Kim Dalstrøm: But if you think hypothetically—and I’m just brainstorming here—it’s about the balance

constantly decides: should we charge the car? Charge the home battery? Sell back to the grid? That kind of

distributed buffer capacity for overproduction from wind or solar. I don’t think the current grid can handle 
that, but it’s the kind of buffer model we could build toward. 
Troels Toft: That’s smart stuff. 
Kim Dalstrøm: And say I had a neighbor with a huge battery, and I needed power—why should I pull from

Troels Toft: And then there’s the whole issue of how you get the power from the solar park to the “smart

Kim Dalstrøm: But what if you just said—from the procurement perspective—we’ll choose the supplier who

Troels Toft: Yes—because they’re just using... 
Kim Dalstrøm: I saw a case like that—I think I understood it correctly. Someone built a new house with this 
kind of setup. And they said, even in a crisis, as long as you caught it at the right time—when the battery was 
charged—you’d have power for quite a while, for basic needs like radio or light. So I think there are multiple 
perspectives here—not necessarily a full solution, but something with potential. I just don’t think we have a 
grid that can handle it all. That’s probably why we don’t redesign the whole system. It’s wildly expensive. But 
if we create incentives to distribute that capacity—to homeowner associations, apartment complexes, single-
family homes—then maybe we can build a network-wide buffer system. 
Troels Toft: That’s really worth thinking more about. I’ll need to go home and reflect on that. 
Kim Dalstrøm: Yes, it’s just theoretical, right? But it’s an attempt to explore whether we could do something 
by setting those kinds of requirements. I think these solutions definitely cost something—getting that kind of 
software in place to manage the system, balance usage, calculate billing, and so on. But it really all starts with 
someone—maybe a municipality or a company—asking for it. And that’s really what we’re trying to explore 
here, I think. We can’t just replace our entire electricity system in one go. So maybe there are simpler solutions. 
The other thing is: we also know that it’s a shame we can’t harvest all the renewable energy. That we have to

waste it because there’s no system to use it at the right time when the demand is highest. That’s really our

Troels Toft: So, related to CHP plants. Actually, I do include tools like “risk management company” or “co-

Kim Dalstrøm: Well, you could say that energy from waste, for instance, has major potential. There’s no

store and feed it in when needed. So that could help balance the differences. And it can be a green solution—
especially if the incineration happens at large-scale facilities with proper flue gas cleaning. So I think, in some

Troels Toft: Yes—because you actually have the ability to ramp production up or down a bit. And if you had

Kim Dalstrøm: Exactly. 
Troels Toft: Or if you—again—looked at how to create synergy between installations so the logistical setup

Troels Toft: [00:18:27] It’s been a huge success. 
Jonas Paludan: [00:18:30] Exciting! When’s your deadline? 
Troels Toft: [00:18:34] I’ll end the interview officially here — now we can have a friendly chat. Thanks for 
everything!

Interview_ Jeppe Trolle 
Troels Toft: My name is Troels Toft, and I’m currently working on my thesis. I’ve invited you, Jeppe, for an 
interview, and you’ve kindly agreed. First, I’d like to ask if it’s okay that I record the interview? 
==PAGE:110==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 51 
Jeppe Trolle: Yes, go ahead. 
Troels Toft: Could you start by introducing yourself? 
Jeppe Trolle: My name is Jeppe Trolle, and I’m the chair of the Climate Council in Roskilde Municipality. 
The Council is an advisory body to the city council and the Climate Committee. We deal specifically with 
green transition and try to figure out what the municipality can do to reach our climate goals. I believe Roskilde 
has quite ambitious climate goals—we’re actually a bit more ambitious than the national targets, though we’re 
still heavily dependent on them. In addition, I’m a high school teacher; I teach social studies and economics at 
Køge Business College, and I’m a city council member for the Social Liberal Party (Radikale Venstre). 
Troels Toft: What’s your educational background? 
Jeppe Trolle: I studied at Roskilde University, where I majored in public administration and philosophy. 
Troels Toft: Thanks for that. Let’s dive in. I’ll just pick something to start with. What I’ve placed in front of 
you is the evaluation table where different tools are assessed against various challenges. Let's take this one. 
For instance, partnerships. 
Jeppe Trolle: Do you mean partnerships with businesses or...? 
Troels Toft: This is soft governance—it could be any kind of partnership. But we’re talking about the kind 
where perhaps the municipality hosts a meeting, shares some information, but there’s no contract. So I 
evaluated how well partnerships address the challenge of lack of public acceptance—like fears of falling house 
prices or disputes over land use. I rated it very highly. It seemed to address those concerns really well. But I’d 
like to hear your thoughts. 
Jeppe Trolle: It depends a lot on who the partnerships are with. A partnership with a company or a farmer 
who wants to build solar panels is probably easy—they have a financial incentive. But then you have unhappy 
neighbors who lose their view. If the partnership doesn’t include those neighbors, they might just see the farmer 
or developer as greedy capitalists. So it’s crucial that the partnership includes local residents. We do have tools 
where local communities receive compensation—maybe a new community center or bike path—but 
sometimes that’s not enough. Some people just don’t want to be "bribed." 
Troels Toft: Isn’t that kind of responsibility on the developer? Like in Gundsømagle? 
Jeppe Trolle: Yes, in that case we told the developer to go back and engage the local community. They failed, 
so we rejected the project. In contrast, Momentum and Ørsted were much better at managing local concerns—
they offered bike paths and nature access, and tried to plant vegetation around the panels to block the view. It 
didn’t convince everyone, but it helped. In another case, we told a company they hadn’t done enough, and they 
tried, but people just sat with crossed arms and refused to engage. Still, the city council sided with the residents, 
saying the company hadn’t done enough. Of course, we can't give people veto rights over everything, but we 
need to give them a fair say. Otherwise, partnerships become meaningless. 
Troels Toft: So you're actually pointing to a good example of soft partnerships working. 
Jeppe Trolle: Yes, but again, it really matters who’s involved. It makes a big difference if the partnership is 
with the local community, where they have control over a compensation budget, for example. That makes 
more sense. But I also don’t think neighbors should be able to control what happens on someone else’s land. 
That’s a political stance, of course. 
==PAGE:111==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 52 
Troels Toft: Let’s move on to the next category—like the district heating company example. 
Jeppe Trolle: So that's a district heating company? 
Troels Toft: Yes, exactly. Let’s say we, as a municipality, use that tool to address fluctuating energy 
production. 
Jeppe Trolle: You mean the issue with variable power supply? 
Troels Toft: Yes. So electricity production fluctuates, and the grid can only handle so much. Often, production 
has to be curtailed—even wind turbines are stopped not because the wind is too strong, but because the grid 
can't absorb the power. But with sector coupling, like using surplus power in large heat pumps connected to a 
district heating system, you can store that energy as heat. 
Jeppe Trolle: So you're suggesting that since the municipality partly owns the heating company, we could 
instruct them to absorb surplus power? 
Troels Toft: Yes. But as a board member, I can’t just tell them to build a heat storage unit—there are financial 
constraints. 
Jeppe Trolle: Right, they’re not allowed to run a deficit. I think it’s a great idea, but the current legislation 
makes it difficult. Still, there could be a viable business case if solar developers provide surplus power at low 
cost and heating companies use it for production. But it requires a decision and a strategy. 
Troels Toft: That’s why I rated it a 2—it has potential, but also significant limitations. 
Jeppe Trolle: I think there’s a lot of potential. For instance, we want to build large heat pumps in Viby and 
Jyllinge instead of every household installing their own. A centralized solution can more easily absorb surplus 
power. It’s easier to build a single power line to a collective system than upgrading hundreds of individual 
ones. 
Troels Toft: Good point. 
Jeppe Trolle: But again, we're limited by laws that require district heating companies to break even—no 
profit, no deficit. It’s incredibly complicated. 
Troels Toft: And municipalities can’t dictate strategy, because the companies are often set up as limited 
liability corporations. 
Jeppe Trolle: Exactly. And once they become joint-stock companies, it’s even worse. Board members have 
confidentiality obligations and can’t report back to the municipality. It all becomes more distanced from 
democratic control. 
Troels Toft: Still, you’re confirming that municipal ownership of heating companies can be a valuable tool. 
Jeppe Trolle: Yes, absolutely. But the legal limitations mean it’s only a “2” in practice, not because of lack 
of potential. 
Troels Toft: Let’s move on to the next category—like the district heating company example. 
Jeppe Trolle: So that's a district heating company? 
==PAGE:112==

APPENDIX      Page 53 
Troels Toft: Yes, exactly. Let’s say we, as a municipality, use that tool to address fluctuating energy

Jeppe Trolle: Yes, absolutely. But the legal limitations mean it’s only a “2” in practice, not because of lack

Troels Toft: Okay, I’ll jump to a slightly different tool: political-administrative coordination. I’ve given that 
a high score for enabling long-term planning. That’s because political leadership may change, but a well-
aligned administration can keep the direction steady. Do you see it that way? 
Jeppe Trolle: Yes, I think the Climate Council is a good example of that. It’s partly composed of city council 
members and partly of experts and citizens. It provides a sort of continuity regardless of political shifts. There’s 
==PAGE:113==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 54 
also the Climate Action Plan, which most parties have backed. It creates a sense of direction beyond the 
electoral cycle. Still, it only works if both the administration and politicians believe in it. 
Troels Toft: So it’s not self-sustaining? 
Jeppe Trolle: No. If a future city council says, “We don’t care about the plan,” they can just shelve it. But as 
long as there’s a shared understanding that it’s a good tool, it does help. 
Troels Toft: Great. Then I’d like to ask about technical barriers. Some municipalities say they want to install 
solar panels, but the grid can't handle it. They literally have to stop projects. 
Jeppe Trolle: Yes, that’s true. We’ve had several projects put on hold because of grid capacity issues. That’s 
one of the biggest challenges. We’re constantly being told by grid companies: “We can’t do this until we’ve 
built a new substation,” and that can take years. 
Troels Toft: Can municipalities do anything about that? 
Jeppe Trolle: Not much. It’s up to the grid companies—and they’re regulated in a way that makes them very 
cautious. They won’t invest unless there’s a guaranteed return. And the regulatory approval process is slow. 
Troels Toft: So that’s another example where the tool is weak not because of poor potential, but because of 
structural limitations. 
Jeppe Trolle: Exactly. 
Troels Toft: Let's talk about ownership models—specifically, local co-ownership of solar parks. Some 
developers are starting to offer co-ownership opportunities, where local citizens can buy shares. I’ve rated that 
tool quite high when it comes to increasing local acceptance. What’s your view? 
Jeppe Trolle: I think that makes a lot of sense. If people can see that they get something out of the project—
economically or socially—they’re more likely to accept it. It’s also a good way to democratize the energy 
transition. But it’s important that it’s real co-ownership, not just symbolic. People need to feel they have a 
stake and a voice—not just a tiny return on an investment. 
Troels Toft: So would you say it’s better than compensation funds? 
Jeppe Trolle: Not necessarily. Compensation funds can also be very effective—especially if they’re controlled 
locally. For example, a village that gets a new solar park could use the funds to renovate a playground, build 
a bike path, or support a community event. That can really strengthen community cohesion. But again, it has 
to be meaningful. If people feel like it’s just hush money, it can backfire. 
Troels Toft: And who should control those funds? 
Jeppe Trolle: Ideally, a local committee or board that includes residents. If the developer or municipality 
controls everything, people won’t feel ownership. That’s the key—ownership, in the broad sense, is what 
makes people support these projects. 
Troels Toft: I’d like to ask about procurement policy as well. Some municipalities have tried to use green 
criteria when they procure electricity, heating solutions, or even construction. Is that something you’ve worked 
with? 
==PAGE:114==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 55 
Jeppe Trolle: Not directly, but I think it’s a really important tool. If the municipality demands greener 
solutions in tenders, it pushes the market in that direction. But it can also be more expensive—at least in the 
short term. So you have to balance that with the budget constraints. But I think we should absolutely be more 
ambitious in procurement. 
Troels Toft: So the challenge is mostly financial? 
Jeppe Trolle: Yes, but also legal. There are EU rules about public procurement, and if you go too far with 
your requirements, you risk violating those. So it’s a balancing act. But if done right, it’s a very powerful tool. 
Troels Toft: Would you say there’s enough expertise in the municipality to use that tool properly? 
Jeppe Trolle: Probably not always. It requires a certain level of legal and technical knowledge to design 
tenders that are both ambitious and compliant. So there might be a need for capacity-building there. 
Troels Toft: One last thing. Looking at all these tools—some are soft, some are more formal, some involve 
partnerships, others focus on ownership or infrastructure—what do you think is the most important thing for a 
municipality like Roskilde to focus on right now? 
Jeppe Trolle: That’s a big question. I think the most important thing is to maintain momentum. We’ve set 
some ambitious goals, but they won’t achieve themselves. We need political will, administrative follow-
through, and local involvement. And we need to think holistically: green transition is not just about reducing 
CO₂—it’s also about social cohesion, local democracy, and long-term planning. 
Troels Toft: So it's about connecting the technical and the social? 
Jeppe Trolle: Exactly. We can build all the wind turbines and solar panels we want, but if people feel excluded 
or overridden, we’ll run into resistance. On the other hand, if people feel part of the process and see concrete 
benefits—whether that’s cheaper heating, new green spaces, or community funds—they’re more likely to 
support the transition. 
Troels Toft: Do you think Roskilde has a good foundation for that? 
Jeppe Trolle: Yes, I do. We have strong civil society, a reasonably engaged population, and a political 
majority that supports the green agenda. But we can’t take that for granted. It requires ongoing dialogue and 
real involvement—not just information campaigns. 
Troels Toft: Thank you. That’s a really good note to end on. 
Jeppe Trolle: My pleasure. Good luck with your thesis. 
 
Interview Transcript – Trine Borre 
Troels Toft: My name is Troels Toft, and I’m working on my master’s thesis. I’ve asked Trine if she’d help 
me with an interview, and she kindly agreed. First, I just need to ask if it’s okay that I record you. 
Trine Borre: Yes, go ahead. 
Troels Toft: Trine, would you like to introduce yourself? 
==PAGE:115==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 56 
Trine Borre: Of course. My name is Trine Borre, and I’m a city council member in Roskilde Municipality. I 
sit on the Planning and Technical Committee and the Climate and Environment Committee. I’m also self-
employed and based in Roskilde. 
Troels Toft: Thanks. What’s your professional background? 
Trine Borre: I’m trained in banking and have spent many years driving around and valuing properties in 
Roskilde and neighboring municipalities like Lejre. I also sit on the Green Tripartite Committee and get some 
input from there, but I don’t have a green professional background as such. 
Troels Toft: Shall we get started? 
Trine Borre: Let’s do it. 
Troels Toft: Should I choose one, or would you like to pick? 
Trine Borre: What’s really close to my heart is the population’s interest—or lack thereof—in the green 
transition, especially when it affects their own backyard. 
Troels Toft: Yes, let’s go with that challenge: lack of public acceptance. 
Trine Borre: Yes. 
Troels Toft: We could also touch on partnerships. 
Trine Borre: Sure. 
Troels Toft: This falls within the context of soft governance—not contractual partnerships, but more informal 
ones. I gave this one a rating of 3 because I found examples of municipalities that had used partnerships to 
create dual-purpose land use, which helped mitigate public opposition. Do you have thoughts on this? 
Trine Borre: My own experience is that it's really helpful when a municipality has a clear goal—what we 
want to achieve—rather than just opening up to every entrepreneur who wants to place a wind turbine or 
something green nearby. That gives me a foundation when talking to residents: “This has been decided. Now 
we need to figure out how to make it beneficial for you, too.” That has really calmed people down. They say, 
“We get it. The green transition is coming, and we’re part of it—but we’d prefer a walking path instead of a 
fence, or a larger windbreak, or a playground.” That’s a great way to start a dialogue. Of course, it’s a bit risky. 
In Roskilde, we had a project that faced heavy opposition, and I’m curious to see if that sets a precedent for 
the next one. People might now think, “If we oppose it, it won’t happen.” So as politicians, we need to be firm 
and united—also across party lines. 
Troels Toft: Yes, and one thing I’ve written about—and you just touched on it with the idea of playgrounds—
is compensation for nearby residents. The underlying fear is often that their property value will decrease. So 
maybe they could be compensated, for instance, through co-ownership of a large solar park. What are your 
thoughts on that? 
Trine Borre: Personally, I’ve never understood the argument that something becomes less ugly if you make 
money off it. But if it works, fine. It wouldn’t change my mind—if I think it’s ugly, it’s still ugly, even if I 
earn from it. What we’ve learned in Roskilde is that early dialogue is crucial, so people don’t feel a decision 
was made behind their backs. Some developers, especially those used to working in Jutland, don’t understand 
==PAGE:116==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 57 
that here in more densely populated areas, dialogue actually has to mean something. You can’t just invite 
people to a random field and call it stakeholder engagement. 
Troels Toft: Yes, the idea that something is ugly near your home is a double-edged sword. It’s not made 
prettier by money, but people might still worry about property values. 
Trine Borre: I actually don’t believe in that argument so much. I think values tend to stabilize. The solar parks 
we’re building are being covered with greenery. In a way, it’s the opposite—we’re creating nature that 
residents can enjoy, with shelters and trails where there used to be just farmland. I think we’ve gotten used to 
calling a plowed field “nature”—but it’s not. 
Troels Toft: And you speak from experience, having valued properties. 
Trine Borre: Yes, and it’s also about dispelling myths. Ten to fifteen years ago, people feared wind turbines 
caused cancer through radiation. It’s important to debunk those myths. If there’s real harm, like a health risk, 
then yes, it affects property value. But if it’s just a rumor spread by opponents, we need to clarify that. 
Troels Toft: That actually inspires me. What you’re describing sounds like an inverted pyramid scheme: 
people assume something is worthless just because they think the next buyer will think so, too. Even if they 
don’t personally mind it, they believe the next person will—and so on. 
Trine Borre: Yes, and that’s why in Roskilde we’ve decided that land used for solar parks should remain 
designated as nature afterwards. So once the panels are gone, your property might be next to a nature reserve—
and suddenly worth a lot more. 
Troels Toft: That’s clever. 
Trine Borre: Long-term, of course. I know most people don’t think 30 years ahead. But I think the fact that 
we, as a municipality, demand nature, biodiversity, and pesticide-free zones makes it more palatable. It’s not 
just some industrial zone with leftover parking lots and abandoned panels. That’s the fear narrative, and we’re 
actively avoiding it. 
Troels Toft: That’s a great point. I didn’t have that idea in my list of findings—that nature can be left better 
than before. I’ll definitely include that. 
Trine Borre: And if you live in the countryside surrounded by flat fields, adding a bit of forest actually 
improves quality of life. 
Troels Toft: Let’s stay on this theme of public acceptance and jump to the topic of ownership in district heating 
companies. Any thoughts? 
Trine Borre: It really annoys me that we don’t utilize opportunities—for example, Microsoft is in Gadstrup, 
and we don’t use their excess heat. 
Troels Toft: Yes. 
Trine Borre: That’s frustrating. Also, people always ask: “Why don’t you put solar panels on municipal roofs 
instead of ruining open landscapes?” 
Troels Toft: Yes. 
==PAGE:117==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 58 
Trine Borre: I’d love more clarity on that. For instance, maybe the economics only work at scale. Or maybe 
the law should be changed so municipalities can more easily install rooftop solar. Right now, you only do it to 
meet building code requirements—like buying indulgences. That’s a missed opportunity. 
Troels Toft: So when it comes to district heating, it’s hard to find specific applications that motivate public 
acceptance—because you quickly move into technical areas. 
Trine Borre: Maybe a large shared heat pump for a neighborhood? 
Troels Toft: Yes, and the communication could highlight that it helps manage resource fluctuations. But again, 
it becomes more of a messaging issue than a real tool for public acceptance. 
Trine Borre: And companies would rather sell 20 small heat pumps than one big one for a whole street. 
Troels Toft: Exactly, and that complicates things. 
Trine Borre: Then it would probably take some overarching financial incentives. For example, from 
Parliament: subsidies if more than ten households joined forces to install a shared system. Carrots like that 
often help steer us in the right direction—if we can either earn or save money. 
Troels Toft: Yes, and then you could communicate that district heating prices have dropped by a few øre 
because it’s powered by free surplus electricity via a heat pump, for example. 
Trine Borre: Or make district heating connection free through a subsidy—like we’ve had for home 
improvement deductions. If we as a nation are serious about this, and since new funding always seems to pop 
up, then we could prioritize this. 
Troels Toft: Great. You’re not even close to setting the record for going off-topic—that goes to me and Jeppe 
Trolle. We ended up discussing Einstein’s theory of relativity and quantum mechanics. You’re safe! 
Trine Borre: I think the “planning for own technology” category really hits a common tension—between 
political ambitions and administrative feasibility. Politicians have ideas and goals, and civil servants try to 
make those workable—sometimes knowing full well the goals are unrealistic. Administrations aren’t really 
allowed to say, “This won’t work.” They’re just expected to solve it. And on a topic like this, where few people 
actually understand the issues, but everyone has an opinion—some want nuclear, some want wind, others 
solar—it becomes hard to navigate. I’d prefer a more professionally grounded approach, where civil servants 
say, “We recommend A, B, and C.” 
Troels Toft: That’s actually what this method tries to enable: a structured, evidence-based overview so 
everyone—not just experts—can participate in the knowledge base and then work backward to see how 
conclusions were reached. 
Trine Borre: Exactly. 
Troels Toft: From there, you could conduct a risk assessment and present that to the council. 
Trine Borre: That’s important, but it requires a mindset shift in the public sector. Politicians should still push 
and challenge, but they also need to accept professional counterarguments and make informed decisions. Right 
now, the discussion happens in the Climate and Environment Committee, but once it reaches the city council, 
we’re starting over. And suddenly people are against anything related to climate—or spouting personal 
==PAGE:118==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 59 
opinions with zero expertise. Climate is one of those fields where everyone considers themselves an expert, 
and that’s risky. 
Troels Toft: Yeah. 
Trine Borre: I actually think Roskilde has a competent administration in this area. I’m not criticizing them. 
But we rarely get to hear their actual professional opinion unless we ask very targeted questions—and that’s 
hard to know in advance. What should I be asking? 
Troels Toft: That’s also what this tool can help with. 
Trine Borre: Yes, but it requires trust in the administration, which isn’t always there. 
Troels Toft: Thanks for that input. Now let’s talk about the tool “planning for own technology.” 
Trine Borre: What does that mean? 
Troels Toft: For example, positive or negative zoning of land for solar—saying in advance where it can or 
can’t go, or placing a Cleantech park opposite Risø. Things like that. 
Trine Borre: Why did you only give that a 2? 
Troels Toft: Because—tell me why you’d rate it higher, and I’ll explain. 
Trine Borre: I think zoning narrows the scope in a useful way. You avoid 50 speculative proposals and instead 
get maybe 5 to 10 serious ones. When the city council designates certain areas, residents also understand that 
it was a political decision. That helps shift the conversation toward possibilities. I often tell people, “This has 
been decided. Now let’s make it work for you.” That’s a better starting point than debating whether it’ll happen 
at all. 
Troels Toft: Yes. 
Trine Borre: It’s been a huge help in my conversations with residents. That said, I don’t necessarily agree 
with all designations. For example, in Roskilde we have something called “cultural landscapes.” Just because 
someone once had a small field there, we can’t put solar panels—that’s strange to me. 
Troels Toft: I gave it a lower score based on a report from Aalborg University called From Opposition to 
Momentum. They mapped out all land use wishes in Denmark and found that if you add them all together, they 
require about 130–140% of the country’s total area. So even with zoning, you can’t avoid conflicts entirely. 
Trine Borre: But for me, disagreement isn’t a problem. That’s part of living with more than two people on 
the island of Venø. 
Troels Toft: That’s why I said it moderately motivates acceptance—so I gave it two checkmarks, which is 
actually relatively high. 
Trine Borre: In my view, this area needs a grown-up in the room—and that grown-up should be Parliament. 
There will always be opponents, but someone needs to say, “This is the way it is.” We still need democracy 
and governance, but not everyone will be satisfied—whether in the council, Parliament, or among citizens. We 
aim for consensus. Of course, putting solar panels in a field affects five families who used to have a view—
==PAGE:119==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 60 
but that’s fewer people than if you put them in Copenhagen, where many already live with noise and 
congestion. 
Troels Toft: That raises the question of why we haven’t tightened building codes—there are plenty of tall 
buildings where you could install solar on façades or above parking lots. 
Trine Borre: Exactly. I think we, as politicians, fail to demand enough of our construction sector—which is 
part of the problem, too. 
Troels Toft: That’s in the regulatory toolbox, which I haven’t included because it would expand the scope too 
much. But thanks for the input. 
Troels Toft: That brings me to something that surprised me. I thought that through the green procurement 
program, Roskilde Municipality had a lot of influence on what it purchases. In some areas, that program is 
highly motivating in relation to certain challenges. But when it comes to solar parks, I’ve struggled to find 
ways in which that purchasing power could be used to address specific challenges—unless I start crossing into 
other tools I’ve already evaluated. Do you see any ways that procurement could help? 
Trine Borre: Well, I guess the municipality could have a political stance, like only purchasing green 
electricity. For example, my daughter just moved into a new apartment and needed to choose an electricity 
provider. We picked one that offered green electricity. 
Troels Toft: I’ve thought about that too—but then the question becomes: does that motivate any of the local 
challenges we’ve identified? 
Trine Borre: It just increases demand for green power. 
Troels Toft: Right, but does it really address public acceptance, biodiversity, or landscape concerns? 
Trine Borre: No, not really. But it does position the municipality as a leader in the green transition. 
Troels Toft: Yes, like with our procurement policy against buying caged or turbo-chickens—it doesn’t 
revolutionize poultry farming, but it sets a direction. 
Trine Borre: Exactly. It makes it easier for me to go out and speak about it publicly. It’s symbolic leadership, 
even if it doesn’t directly solve local solar park issues. 
Troels Toft: So it doesn’t directly motivate the relevant challenges. 
Trine Borre: No, unless the municipality commits to purchasing electricity directly from a specific solar park, 
which might make that project more viable for the developer. But I don’t know if that would be enough. 
Troels Toft: Probably not, because even if the municipality uses the electricity, the power still has to be 
transmitted. That challenge remains. 
Trine Borre: The solar developers I’ve spoken to say this region is attractive because we have good grid 
capacity. That’s also clear from the grid maps—it’s in South Zealand where you’d need to invest in grid 
development. Naturally, they prefer to build where costs are low and returns are high. But maybe we need to 
plan projects so electricity travels shorter distances. 
==PAGE:120==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 61 
Troels Toft: That’s a good point. This project is built for a generic municipality, but ideally, the model would 
be adapted to fit each municipality. Then you could attach the tool to specific projects, and politicians could 
dig into the background without having to rely solely on a few people—or the administration—knowing the 
deeper issues. 
Trine Borre: Yes. 
Troels Toft: That would expose the challenges clearly so the decision-makers are fully informed. Now let’s 
move on to the tool evaluation itself. Did you think the number of steps in the model was appropriate? 
Trine Borre: I don’t think it needs more or fewer steps. It depends on how close to the actual decision-making 
this tool will be applied. If it’s part of a process, five steps are fine. You get many possible combinations—
maybe too many if you’re comparing 10 different projects and trying to weigh their pros and cons. But as a 
guide to decision-making, it’s helpful. With only three steps, everything would land in the middle. So I think 
five is good. 
Troels Toft: And finally: What do you think about the method as a whole? Is it something you’d find useful? 
Trine Borre: The biggest challenge I see is that it’s very subjective. An administration could easily shape the 
outcome based on their own preferences—without me as a politician necessarily noticing. 
Troels Toft: But with this model, they’d have to commit their assessment to paper. And if that turns out to be 
wrong, they’ll be held accountable later. That might make them more cautious and transparent. 
Trine Borre: Yes, and there's no doubt that making decisions with less emotional bias is a good thing. 
Troels Toft: Or at least putting a foundation under those emotions—then you can layer your values on top. 
Trine Borre: Exactly. We talked earlier about kindergarten closures—if we had a tool like this for that, we 
could say: “We’re closing these four kindergartens because of these specific, reasoned factors,” instead of 
making decisions based on gut feelings or personal preferences. 
Troels Toft: And it would show that no solution solves the issue 100%, but that some handle certain challenges 
better than others. And that’s important for political accountability. 
Trine Borre: Yes, and it also gives the administration a level of responsibility—which I think is appropriate. 
Troels Toft: Great—thank you so much, Trine. That was exactly one hour. Perfect timing. 
Trine Borre: Then I’ll just say goodbye. 
Troels Toft: Just say it. 
Trine Borre: See you in Struer. Bye!
