Interview_transcript_Trine Engel 
 
Troels Toft: Hi, my name is Troels and I'm working on my final thesis. I've been fortunate enough to have 
Trine help me with an interview. First, I’d like to ask you if it’s okay that I record the interview. 
Trine Engel: Yes, that’s fine. 
Troels Toft: Could you introduce yourself, Trine? 
Trine Engel: Hi, my name is Trine Engel. I'm a former colleague of Troels, and I was lucky enough to 
complete the same degree many years ago that Troels is now pursuing. But I’m probably being interviewed 
more in my capacity as an employee in a municipally owned district heating company, and as a board member 
in another municipally owned district heating company, as well as my previous experience working in 
environment and technical administration in a municipality. That’s it, thanks. 
Troels Toft: Fantastic. Let's jump into it. I’ve placed this tool evaluation sheet in front of Trine, and we’ll pick 
one of the items. We’re basically assessing how well a tool addresses a given challenge. Let’s start with 
Partnerships – soft governance partnerships. How well can they help motivate the challenge of Resource 
Levitation, which here is defined as Grid Challenges – essentially if we imagine someone could even out the 
power flow from a solar park. I rated it a 2. Do you have any immediate thoughts on where you’d place it? 
Trine Engel: Yes, when I read "Grid Challenge," my poor engineer brain immediately thinks of the difficulty 
of connecting to the grid and having enough capacity in the grid to transport the energy where it’s needed. 
That largely depends on the utility company’s ability to build the necessary infrastructure. But it’s also about 
the utility company having a good dialogue with the municipality regarding where these solar parks are planned 
in time to allow for grid development. And the municipality must also be willing to allocate land. These 
installations take up space – transformers, for example. 
Troels Toft: Interesting. 
Trine Engel: That mutual understanding is important – that there’s ongoing dialogue. It shouldn’t be a surprise 
from either side like, “Oops, you can’t connect your solar park here – there’s no grid capacity,” or “Oops, we 
want a grid connection here, but you haven’t allocated land for it.” Maybe only for production facilities and 
not for transformer stations, or maybe you’ve even designated a negative zoning area where transformers aren’t 
allowed. That’s why I believe it’s a really good idea to enter partnerships and start the dialogue long before a 
solar park is built. 
Troels Toft: That’s actually quite interesting. So we’re talking about partnerships with the grid company itself. 
And the challenge with the grid connection – what exactly is the problem? 
Trine Engel: It’s a huge issue. If you want to build a solar park tomorrow, you might have to wait three years. 
Troels Toft: So is the challenge ultimately about lacking capacity? 
Trine Engel: It’s about capacity, yes, but the grid companies develop capacity continuously. The sooner 
they’re told about future needs in an area, the easier it is to plan. For example, if a municipality sent its solar 
zoning plan to the grid company and engaged in dialogue, the grid company could already be aware of the 
expected connections and respond, either by saying “that’s completely impossible – it’ll take five years” or 
suggest alternative sites, or warn you that it will take a long time and you’ll need space for three transformer 
stations. 
==PAGE:123==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 64 
Troels Toft: I was thinking about how partnerships can facilitate arrangements – like with soft governance – 
that allow for multifunctional land use. In some municipalities, like Assens, they’ve managed to combine solar 
panels with other uses – for example, raising the panels slightly so you can graze animals underneath and 
improve biodiversity. That helps resolve land-use conflicts. 
Trine Engel: Yes, I absolutely believe in the importance of dialogue when it comes to multifunctional land 
use. I recently saw a news segment with a biologist from Guldborgsund Municipality who was going door-to-
door with farmers, sharing the municipality’s ideas and drawing on maps to reach mutual agreements. That’s 
the kind of dialogue I believe in. But it must be followed up with actual authority measures – regulations and 
guidelines. For example, you could approve a solar installation on the condition that the land is used 
multifunctionally, or that it helps protect groundwater. There must be trust that technical installations and 
protection goals can coexist. 
Troels Toft: That’s a great point. So, you can get part of the way with partnerships, but you also need a 
regulatory framework in place. 
Trine Engel: I see solar parks as something that should be considered in the very early idea stages of a project 
– in classic project models like PRINCE2. Early on, you can bring many different stakeholders together: 
farmers or their associations (who own the land), water utilities (who want to protect groundwater), developers 
(who want to make a profit), the electricity company (who ensures grid access), and the municipality (as the 
authority). That’s where dialogue is really useful – it helps narrow things down. Later, as the project becomes 
more specific, then you need legislation, local plans, land reservations, and legal frameworks to say yes here, 
no there. 
Troels Toft: Right, and when it comes to the grid challenge – the municipality doesn’t even own the grid. So 
you’re still dependent on external players to collaborate. 
Trine Engel: Depends on the municipality. Some municipalities still own and operate their electricity grid – 
though they’re few. That might be worth looking into: ask those municipalities whether they feel they have 
more control. For instance, Slagelse and Korsør still own their grids. 
Troels Toft: Oh, interesting! 
Trine Engel: There are a few in Jutland too. But you’re right – most have sold off their grid companies. 
Næstved just sold theirs to SEAS-NVE (Sirius). 
Troels Toft: Yeah, makes sense. 
Trine Engel: So the general picture is that municipalities in Denmark usually don’t own their grids. 
Troels Toft: Great. Thanks for that input. Let’s move on in a slightly more fun direction. I’d be remiss not to 
bring up “Hiding Companies” – where the municipality uses its ownership of a district heating company as a 
tool. Is there a particular challenge where you think that applies best? 
Trine Engel: I’m curious why you grayed out “Alternative Hubs”? Why is that one excluded? 
Troels Toft: It’s just methodological. The project has a word count limit, so I’ve only included a selection of 
tools and challenges to keep things manageable. 
Trine Engel: So you kept the two middle ones – partnerships and public awareness campaigns – as your 
representative examples? 
==PAGE:124==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 65 
Troels Toft: Yes, and I also wanted to test how the method handles tools that don’t work well. It’s important 
to include those too. 
Trine Engel: Fair enough. 
Troels Toft: Looking at the “Limited Public Acceptance” challenge, how do you think municipal ownership 
of a district heating company could help? 
Trine Engel: That one’s tricky, but you could imagine a model where the local district heating company is a 
co-investor or sole owner of a solar park. There are businesses doing this – they build solar installations and 
use a Power Purchase Agreement (PPA) to buy electricity from themselves. They might also have an electric 
boiler or battery to use or store the electricity onsite. 
Troels Toft: Right, right next to each other? 
Trine Engel: Exactly. And if the district heating company already has a good reputation in the community – 
which is not always the case – it could serve as a lever. In such a case, the public might be more accepting if 
the solar installation was owned by their heating company, and the electricity was used to produce heat for 
their radiators. Then it’s not some developer getting rich while they have to look at something ugly – instead, 
it’s “ours,” part of our local co-op or municipal company. 
Troels Toft: Got it. 
Trine Engel: But if your heating company is already unpopular – I won’t name names, but take Odsherred 
District Heating, for example – then it's a really bad idea. You’re combining something unpopular with 
something else that’s controversial. 
Troels Toft: Yeah. 
Trine Engel: Unless it’s a really good business case that clearly reduces heating costs. Then you need to 
communicate that: “Yes, this might be visually unappealing, but it saves you this much money per year.” Either 
that, or foster a sense of ownership – “this is ours.” 
Troels Toft: So it’s about creating a sense of ownership? 
Trine Engel: Yes, exactly. People are willing to accept much more if it benefits them directly or indirectly. 
Troels Toft: That’s a really interesting insight. 
Trine Engel: But you have to build on a story that’s already positive. You can’t just invent a positive narrative 
out of thin air. Some local district heating companies already have that – especially the cooperative ones. It 
doesn’t even have to be fully municipal. The municipality is often a co-owner in cooperative companies but 
usually with a smaller share than in municipal companies. Yet they still typically have a seat on the board, 
even if it’s just as an observer. 
Troels Toft: So in a way, it becomes a kind of small-scale sector coupling. 
Trine Engel: Yes, exactly – a sector coupling case. 
Troels Toft: That kind of thing can build a positive sentiment. And there are a lot of small communities or co-
housing projects that do exactly that – they install all sorts of stuff themselves and don’t mind looking at wind 
==PAGE:125==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 66 
turbines or solar panels, because it’s their project. They tolerate the noise and the visual impact because it 
benefits them. A lot of the opposition to technical installations comes from the perception that the benefits go 
to others. 
Troels Toft: But here’s what I’m struggling with: if it’s such a good business case – say, a solar park combined 
with district heating – why doesn’t the market figure it out by itself? Why don’t heating companies naturally 
partner with solar developers? 
Trine Engel: First of all, it comes down to resource limitations. If you’re asking why a local district heating 
company hasn’t already developed a solar park, it’s because they lack the money and the expertise. 
Troels Toft: But why don’t solar developers approach them? 
Trine Engel: Because developers can more easily find capital elsewhere. 
Troels Toft: But then it’s not a truly optimal business case, is it? 
Trine Engel: Well – that’s exactly the point. The green transition cannot be left to the market alone. Period. 
If we want a green transition, it’s going to be more expensive and more complicated. It would be much easier 
to just not sort waste, for example – thousands of trucks, new containers, production of bins – that’s all very 
costly. But we do it because we believe in circularity and sustainability. The green transition doesn't come with 
a discount – it costs money. And I believe it’s the responsibility of municipalities and lawmakers to ensure that 
any profits generated are directed toward where they're most needed. Otherwise, it just collapses into chaos. 
Troels Toft: Right, so basically we’re forcing the district heating company to deviate from what’s 
economically optimal by coupling with solar – and that creates a shortfall. Unless you charge customers more 
for their heat, someone has to absorb that cost. 
Trine Engel: Exactly. Either the cost lands on the consumers, or you provide subsidies. Alternatively, you 
might recover some of the costs through multifunctionality. If you have to buy land for groundwater protection, 
wetland restoration, biodiversity – and solar parks – paying farmers for all of that separately is insanely 
expensive. But if you manage to combine the goals, you don’t have to pay for as much land. That creates added 
value – a bigger pie – and maybe that makes it viable. I don’t know. I’m not an agricultural economist. But 
one can hope. 
Troels Toft: That makes sense. There were times when solar parks were hugely profitable. But right now, 
returns are falling below expectations, and few dare invest in them – whether you’re a speculator, a 
municipality, or a district heating company. 
Trine Engel: Exactly. 
Troels Toft: So maybe it's a question of learning effects – something that used to be a good business case now 
isn’t, or vice versa. And if electricity prices spike again, that could make heat storage capacity more valuable 
– and then it is a good business case. 
Trine Engel: Exactly! 
Troels Toft: While I have you here – someone who knows about this – how much flexibility is there really in 
the temperature range of district heating networks? Couldn’t you fluctuate it a bit more to accommodate 
variations? 
==PAGE:126==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 67 
Trine Engel: That depends on the size and technical setup of your network. But here’s a simple comparison: 
if the power goes out, you notice immediately. If heat production drops, it takes hours before people feel it. 
That tells you a lot about flexibility. The average customer doesn’t notice production stops right away. 
Troels Toft: Right. 
Trine Engel: There’s a built-in flexibility – it can take hours before it gets cold. And even then, it’s only the 
houses at the end of the pipe that feel it first. That’s why so many install electric boilers – because it allows 
them to react to electricity market prices in real time, without seriously impacting heat production. But for that 
to work, you also need large heat storage tanks – giant thermos flasks. The real limitation is the lack of technical 
competence in small district heating companies. 
Trine Engel (continued): So the best thing a municipality can do is to consolidate – either by merging 
companies or by forming very close partnerships – because small companies don’t have the muscles to 
implement new technologies and reap the benefits. The market can’t fix that on its own – the competencies 
just don’t exist. And that’s a political question too – municipalities often don’t want to give up seats on their 
boards, even if consolidation would create more efficient operations. 
Troels Toft: Yes. 
Trine Engel: Or put another way – I used to run a small secretariat for a joint venture between four 
municipalities that managed a little stormwater basin. Each municipality owned part of it, and they wanted to 
hold board meetings and run local projects – but it would’ve been much more efficient to just hand it over to 
one municipality. That same dynamic plays out in district heating: people want to feel in control and make 
decisions, even if it’s not the most rational setup. 
Troels Toft: And if you said that flexibility also comes from adjusting production in the heating plants — like 
just putting less biomass in the boiler — is there a limit to how much you can regulate up and down? 
Trine Engel: The district heating system is dimensioned based on criteria for what it needs to deliver — for 
example, during a cold winter. So you never go below a level where it can meet that demand. The issue with 
solar in relation to district heating is that the problem isn’t in the summer — quite the opposite. In summer, 
there’s way too much heat, especially from waste incineration plants. So solar isn’t a very interesting business 
case for district heating companies, at least not in that season. 
Troels Toft: So maybe water utilities could use it more — for pumps or electricity? 
Trine Engel: Yes, possibly. Sector coupling always has potential value. But for district heating companies, 
solar isn’t going to solve the big problems. That’s not what I was referring to when I mentioned using the 
district heating companies — it’s more about where municipalities can exercise influence, directly or 
indirectly. Whether that helps with the actual challenges of district heating is another matter. I’m not sure it 
does. 
Troels Toft: Got it. That makes sense. 
Trine Engel: Some municipalities have even created their own solar companies — like a municipal solar 
company (A/S) — and become the owner of a solar park themselves. 
Troels Toft: Okay. 
==PAGE:127==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 68 
Trine Engel: I know that Albertslund Municipality has attempted that, though I don’t know if it’s strictly 
legal. But it’s an example of taking control: they can build it on their own land or rooftops, and they own both 
the income and the cost. No need to negotiate with anyone else. They just do it. 
Troels Toft: Thanks so much for all these insights. I think we’ll park the analysis grid for now — we’ve gone 
through two cases, but touched on a lot more. Instead, I’d like to ask about the challenges I’ve identified, 
especially the “shadow challenges” and “technical barriers.” Do you think I’ve chosen the right ones, or are 
there any I’ve missed? 
Trine Engel: I’m surprised you haven’t included the issue of sustainability of materials. Maybe you covered 
it under something like “Wizard Limitless,” but when you build a solar park, you need equipment that’s often 
produced in China, using poor labor conditions. How do municipalities handle that — both internally and in 
their communication with citizens — while still trying to maintain a green and ethical image? That could really 
affect public sentiment. It might be worth mentioning in your exam, even if you don’t have time to go deep in 
the project itself. 
Troels Toft: That’s a really good point. I actually had a different... 
Trine Engel: Even if you avoid lifecycle analyses (LCA) and such, we can’t ignore that this is a fact. 
Troels Toft: Yeah, you’re absolutely right. It becomes politically sensitive too. Because if people dig into it 
and find out these panels were made with forced labor or under poor environmental conditions, it could trigger 
a backlash. 
Trine Engel: Exactly. You’ve highlighted visual opposition — but there can also be political opposition. 
Think about how quickly public opinion changed when natural gas became synonymous with being “in Putin’s 
pocket,” even though we also import gas from Norway. So if a technology gets tied up in a geopolitical 
narrative — whether in support or opposition — that affects its legitimacy. 
Troels Toft: That’s a very good point. I’ve heard that before. I’ve just been unsure where to categorize it — 
should it be a separate challenge or part of “visual impact,” or even a broader question of whether the 
technology is truly sustainable? 
Trine Engel: Yes, and you could argue that sometimes, solar panels never earn back their carbon footprint — 
especially when paired with batteries. You can get a good Scope 2 business case, but never look at Scope 3. 
And Scope 3 emissions are often where most of the impact lies. In Denmark, Scope 1 and 2 might account for 
8% of emissions. Scope 3 is the other 92%. 
Troels Toft: Exactly — that’s what makes your point so relevant. If we ignore Scope 3, we can convince 
ourselves we’re doing the right thing, while hiding a lot of the real impact. 
Trine Engel: It’s not just about emissions. It’s also about which regimes you’re supporting with your money. 
Troels Toft: Yes. 
Trine Engel: I recently drove to Hamburg, and it’s funny — in Denmark, we debate whether solar is profitable, 
whether it’s ugly, whether it takes up too much farmland. But the moment you cross the border into Germany, 
it’s completely different. The landscape is covered in solar panels on both sides of the highway for miles. 
Troels Toft: Yeah. 
==PAGE:128==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 69 
Trine Engel: So the question is: what did the Germans do differently? Maybe they just succeeded in building 
a different kind of public sentiment — a sense of urgency. Like, “We’re an industrial country. We need 
electricity to sustain our GDP.” Not this romantic notion of wheat fields. Which aren’t great for biodiversity, 
by the way. High-voltage lines are also ugly. Years ago, someone proposed burying them, but it was way too 
expensive. So in Denmark, we often have this romantic ideal — grandma’s garden and wheat in the 
background. We need to change that mindset. 
Troels Toft: I think the Germans also wouldn’t start building solar until they had the grid capacity ready. 
They’re more aware of causality — or the full value chain, you might say. 
Trine Engel: I think they’ve been clearer: “We will make this happen.” Plus, they’ve used a lot of subsidy 
programs. 
Troels Toft: And the professional level of the debate is often higher. In Germany, you have to support your 
argument with facts. In Denmark, you can just talk. 
Trine Engel: But in Denmark, you also have to appeal to emotions. Back to the solar roof — do you feel like 
it creates value? Whether it actually does is less important than whether people feel that it does — for them, 
for the local community, for Denmark. 
Troels Toft: In politics, emotions rule. Especially negative ones. You can create arenas to fight over them. 
What doesn’t work in politics are boring things — like underground cabling. No one wants to discuss that. 
Trine Engel: You can’t see it. 
Troels Toft: Exactly. So politicians would rather talk about building something new — “X megawatts of solar 
capacity” — than the invisible things that matter. Like actual kilowatt-hours or grid investments. 
Trine Engel: I mean, they put up weird screens on bike paths just to count how many bikes go by. Does that 
really add value for Roskilde Municipality? Or is it just so we can say, “Look, we’re a bike-friendly city,” and 
the cyclist passing by can wave at the counter and feel seen? But with solar parks, you need to create a narrative. 
You need to tell a story that makes people feel good about it. And I believe that’s a municipal responsibility 
— to help create that positive story. 
Troels Toft: Yeah. 
Trine Engel: And you don’t do that by using negative zoning designations. 
Troels Toft: Right. Let’s move on. So now we’re looking at the municipal tools. That’s actually easier, because 
it’s a defined set. So here’s the question: are there any tools you feel are missing? 
Trine Engel: I think you're missing: lobbying for national legislation. 
Troels Toft: Lobbying for national legislation, yeah. That’s actually sort of included under “Partnerships” in 
my model — but only because I defined partnerships broadly, to include soft governance partnerships with 
higher levels of government. 
Trine Engel: Well, I’d argue lobbying deserves its own place. There are inter-municipal partnerships that I 
think are very important, especially if you’re a small municipality. But also — I’m not a politician, but there 
are areas where municipalities have a better sense of what’s needed than national legislators do. Mayors speak 
==PAGE:129==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 70 
out all the time in the media about immigration, for example. When it comes to energy, they mostly just ask 
for more funding — but they could also highlight other barriers, where only national law can make a difference. 
Troels Toft: I actually thought I had that covered under “State Government Partnerships.” 
Trine Engel: You placed that under partnerships. 
Troels Toft: Yes, it was added a bit later. I think you're right, though. 
Trine Engel: And I see it as part of the municipality’s responsibility. Yes, you’re an authority enforcing 
existing laws, but you also need to monitor whether those laws make sense. If the law is outdated or impractical, 
it’s your job to report that up the chain. 
Troels Toft: Yeah, that could be seen as feedback to lawmakers. That's a good point. Thanks for that. Then 
there's this scoring system... 
Trine Engel: I noticed you haven’t included anything about media — but I assume you group that under 
“Public Awareness Campaigns”? 
Troels Toft: Yeah, honestly I feel like I almost have too much of that. I’ve got a citizens' panel, awareness 
campaigns… they all blend a bit together. 
Trine Engel: Okay. 
Troels Toft: So I just decided to include one of them. Awareness campaigns include things like those bike 
counters and general storytelling tools. 
Trine Engel: Got it. 
Troels Toft: Then we’ve got the five-level motivation scale — how well each tool motivates each challenge. 
I think the five steps work well. I tried to spread it across the full range. 
Trine Engel: I think it’s great that you included both ends — even if hardly anything scores a “4” (very high), 
at least you’ve included the possibility. And you’ve used “0” quite well — to indicate where a tool is irrelevant. 
That’s good. 
You’re essentially capturing that most tools fall somewhere in the middle. But when you move from a generic 
to a specific case — a specific municipality, a specific solar park — these scores will shift. One tool might 
jump up or drop down by a whole point depending on local conditions. So maybe you can’t get more precise 
than “plus or minus one,” but as a framework, it works well. 
Troels Toft: Yeah, and what I found is that combining tools across phases can increase the total motivation 
level. For a specific project, you might use one tool early on and another later, and together they achieve more 
than either alone. 
Trine Engel: Exactly. And you’d ideally walk through each tool and ask: “Do we even have this tool in our 
municipality?” “Do we use it actively?” That’s how to align the framework with a specific municipal context 
and specific technologies. 
Troels Toft: Some municipalities might not even have the basic tools or capabilities. And others may not have 
relevant technologies — like ocean wave power, if they don’t have a coastline. 
==PAGE:130==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 71 
Trine Engel: Yes. Final question? 
Troels Toft: Trine, do you think this tool could actually be useful in a municipal setting? 
Trine Engel: Yes, I do. I think it could help raise awareness that the toolbox is broader — and more nuanced 
— than many think. There’s a whole palette of tools available, not just the hard regulatory levers. And 
surprisingly many of the soft governance tools can be used — not just to influence citizens, but already in the 
early idea phase — to ensure that projects don’t die due to technical barriers later on. 
Troels Toft: And what if a municipality goes through the framework and discovers that it actually has very 
few tools to motivate a given challenge? What should it do then? 
Trine Engel: Then you need to reflect on how to act within your responsibility area. Municipalities can’t solve 
everything at once, but they do need to go deeper on the green transition and ask: “What would it actually take 
to reach our political goals?” 
And from there, the administration can go back to the politicians and say: “To make this happen, we need these 
competencies and these tools — and right now, we don’t have them. Also, we’re too small to do this alone — 
we need to collaborate with neighboring municipalities.” Even if those neighbors are political rivals. 
Troels Toft: Yeah. I actually intended this tool to function as a kind of precursor to a risk analysis — like a 
way to map out the challenges and understand the landscape before doing a full-on risk matrix. 
Trine Engel: That makes sense. But remember — sometimes politicians don’t want “facts.” They’ve already 
decided what they want to do. So while risk analysis is valuable, the most useful thing you can do is show a 
pathway — a strategy — to get from where you are to where the policy says you need to be. And that includes 
showing which tools are needed along the way. 
Troels Toft: Yeah. Although, one thing that can influence politicians is risk language. If you tell them 
something is “high-risk” and you’ve tried everything — and it’s still red — that’s a heavy burden for them to 
accept. Most will shy away from endorsing a project marked with a red flag. 
Trine Engel: True — but then you’re back to the question: do you want to drive the transition with fear, or 
with a positive narrative? Yes, the facts matter — but they must be packaged in a story of what’s possible. A 
message that shows where value can be created. I don’t believe a magical free market will solve this — and 
that’s why partnerships are so valuable. They help build the room, the expertise, the momentum for action. 
But the municipality has to enter that room, and that requires time, skill, and decisiveness. 
Troels Toft: I like holding the market up as a mirror sometimes — not because I believe in it blindly, but 
because it’s a good thought experiment. You get clarity by asking, “Why doesn’t this solve itself?” 
Trine Engel: I know you’re provoking me with that — but that’s fine. 
Troels Toft: Yeah, you’re sharp enough to handle it! 
Trine Engel: But yes — municipalities and politics are often obstacles in themselves. Your model assumes a 
perfect, aligned municipality — where everyone pulls in the same direction. That’s rarely the case. You might 
have competing departments, conflicting interests, and irrational decisions based on short-term politics. 
Troels Toft: Yeah. 
==PAGE:131==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 72 
Trine Engel: And that’s something you might want to include in your thesis. There’s no such thing as a 
“rational municipality.” Sometimes, departments work against each other. The planning department might not 
talk to the water utility. Or one part wants to sell land that another wants to preserve. 
So this framework is useful for raising awareness. It helps move a municipality from unconscious to conscious 
incompetence. And that’s the first step. But it also shows how important it is for the municipal council to set a 
clear political direction — and accept the costs, conflicts, or compromises that come with it. 
Troels Toft: Yeah. 
Trine Engel: No matter what, someone’s going to be unhappy. The question is: whose voters are they? 
Troels Toft: Yeah. 
Trine Engel: That’s why we also have all these debates about agriculture. Do we protect the sector because 
of GDP? Or because of party politics? The same dynamic plays out locally. So don’t expect pure rationality. 
Just try to structure the facts clearly and give them a pathway to act. 
Troels Toft: I just want to say — thank you so much for a really good interview. You brought up so many 
valuable insights.

Tina Cartey: Yeah, I think that’s something we’ve run into a couple of times—that exact thing. Where I hear,

==PAGE:139==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 80 
Troels Toft: That’s exactly what it does. What you do is you calculate it—the way Randers does—where you 
say: everything has to be reinvested. You have to do that with a nuclear plant. You have to do that with gas, 
coal—all of it. Solar panels too—no matter the technology, you have to reinvest. That’s what we call capex—
that’s what you call reinvestment. Then there’s opex—that’s for maintaining, removing rust, things that break. 
That’s operations. Then you have your fuel. Coal power has coal. With solar, the fuel is the sun—so that’s 
zero kroner. Then you add all of that together. You say yes, it goes up and down—there are reinvestments—
but some things are cheaper and smarter. We can’t just say: now solar panels are free. No, I calculate it all with 
electricity prices compared to others. It’s called LCOE—levelized cost of energy. 
Troels Toft: And that’s the price of energy without subsidies, without support, without taxes—nothing. All 
costs included, spread across all the years and all the kilowatt-hours produced. That gives you a price per 
kilowatt-hour. Then you get a different price per kilowatt-hour for coal, etc. Then you factor in housing, 
location, insulation. Then we run all the numbers and say: if we’re going to live here for 30 years, with 
reinvestments and everything, what would it cost for one tech vs. the other? That’s how you calculate it. So 
when you reach 2030 and have to reinvest in everything, you could choose not to. But then you have to invest 
in coal plants and other tech—which ends up costing more per kilowatt-hour when everything is counted. 
Tina Cartey: I also assume that when I install new solar panels in 2040, the technological development will 
have helped—so maybe the next ones will last 30 years. And climate change might give us two more hours of 
sunshine per day in Denmark. All of those things will help the business case too. 
Troels Toft: That’s exactly the last part. Yes—everything could last longer. It could be cheaper to build. All 
of that ends up in the LCOE. If that gives you, say, 29 dollars per kilowatt-hour for solar, and that’s cheaper 
than coal—then as a politician, you sit there and say, “It costs a lot to establish.” And I go, “Sure—but it’s still 
cheaper than coal. You can go out to the market, choose this, and I promise a purchase price of 29 dollars.” 
Then you, as a politician, say, “But I don’t want that.” Then the administration says, “Okay—that means we’ll 
have to buy it for 35 dollars per kilowatt from gas plants.” And we’ll have to raise electricity prices. Because 
of your decision. But that’s fine—you go explain why you don’t want this. That’s what political resilience 
means. But it’s just to say—you were totally right. And that part of the project is just covered elsewhere. So 
let’s focus on this section here. 
Tina Cartey: Yes, because you could say, I was thinking about economy as a question—that’s what I started 
with. So where I sit on committees, I’m often focused on labor hours. Because one thing is the cost of building 
the solar panels, but what does it cost me as a municipality? Based on, as you said, norms and types—how 
many man-hours, how many full-time equivalents do I need to allocate? So that has to be weighed against the 
mitigation—or whatever you call it. If I’m supposed to be the planning authority, but I have to hire 20 people 
for that, then my profit disappears completely. 
Troels Toft: The economy isn’t included in this one. No, this one only deals with where there’s tech and 
politics. 
Tina Cartey: That’s because—if I were to point out what I thought might be missing, that’s where I’d 
immediately think: where is my economic consideration? But you explain that it’s located elsewhere. 
Troels Toft: That’s because it’s in another section, so you’re completely right. I mean, the man-hours you’re 
spending there—if you, like me, spend man-hours on it… the green transition is a transition—you’re moving 
from one thing to another. If you build a new incineration plant on the exact same site where the old one was, 
of the same type—you won’t use many man-hours. You just build the same thing again. If you build solar 
parks in a place where there weren’t solar parks before—that’s where you use all those man-hours. So then 
you can ask: is it worth it? So you’re investing money. You’re using resources. It could also be any other 
technology. Then you can look at my table and ask: the next time we build a new solar park, I won’t need to 
spend the same number of man-hours. But what if solar parks are more expensive than gas? If gas gets very, 
==PAGE:140==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 81 
very cheap or something, then maybe it becomes hard to compete in the market. Then it might be that many 
right-leaning parties manage to push it out—and all the effort you’ve invested now could be wasted. So it’s 
also about not wasting your resources. Does that make sense? 
Tina Cartey: Yes, because the man-hours will have to be spent again if the legislation changes. 
Troels Toft: If someone decides not to do it because they say: I won’t spend the man-hours. I can see—looking 
ahead—it won’t be worth it. So when we have to install them again, this technology is expensive compared to 
gas. It can’t compete in the market. Then this is a fragile economic technology. But if you took your solar 
panels on the motorway and ran the numbers—how much did you earn per kilowatt-hour, what was the cost 
of building that kind of clean tech initiative in 2030—and if it turns out to be a very expensive way to do it, 
then that could be a fragile path to take. Because in my view, the whole thing would just be torn down again 
after 50 years. And what did you really achieve? Maybe you got some very expensive CO₂ reductions that 
could’ve been achieved more cheaply another way. Maybe. 
Tina Cartey: That’s because you think in silos, and I think in terms of health. That’s the leg that’s our 
argument to get it approved one day—and it will happen. The only question is when. 
Troels Toft: Yes, it’s really good—but it will happen. I’m not saying otherwise—I just don’t know what the 
numbers will say. Maybe they’ll show it’s brilliant. Maybe the business case will be excellent. 
Tina Cartey: The societal economics are excellent in this case. 
Troels Toft: Yes. Thank you for the input. What do we have here? Now we have the evaluation charts. You 
tick the boxes. Do you think this is the right way to evaluate? One, two, three, zero, two, three, four? 
Tina Cartey: I think so. Because as politicians, we’re busy. Like we talked about at the beginning—I’m not 
an expert in this area. I need to get a quick overview. I have to be able to get a quick overview. And I can with 
a model like this—something very simple. Whether it’s colors or checkmarks or whatever. I actually think the 
checkmarks give me what I need—they also make me curious. Why is that one a zero? Why is that one...? 
Troels Toft: Let’s move on to the next one—we’ve already started to move in that direction. What do you 
think about the model as a whole? I mean, do you think it could be useful in a municipal context—as a full 
model? Like if you were running those motorway solar panels, driving the water through them and saying: 
they need to go green here, and then come back with... this schema—I get that the black boxes are there because 
I had 50 pages to write. I can’t go through all of them. Then I’d exceed 50 pages. I had to pick a few. But then 
this would run everything through it—and then you’d see if there are areas down here that score something. 
For example, if there’s no grid in the area, and it’s hard to get the power out. That could be a problem. Or 
maybe it’s something else. Do you think a tool like this could be used in a municipal context? 
Tina Cartey: I definitely think so. Because it gives us that clear overview and helps focus on: what’s possible, 
what’s not? Where are we lacking knowledge? Do we have the competences? Do we need to find something 
externally? Is it going to be too expensive that way? Like Sebastian said earlier—it sparks curiosity. So as a 
politician, I’d immediately dig in and say, “Okay, this item here...” And then this helps clarify what I know 
and don’t know—or what the administration knows or doesn’t know—where we’ll need to buy consultants or 
where we can manage in-house. So yes, I really believe this would be useful to have when sitting in a committee 
situation. Because the thing about this is—we maybe have 5 minutes to go through a project. 10 minutes. 
That’s all we’ve got. If we had 10 hours for each project, we could do it differently. But we sit and have two 
hours to go through 20 to 30 cases. So yes, I definitely think it’s good for forming that overview—so that the 
politicians can decide: what do we need to explore further? What should we shut down because it makes no 
sense? So yes—I believe in it.

Interview with Charlotte von Hessberg 
Dato: 
8. 
august 
2025 
Format: 
Online 
interview 
Transkription: 
Started 
by 
Hvidovre 
Municipality 
Deltagere: 
• 
Troels Toft (interviewer, specialestuderende, DTU, Sustainable Energy) 
• 
Charlotte von Hessberg, varmeplanlægger og klimakonsulent, Hvidovre Kommune 
==PAGE:149==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 90 
 
Troels Toft: Could you start by briefly introducing yourself and your role in Hvidovre Municipality? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: I work as a heat planning officer and climate consultant. I’ve been involved with 
the climate plan here in Hvidovre Municipality and its implementation. 
Troels Toft: My thesis project is called From National Climate Plan to Municipal Climate Plan. The idea is 
that Denmark’s national energy and climate plan contains a range of technologies. Some are handled at the 
national level, but many must be implemented locally. At the municipal level, climate work has shifted from 
something as simple as growing carrots in your backyard to cutting-edge technology with significant risks. My 
project aims to create a risk analysis tool municipalities can use to assess technologies from the national plan—
here tested on solar parks in open land. 
The method identifies typical challenges for the chosen technology, compares them against the municipal tools 
available, and rates how well each tool mitigates each challenge. The result is a table showing where the 
municipality is strong and where gaps exist—gaps that could cause costly setbacks later. 
What’s your immediate impression of applying such a method to municipal planning? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: In principle, a structured approach to problem-solving is important. In 
municipalities, we often get thrown into a case without fully mapping the framework and stakeholders. Having 
a process that forces you to consider challenges systematically could be valuable. 
Troels Toft: One key finding in my test case is that fluctuating energy production from renewables is difficult 
for municipalities to manage—especially without requiring developers to add battery storage to solar parks 
from the start. What’s your view on that? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: We don’t have open-land solar parks in Hvidovre—our available land is either built 
up or protected—so we don’t work much with external developers in that sense. We do, however, work with 
companies on energy communities, often focusing on timing consumption to match production and, where 
possible, adding batteries. 
A good example is when neighbouring businesses coordinate usage: for instance, an electric bus company and 
a bakery with electric delivery trucks can share charging infrastructure, charging buses during lunch breaks 
and trucks at night. These arrangements reduce strain on the grid without requiring major new connections. 
Troels Toft: That’s very much in line with what I’ve found—soft governance, partnerships, and coordination 
can sometimes mitigate technical constraints better than hard regulation. But such partnerships require 
resources, right? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Absolutely. Facilitating partnerships takes staff time and persistence. Businesses 
exist to make money, so cooperation isn’t automatic—especially if potential partners are competitors. You 
need committed people, often at the director level, to make decisions that cost money. The most successful 
examples here in Hvidovre have come from local “champions” who drive collaboration because they also see 
value in branding or sustainability—not just profit. 
Troels Toft: On regulatory hurdles—are there particular examples you’ve had to work with? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: One big one is that large companies can’t currently be part of renewable energy 
communities under Danish implementation of the EU rules. The EU requires inclusion of small and medium-
==PAGE:150==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 91 
sized enterprises, but Denmark chose minimum implementation, excluding large companies. The problem is 
that many growing businesses quickly become “large” under EU definitions—sometimes just because a bigger 
company takes a 25% ownership stake. That disqualifies them from participating, even if they’re otherwise 
ideal partners. 
Troels Toft: That sounds like a clear case where national regulation limits local potential. 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Exactly. We’ve been pushing for broader implementation to allow large companies 
to join, but changing that requires national political will. 
Troels Toft: Another challenge I’ve identified is lack of public acceptance. How has that played out in 
Hvidovre? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Honestly, we don’t face much resistance here—largely because of local ownership 
models. For example, the Hvidovre Wind Turbine Cooperative, founded around 2007–2009, allowed all 
residents to buy shares. Ownership gives people a sense of involvement, which research shows increases 
acceptance. If you benefit directly, even noise from a turbine becomes “the sound of money” rather than an 
annoyance. 
Troels Toft: That’s a strong argument for including ownership models as part of municipal strategies. 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Yes, but ownership models and partnerships are different tools, and you have to 
define them clearly. Both can be effective in building acceptance. 
Troels Toft: On green procurement—my evaluation suggests it doesn’t help much for open-land solar parks, 
since municipalities can’t own or operate them directly. 
Charlotte von Hessberg: That’s correct. Municipalities can’t be electricity producers, even for their own 
consumption, unless they set up a separate company that operates under market conditions. Alternatively, a 
third party can own the panels. But the economics aren’t always attractive, especially given low summer 
electricity prices. 
Troels Toft: In your work, have you found soft or hard tools to be more effective? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Soft tools are often the most effective in practice. Hard regulation sets the 
framework, but cooperation and engagement are what make projects work locally. In Hvidovre, we’ve used 
both, but the breakthroughs usually come from building relationships, finding common ground, and making 
sure people feel part of the process. 
Troels Toft: Have you seen cases where soft tools alone weren’t enough? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Yes—especially where there’s competition for land. If two developers want the 
same site, or if a potential site overlaps with protected areas, you need hard tools like planning restrictions to 
make a clear decision. Dialogue can’t solve that kind of conflict on its own. 
Troels Toft: You mentioned earlier that Hvidovre has no large open-land solar parks. Does that mean the 
municipality’s main renewable energy focus is elsewhere? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Exactly. Our main focus is on rooftop solar, energy efficiency in buildings, and 
integrating smaller-scale renewables into urban areas. We’re also investing in district heating expansion and 
==PAGE:151==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 92 
electrification of transport. Land here is so scarce and valuable that large-scale solar parks just aren’t feasible 
without sacrificing something more important. 
Troels Toft: That’s interesting, because one of the critiques of Denmark’s national climate plan is that it 
assumes municipalities will simply “find the space” for these large installations. 
Charlotte von Hessberg: That’s a good example of the mismatch between national planning and local reality. 
On paper, it looks easy—you draw a box on a map. But when you go out into the real world, you discover that 
every square metre already has a use or a protection status. 
Troels Toft: If you had a tool like the one I’m developing, showing early on that large-scale solar isn’t a 
realistic option for Hvidovre, do you think it could help in discussions with the state? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Definitely. If you can present a clear, evidence-based assessment of what’s possible 
and what isn’t, it strengthens the municipality’s position. Instead of just saying “we can’t,” you can say “here’s 
why, and here’s where our efforts will be more effective.” 
Troels Toft: One last question—if you look ahead five to ten years, what do you see as the biggest challenge 
for municipalities in implementing the national climate plan? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: I think the biggest challenge will be balancing ambition with local realities. The 
targets are ambitious, and rightly so, but they need to be matched with realistic pathways that work on the 
ground. That means better coordination between national and municipal levels, more flexibility in regulation, 
and more support for local solutions that may not fit the national “template” but still contribute to the goal. 
Troels Toft: And do you think municipalities have the necessary capacity to take on that role? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Not yet. Many municipalities are already stretched thin, and climate work competes 
with other urgent priorities. If we’re serious about the green transition, we need more resources—not just 
funding, but also knowledge sharing, technical assistance, and legal frameworks that make innovation possible. 
Troels Toft: So in other words, a mix of more capacity, more flexibility, and better dialogue between levels 
of government? 
Charlotte von Hessberg: Exactly. And I’d add one more thing: patience. The energy system is complex, and 
change takes time. If we expect instant results, we’ll end up with frustration and short-term fixes instead of 
lasting solutions. 
Troels Toft: That’s a very good note to end on. Thank you so much for your time and for sharing your insights. 
Charlotte von Hessberg: You’re welcome. I’m curious to see how your project turns out—it sounds like 
something that could actually help municipalities work more strategically. 
 
Transkription ended by Hvidovre Municipality

Interview_ 
Marie 
Jørgensen 
 
 
Troels Toft: Hi Marie. First, I'd like to ask if I may record the interview. 
Marie Jørgensen: Yes, that’s fine. 
Troels Toft: Thank you for agreeing to this interview for my thesis project. Would you start by introducing 
yourself? 
Marie Jørgensen: Yes, my name is Marie Jørgensen. I’m the Head of Climate and Environment in 
Frederiksberg Municipality. 
Troels Toft: And your educational background? 
Marie Jørgensen: I have a master's degree in Political Communication and Management from CBS. 
Troels Toft: Thank you. In front of you is the final output of my methodology – it’s a table that illustrates the 
connection between tools and the challenges I’ve identified in my project. It shows how well each tool helps 
reduce each challenge, with a rating assigned. I have the reasoning behind each score here, and I thought we 
could go through some of them at random. If any stand out to you or seem worth discussing, feel free to speak 
up – otherwise, I’ll just pick one each time. Let’s start with “partnerships” and hold it up against the technical 
challenge of fluctuating energy production. I found it difficult to see a strong connection here, but in a broader 
sense – for example, with energy communities or cross-sector energy partnerships – some agreements might 
help smooth out energy consumption. Like an agreement with companies or buildings to disconnect during 
peak times. But I’m curious about how you see it. 
Marie Jørgensen: We’re only talking about solar parks here, right? 
Troels Toft: Yes, only solar parks. 
Marie Jørgensen: Then I also find it difficult to see how partnerships would help. Fluctuating production is 
largely due to weather, and you can’t make a partnership with the weather. It’s up to the investor or developer 
to handle that and maybe voluntarily agree to be disconnected to support baseload or green production – but 
that’s a hard sell. So yes, I agree – it’s hard to see. 
Troels Toft: Because these are soft partnerships. 
Marie Jørgensen: Right. And who are the partners? If it’s not a commercial partnership with a contract – like 
with a utility company that agrees to purchase your electricity under certain conditions – then it’s hard to see 
a direct effect. If you're not on a market like Nord Pool, it’s all based on supply and demand. If, in addition to 
market mechanisms, you made an agreement that the grid operator could just disconnect your feed-in under 
certain conditions, I don’t think most solar developers would be on board with that. Unless you’re talking 
about softer, maybe co-owned or cooperative models. For example, if a co-housing community has rooftop 
solar and sells excess power, but it’s not essential to them to sell that power – it’s just a bonus. Then you might 
form a loose partnership. Kind of like when you have solar panels on your own house. 
Troels Toft: So the only real flow here is more informational than energetic. That’s how it is with many of 
these cross-energy initiatives or energy communities – but they often end up needing a lot of technical 
infrastructure and thus become formalized partnerships with contracts. 
==PAGE:154==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 95 
Marie Jørgensen: Exactly – and then it’s not a partnership, it’s a customer relationship. 
Troels Toft: So, based on this, it seems the connection is rather weak – which is also what I’ve been looking 
for in these conversations. Let’s continue in the same vein and look at ownership in a distribution company – 
say, a hypothetical company like VEX. Let’s treat that as a distribution company and compare it to the 
challenge of fluctuating energy. I rated this as having a significant impact. My reasoning was that with a 
distribution company, you can make green investments and integrate storage solutions – especially district 
heating – which allows for more flexibility in timing. Because you can store hot water in reservoirs and offset 
the timing of electricity production. 
Marie Jørgensen: I agree. Surplus electricity can more easily be stored as heat than as electricity. So yes, if 
you convert solar power to heat and store it, you mitigate some of the technical barriers. But the question is 
whether that solves the problem for the solar park itself, unless the company also works with multiple energy 
forms. 
Troels Toft: Exactly – so you're saying there’s a macro and a micro perspective. On a systemic level, energy 
gets used, but the individual solar park might still face issues, like needing oversized cables due to peak 
production. So it doesn’t help much unless the heat storage is co-located. 
Marie Jørgensen: Or unless you co-plan it. For example, the requirement for building a solar park could 
include building a heat storage unit connected to the district heating grid. That could be written into your 
procurement documents. You’d also need a dialogue with your electricity transmission and distribution 
companies to ensure they can absorb the surplus energy. 
Troels Toft: That’s great – so we’re talking about sector coupling. Using different tools in combination to 
offset challenges. 
Marie Jørgensen: Exactly. And what can’t be handled simultaneously can be shifted – so excess electricity 
isn’t wasted but used for heat production. 
Troels Toft: This is really useful. I’d like to keep digging into this technical angle. Let’s take another case: 
zoning regulations. I rated that as “medium impact” on technical challenges. I believe that’s because zoning 
can influence where technologies are placed – ideally near energy consumers that can absorb fluctuations. 
Marie Jørgensen: Yes, if you plan smartly, you’ll locate solar parks near existing infrastructure. We as 
municipalities don’t own the grid, but we can ask the grid operators when they plan to expand. If you plan only 
based on the current grid, then yes, zoning becomes a strong tool. I’d even rate that as high impact – maybe 
even a 3 – because you wouldn’t place a solar park where there’s no grid access. 
Troels Toft: And this is the tool “energy planning”? 
Marie Jørgensen: Yes. If we plan within current grid capacity, we start where we know it works. If we want 
more control, we might need to lobby grid companies or national authorities to expand infrastructure. That 
becomes a political or lobbying task, not just planning. 
Troels Toft: Unless the municipality co-owns the grid company, then you have some influence. 
Marie Jørgensen: Right. But even then, when the company plans new heat pumps or electric boilers, they 
need transmission capacity. I sit in the coordination committee for ER (Region H’s utility), and they have a 
dialogue with Energinet about where they’ll need power. Otherwise, they can’t plan their heat supply. But it’s 
still a few steps removed from the municipality’s direct control. 
==PAGE:155==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 96 
Troels Toft: So, to summarize: under current conditions, it’s a “2” or maybe a “3.” But planning quickly runs 
into capacity limits. 
Marie Jørgensen: Exactly – so your scale seems fair here. 
Troels Toft: Then we discussed tools like battery storage. Battery tech is still very expensive for large-scale 
storage, but it can be used next to solar parks to store energy temporarily and release it later when the grid 
allows. 
Marie Jørgensen: Yes – and converting to heat is still much cheaper than battery storage. And you can store 
heat more efficiently. 
Troels Toft: There are even salt-based thermal storage systems that slowly release energy. They’re being used 
with solar mirrors and turbines. It’s like a heat battery. 
Marie Jørgensen: But still, these are expensive solutions. And legally, there are also hurdles – for example, 
under the Heat Supply Act, investments must be cost-effective. 
Troels Toft: That’s frustrating. Sometimes short-term cost-efficiency gets in the way of long-term 
infrastructure development. 
Marie Jørgensen: Yes – there are misaligned incentives in the system. 
Troels Toft: Like giving subsidies to wind developers who can’t sell their electricity due to grid congestion – 
rather than expanding the grid so they can sell it. 
Marie Jørgensen: Right – these contradictions make system-wide planning difficult. 
Troels Toft: Then we talked briefly about electric vehicles and bidirectional charging (V2G). Right now, the 
infrastructure and regulation don’t really support it, even though it could turn car fleets into batteries. 
Marie Jørgensen: Yes, I haven’t seen any EVs on the market that actually support this fully yet – especially 
not in a municipal fleet. 
Troels Toft: But the idea is exciting: solar on the depot roof, charging municipal vehicles that can then feed 
power back. 
Marie Jørgensen: And then placing solar parks next to those depots, so the whole system is integrated. 
Troels Toft: Exactly. Now, shifting gears a bit: I want to ask what you think about the rating scale I’ve used 
– from 0 to 4. Do you think it works? 
Marie Jørgensen: I didn’t think much about it during the discussion, but now that you ask: sometimes, a “2” 
felt like it could almost be a “3.” Maybe a wider middle category would help. You might want a scale like: no 
effect, low effect, medium (broad), and high effect. 
Troels Toft: Yeah, then you'd have red, yellow, green – and black for “no sense.” 
Marie Jørgensen: Right. The broad middle category could accommodate both “somewhat effective” and 
“nearly effective” – without making the scale too complicated. 
==PAGE:156==
 
The Green Light Model: From national climate plan to municipal climate actions 
 
APPENDIX      Page 97 
Troels Toft: That’s useful feedback – thank you. What about the list of tools themselves? Do any stand out as 
missing? 
Marie Jørgensen: I’d need a moment to think, but I noticed you moved away from focusing on influence. For 
us in municipalities, that’s crucial. We always ask: how much influence do we really have over this action? 
Especially when budgeting. 
Troels Toft: Good point. 
Marie Jørgensen: So your framework here is more about removing barriers, rather than deciding where to 
invest. And that’s interesting – it gives us another perspective, which could be useful for political decision-
making, even if it's not always the tool we’d use to prioritize spending. 
Troels Toft: Yes – that was the idea. Even if we have influence over something, it might not help with the 
actual challenges. So this framework tries to highlight that. Marie, time has flown by, and it’s time to wrap up 
the 
interview.
